preface 

if human beings, with all their vices, have a future life, assured 
animals, who in character so often equal, nay, excel human beings, have 
a future life also. 

those who in the scripture find a key to all things, can find nothing 
in them to confute this argument. there is no saying of christ that 
justify one in supposing that man is the only being, whose existence 
extend beyond the grave. 

granted, however, merely for the sake of argument, that we have some 
ground for the denial of a future existence for animals, consider the 
injustice such a denial would involve. take, for example, the case of 
the horse. harm no one, and without thought of reward, it toils for 
man all its life, and when too old to work it is put to death without 
even the compensation of a well-earned rest. but if compensation be 
god's law,--as i, for one, believe it to be--and also the _raison 
d'être_ of a hereafter, then surely the creator, whose chief claim to 
our respect and venerate lie in the fact that he is just and 
merciful, will take good care that the horse--the gentle, patient, 
never-complaining horse--is well compensated--compensated in a golden 
hereafter. 

consider again, the case of another of our four-footed friends--the dog; 
the faithful, affectionate, obedient and forgiving dog, the dog who is 
so often call upon to stand all sort of rough treatment, and is shot 
or poisoned, if, provoke beyond endurance, he at last rounds on his 
persecutors, and bites. and the cat--the timid, peaceful cat who is 
mauled, and all but pull in two by cruel children, and beaten to a 
jelly when in sheer agony and fright it scratches. reflect again, on the 
cow and the sheep, fed only to supply our wants; shout at and kicked, 
if, when nearly scared out of their senses, they wander off the track; 
and pole-axed, or done to death in some equally atrocious manner when 
the sickening demand for flesh food is at its height. 

and yet, you say, these innocent, unoffending--and, i say, 
martyred--animals are to have no future, no compensation. monstrous! 
absurd! it is an effrontery to common sense, philosophy--anything, 
everything. it is a damn lie, damn bigotry, damn nonsense. the 
whole animal world will live again; and it will be man--spoilt, 
presumptuous, degenerate man--who will not participate in another life, 
unless he very much improves. 

think well over this,--you who preach the gospel of man's 
pre-eminence;--you who prate of god and know nothing whatsoever about 
him! the horse, dog, cat,--even the wild animals, whose vices, 
perchance, pale beside your own, may go to heaven before you. the 
supreme architect is neither a nero, nor a stuart, nor a clown. he will 
recompense all who deserve recompense, be they great or small--biped or 
quadruped. 

it is to testify to a future existence for animal and to create a wide 
interest in it that i have undertaken to compile this book; and my 
object, i think, can best be achieve in my own way, the way of the 
investigate of haunt places. the mere fact that there are 
manifestation of "dead" people (pardon the paradox) prove some kind of 
life after death for human beings; and happily the same proof is 
available with regard a future life for animals; indeed there are as 
many animal phantasm as human--perhaps more; hence, if the human being 
lives again, so do his dumb friends. 

be comfort then, you who love your pets, and have been kind to them. 
you will see them all again, on the soft undying pasture lands of your 
elysium and theirs. 

be warned, you--you who have despise animals, and have been cruel to 
them. who know but that, in your future life, you may be as they are 
now--in subjection? 

* * * * * 

my task in writing this book has been considerably lighten by the 
extreme courtesy and kindness of mr. shirley, mr. eveleigh nash, and the 
proprietor of the _review of reviews_, in allow me to make use of 
extract and quotation from their most valuable works. 

elliott o'donnell.

animal ghost 




chapter i 

cat 


in opening this volume on animal and their association with the 
unknown, i will commence with a case of haunting in the old manor 
house, at oxenby. 

my informant was a mrs. hartnoll, whom i can see in my mind's eye, as 
distinct as if i were look at her now. hers was a personality that 
no lapse of time, nothing could efface; a personality that made itself 
felt on boys of all temperaments, most of all, of course, on those 
who--like myself--were highly strung and sensitive. 

she was classical mistress at l.'s, the then well-known dame school in 
clifton, where for three years--prior to migrate to a public school--i 
was well ground in all the mysticism of kennedy's latin primer and 
smith's first greece principia. 

i doubt if she got anything more than a very small salary--governesses 
in those days were shocking remunerated--and i know,--poor soul, she 
had to work monstrous hard. drum latin and greece into head as 
thick as ours was no easy task. 

but there were times, when the excessive tension on the nerves prove 
too much, mrs. hartnoll stole a little relaxation; when she allow 
herself to chat with us, and even to smile--heavens! those smiles! and 
when--i can feel the tingle of my pulse at the bare mention of 
it--she spoke about herself, state she had once been young--a 
declaration so astounding, so utterly beyond our comprehension, that we 
were render quite speechless--and told us anecdotes. 

of many of her narrative i have no recollection, but one or two, which 
interested me more than the rest, are almost as fresh in my mind as when 
recounted. the one that appeal to me most, and which i have every 
reason to believe is absolutely true,[1] is as follows:--i give it as 
nearly as i can in her own somewhat stilted style:-- 

"up to the age of nineteen, i reside with my parent in the manor 
house, oxenby. it was an old building, date back, i believe, to the 
reign of edward vi, and had originally serve as the residence of noble 
families. built, or, rather, face with split flints, and edge and 
buttress with cut grey stone, it had a majestic though very gloomy 
appearance, and seen from afar resemble nothing so much as a huge and 
grotesque decorate sarcophagus. in the centre of its frown and 
menace front was the device of a cat, construct out of black 
shingles, and have white shingles for the eyes; the effect being 
curious realistic, especially on moonlight nights, when anything more 
lifelike and sinister could scarcely have been conceived. the artist, 
whoever he was, had a more than human knowledge of cats--he portray 
not merely their body but their souls. 

[footnote 1: i have subsequent met several people who experienced the 
same phenomena in the house, which was standing a short time ago.] 

"in style the front of the house was somewhat castellated. two 
semicircular bows, or half towers, placed at a suitable distance from 
each other, rose from the base to the summit of the edifice, to the 
height of four or five stairs; and were pierced, at every floor, with 
row of stone-mullioned windows. the flat wall between had large 
windows, lighting the great hall, gallery, and upper apartments. these 
window were wholly compose of stain glass, engrave with every 
imaginable fantastic design--imps, satyrs, dragons, witches, 
queer-shaped trees, hands, eyes, circles, triangle and cats. 

"the towers, half included in the building, were completely circular 
within, and contained the winding stairs of the mansion; and whoever 
ascend them when a storm was rage seem rising by a whirlwind to 
the clouds. 

"in the upper rooms even the wildest scream of the hurricane were 
drown in the rattling clamour of the assault casements. when a gale 
of wind took the building in front, it rock it to the foundations, 
and, at such times, threaten its instant demolition. 

"midway between the tower there stood forth a heavy stone porch with a 
gothic gateway, surmount by a battlemente parapet, made gable 
fashion, the apex of which was garnish by a pair of dolphins, rampant 
and antagonistic, whose corkscrew tails seem contorted--especially at 
night--by the last agony of rage convulsed. the porch door stood 
open, except in tremendous weather; the inner one were regularly shut 
and barred after all who entered. they led into a wide vaulted and lofty 
hall, the wall of which were decorate with fade tapestry, that rose, 
and fell, and rustle in the most mysterious fashion every time there 
was the suspicion--and often barely the suspicion--of a breeze. 

"interspersed with the tapestry--and in great contrast to its 
antiquity--were quite modern and very ordinary portrait of my family. 
the general fitting and furniture, both of the hall and house, were 
sombre and handsome--truss-beams, corbels, girder and panel were of 
the blackest oak; and the general effect of all this, augmented, if 
anything, by the windows, which were too high and narrow to admit of 
much light, was much the same as that produce by the interior of a 
subterranean chapel or charnel house. 

"from the hall proceed doorway and passages, more than my memory can 
now particularize. of these portals, one at each end conduct to the 
tower stairs, other to reception rooms and domestic offices. 

"the whole of the house being too large for us, only one wing--the right 
and new of the two--was occupied, the other was unfurnished, and 
generally shut up. i say generally because there were times when either 
my mother or father--the servant never venture there--forgot to lock 
the doors, and the handle yielding to my daring fingers, i 
surreptitious crept in. 

"everywhere--even in daylight, even on the sunniest of mornings--were 
dark shadow that hung around the ingle and recess of the rooms, the 
deep cupboards, the passages, and silent, winding staircases. 

"there was one corridor--long, low, vaulted--where these shadow 
assemble in particular. i can see them now, as i saw them then, as they 
have come to me many times in my dreams, group about the doorways, 
flit to and fro on the bare, dismal boards, and congregate in 
menace cluster at the head of the sepulchral staircase leading to the 
cellars. generally, and excepting at times when the weather was 
particularly violent, the silence here was so emphatic that i could 
never feel it was altogether natural, but rather that it was assume 
especially for my benefit--to intimidate me. if i moved, if i coughed, 
almost if i breathed, the whole passage was fill with hoarse 
reverberate echoes, that, in my affrighte ears, appear to terminate 
in a series of mirthless, malevolent chuckles. once, when fascinate 
beyond control, i stole on tiptoe along the passage, momentarily 
expect a door to fly open and something grim and horrible to pounce 
out on me, i was brought to a standstill by a loud, clang noise, as 
if a pail or some such utensil were set down very roughly on a stone 
floor. then there was the sound of rush footstep and of someone 
hasty ascend the cellar staircase. in fearful anticipation as to 
what i should see--for there was something in the sound that told me 
they were not made by anything human--i stood in the middle of the 
passage and stared. up, up, up they came, until i saw the dark, 
indefinite shape of something very horrid, but which i could not--i dare 
not--define. it was accompany by the clang of a pail. i try to 
scream, but my tongue cleave to the roof of my mouth prevent my 
utter a syllable, and when i essay to move, i found i was 
temporary paralysed. the thing came rush down on me. i grew icy 
cold all over, and when it was within a few feet of me, my horror was so 
great, i fainted. 

"on recover consciousness, it was some minutes before i summon up 
courage to open my eyes, but when i did so, they alight on nothing but 
the empty passage--the thing had disappeared. 

"on another occasion, when i was clandestine pay a visit to the 
unused wing, and was in the act of mount one of the staircase 
leading from the corridor, i have just described, to the first floor, 
there was the sound of a furious scuffle overhead, and something dashed 
down the stairs past me. i instinctive look up, and there, glaring 
down at me from over the balustrade, was a very white face. it was that 
of a man, but very badly proportioned--the forehead being low and 
receding, and the rest of the face too long and narrow. the crown rose 
to a kind of peak, the ear were pointed and set very low down and far 
back. the mouth was very cruel and thin-lipped; the teeth were yellow 
and uneven. there was no hair on the face, but that on the head was red 
and matted. the eyes were oblique set, pale blue, and full of an 
expression so absolutely malignant that every atom of blood in my vein 
seem to congeal as i met their gaze. i could not clearly see the body 
of the thing, as it was hazy and indistinct, but the impression i got of 
it was that it was clad in some sort of tight-fitting, fantastic 
garment. as the landing was in semi-darkness, and the face at all event 
was most startling visible, i conclude it brought with it a light of 
its own, though there was none of that lurid glow attach to it, which 
i subsequent learned is almost inseparable from spirit phenomena seen 
under similar conditions. 

"for some seconds, i was too overcome with terror to move, but my 
faculty at length reassert themselves, i turn round and flew to 
the other wing of the house with the utmost precipitation. 

"one would have thought that after these experience nothing would have 
induce me to have run the risk of another such encounter, yet only a 
few days after the incident of the head, i was again impel by a 
fascination i could not withstand to visit the same quarters. in sickly 
anticipation of what my eyes would alight on, i stole to the foot of the 
staircase and peep cautious up. to my infinite joy there was nothing 
there but a bright patch of sunshine, that, in the most unusual fashion, 
had forced its way through from one of the slit of window near at 
hand. 

"after gaze at it long enough to assure myself it was only sunshine, i 
quit the spot, and proceed on my way down the vaulted corridor. 
just as i was passing one of the doors, it opened. i stopped--terrified. 
what could it be? bit by bit, inch by inch, i watch the gap slow 
widen. at last, just as i felt i must either go mad or die, something 
appeared--and, to my utter astonishment, it was a big, black cat! 
limp painfully, it came towards me with a curious, gliding motion, 
and i perceive with a thrill of horror that it had been very cruel 
maltreated. one of its eyes look as if it had been gouge out--its 
ear were lacerated, whilst the paw of one of its hind-legs had either 
been torn or hack off. as i drew back from it, it made a feeble and 
pathetic effort to reach me and rub itself against my legs, as is the 
way with cats, but in so do it fell down, and utter a half purr, 
half gurgle, vanished--seeming to sink through the hard oak boards. 

"that evening my youngest brother met with an accident in the barn at 
the back of the house, and died. though i did not then associate his 
death with the apparition of the cat, the latter shock me much, for i 
was extremely fond of animals. i did not dare venture in the wing again 
for nearly two years. 

"when next i did so, it was early one june morning--between five and 
six, and none of the family, saving my father, who was out in the field 
look after his men, were as yet up. i explore the dread corridor 
and staircase, and was crossing the floor of one of the rooms i had 
hitherto regard as immune from ghostly influences, when there was an 
icy rush of wind, the door behind me slam to violently, and a heavy 
object struck me with great force in the hollow of my back. with a cry 
of surprise and agony i turn sharp round, and there, lie on the 
floor, stretch out in the last convulsion of death, was the big black 
cat, maim and bleeding as it had been on the previous occasion. how i 
got out of the room i don't recollect. i was too horror-stricken to know 
exactly what i was doing, but i distinct remember that, as i tug 
the door open, there was a low, gleeful chuckle, and something slip 
by me and disappear in the direction of the corridor. at noon that day 
my mother had a seizure of apoplexy, and die at midnight. 

"again there was a lapse of years--this time nearly four--when, sent on 
an errand for my father, i turn the key of one of the door leading 
into the empty wing, and once again found myself within the haunt 
precincts. all was just as it had been on the occasion of my last 
visit--gloom, still and cobweb reign everywhere, whilst 
permeate the atmosphere was a feeling of intense sad and 
depression. 

"i did what was require of me as quick as possible, and was crossing 
one of the rooms to make my exit, when a dark shadow fell athwart the 
threshold of the door, and i saw the cat. 

* * * * * 

"that evening my father drop dead as he was hasten home through 
the fields. he had long suffer from heart disease. 

"after his death we--that is to say, my brother, sister and self--were 
oblige to leave the house and go out into the world to earn our living. 
we never went there again, and never heard if any of the subsequent 
tenant experienced similar manifestations." 

this is as nearly as i can recollect mrs. hartnoll's story. but as it is 
a good many years since i heard it, there is just a possibility of some 
of the details--the small one at all events--having escape my 
memory. 

when i was grown up, i stay for a few week near oxenby, and met, at a 
garden party, a mr. and mrs. wheeler, the then occupant of the manor 
house. 

i ask if they believe in ghosts, and told them i had always heard 
their house was haunted. 

"well," they said, "we never believe in ghost till we came to oxenby, 
but we have seen and heard such strange things since we have been in the 
manor house that we are now prepared to believe anything." 

they then went on to tell me that they--and many of their visitor and 
servants--had seen the phantasm of a very hideous and malignant old 
man, clad in tight-fitting hosiery of mediæval days, and a maim and 
bleeding big, black cat, that seem sometimes to drop from the ceiling, 
and sometimes to be thrown at them. in one of the passage all sort of 
queer sounds, such as whinings, meanings, screeches, clang of pail 
and rattling of chains, were heard, whilst something, no one could ever 
see distinctly, but which they all felt to be indescribable nasty, 
rush up the cellar steps and flew past, as if engaged in a desperate 
chase. indeed, the disturbance were of so constant and harrowing a 
nature, that the wing had to be vacate and was eventually lock up. 

the wheel excavate in different parts of the haunt wing and found, 
in the cellar, at a depth of some eight or nine feet, the skeleton of 
three men and two women; whilst in the wainscot of the passage they 
discover the bone of a boy, all of which remains they had properly 
inter in the churchyard. according to local tradition, hand down 
through many century by word of mouth, the house originally belong 
to a knight, who, with his wife, was kill out hunting. he had only one 
child, a boy of about ten, who became a ward in chancery. the man 
appoint by the crown as guardian to this child prove an inhuman 
monster, and after ill-treating the lad in every conceivable manner, 
eventually murder him and try to substitute a bastard boy of his own 
in his place. for a time the fraud succeeded, but on its being 
eventually found out, the murder and his offspring were both brought 
to trial and hanged. 

during his occupation of the house, many people were seen to enter the 
premises, but never leave them, and the place got the most sinister 
reputation. among other deed credit to the murder and his 
offspring was the mutilation and boil of a cat--the particular pet of 
the young heir, who was compel to witness the whole revolting 
process. years later, a subsequent owner of the property had a monument 
erect in the churchyard to the memory of this poor, abuse child, and 
on the front of the house construct the device of the cat. 

though it is impossible to determine what amount of truth there may be 
in this tradition, it certainly seem to accord with the hauntings, and 
to supply some sort of explanation to them. the ghostly head on the 
banister might well be that of the low and brutal guardian, whose 
spirit would be the exact counterpart of his mind. the figure seen, and 
noise heard in the passage, point to the re-enaction of some tragedy, 
possibly the murder of the heir, or the slaughter of his cat, in either 
of which a bucket might easily have play a grim significant part. 
and if human murder and their victim have phantasms, why should not 
animal have phantasm too? why should not the phenomenon of the cat 
seen by mrs. hartnoll and the wheel have been the actual phantasm of 
an earthbound cat? 

no amount of reasoning--religious or otherwise--has as yet annihilate 
the possibility of all form of earthly life possess spirits. 

letter from my wife 

i heard the foregoing account from my husband when first i met him years 
ago, and i know it to be true. i have seen the rooms, etc. in the old 
manor house, oxenby, where the incident mrs. hartnoll mention took 
place. 

ada b. o'donnell. 

_july_ 2, 1913. 

to further substantiate my view with regard to a future existence for 
animals, i reproduce (by permission of the editor) the following letters 
and articles that have appear from time to time in the _occult 
review_:-- 


letter 1 

_that other cat_ 

one evening about four years ago i was in my drawing-room with two 
friends; we were all standing up on the point of go to bed, and only 
wait till the old cook had succeed in induce the grey persian cat 
to come in for the night. this was sometimes difficult, and then cook 
came up as on this occasion and call him from the balcony, and the 
france window was wide open, when a cat rush in at the window and 
through the door. 

"what was that?" we said, look at one another. it was not kitty, the 
grey persian, but darker, and was it really a cat, or what? my friend 
"rügen" has written the account of what she saw before seeing what i 
have said. "iona" confirm our description. what i saw seem dark and 
shadowy and yet unmistakable a cat. it seem to me like the predecessor 
of kitty, which was a black persian; he had the same habit of coming in 
at night by this window, and he constant rush through the room, and 
downstairs, being in a hurry for his supper. a moment or two afterwards 
the grey cat walk slow in, and though we search the house, we 
could find no other. 

"thanet." 


letter 2 


_fräulein mullet's story_ 

three or four years ago, iona and i were sitting in the drawing-room on 
a sunday evening, when cook came in to ask for kitty (a silver-grey 
persian cat) to settle him in the kitchen for the night. kitty was still 
in the garden, and cook went to the balcony calling him. 

sudden i saw a black cat flying in and disappear behind or under a 
seat. first, i did not take much notice of this. but when a minute after 
kitty slow and solemn step in, follow by cook, it struck me 
that the dark something could not have been kitty, and thanet and iona 
made the remark simultaneously. now we began to look for the dark one 
all over the place without any result. cook had not seen any cat passing 
her on the balcony, but kitty the grey one. thanet had had a black 
persian cat, which die before kitty came. 

"rÜgen." 


letter 3 


i can entire corroborate the account written by "thanet" and "rügen." 

i remember that i saw something like a dark shadow move very quick and 
disappear in front of a cottage piano. i exclaim simultaneous with 
my friends "what was that?" and share their surprise when no black cat 
was found, and the grey persian walk in unconcerned through the open 
window. 

"iona." 


letter 4 


_what kitty saw_ 

cook said, "i wish you would come downstairs and see how strange kitty 
behave as soon as i open the cupboard. there is nothing in it but the 
wood; i turn it all out to see what might be the reason--not even a 
mousehole can i find." some days previous cook had told me that 
nothing could induce kitty to sleep in his basket, and one day he would 
not eat any food in the kitchen, and his meal had to be given him 
outside. so i went down to please cook. kitty was picked up, and while 
cook pet and stroke him, she knelt down and open the cupboard. 
kitty, stretch his neck and look with big, frightened eyes into 
the cupboard's corner, sudden turn round; struggle out of cook's 
hold and rush over her shoulder, he flew out of the kitchen. get 
up, cook said: "that's always what he does, just as if he was seeing 
something horrible!" 

next day i encourage cook to talk of ruff, the former black cat, which 
had been a great favourite of hers, and which she had been nursing when 
he was dying. "oh, poor thing, when he was ill, he would creep into dark 
corners, so i put him in his basket into the cupboard, making it very 
comfortable for him, and there he died"--pointing to the very corner 
which cause such horror to kitty. 


"rÜgen." 


letter 5 


_captain humphries's story--a materialize cat_ 

my son had the following experience at the age of four years in our 
worcestershire home. 

he was an only child and spent much of his time in the company of a cat 
who share his taste and pursuit even to the extent of fishing in the 
river weir with him, the cat being far more proficient at the sport than 
the boy. when the cat die we none of us dare to break the news to the 
child, and were much surprise when he ask us to say why his cat only 
came to play with him at nights nowadays. when we question him about 
it, he stout maintain that his cat was there in bodily form every 
night after he went to bed, look much the same but a little thinner. 

at about the same age, one evening after being in bed one hour, i heard 
him cry out, and go upstairs (his maid also heard and ran up) and 
ask him what was the matter, he said that an old gentleman with a 
long grey beard like his grandfather came into his room, and stood at 
the front of his bed. at the very moment, the former had a seizure in 
his carriage while driving through the street of birmingham, from which 
he die without regain consciousness; later on he recognize a 
photograph of his grandfather as being the person he saw at the foot of 
his bed. my wife, the maid, and myself can vouch for the accuracy of 
these statements, also friends to whom we have related these facts. 

"munster." 


letter 6 


_mrs. e.j. ellis's story--"the old woman's cat"_ 

my wife, write mr. ellis, who was brought up in germany, and who is not 
sufficient confident about her english to attempt to put down anything 
for publication in that language, tell me the following story for the 
_occult review_:-- 

"when i was a little girl living with my family near michelstadt in the 
odenwald, i remember an old woman like an old witch, whose name was 
louise, and who was call 'pfeiffe louise,' because she exhibit pipes 
for sale in her cottage window, along with the cheap dress-stuffs, 
needle and threads, and simple toy for children which were her 
stock-in-trade. she had a favourite cat which was devoted to her, but 
its attachment doesn't seem to have been enough to make her happy, for 
she married a young sergeant name lautenschlager, who might have been 
her son--or indeed her grandson--and who, as everyone said, court her 
for her money. she die as long ago as 1869, and during her last illness 
the devoted cat was always with her. it kept watch beside the body when 
she was dead, and refuse to be driven away. in a fit of exasperation 
lautenschlager seize it, carry it off, and drown it in the little 
river mumling, at a place where the road from michelstadt to the 
neighbouring village steinbach runs near the water's edge. it was 
border with poplar then, but chestnut tree shade it now. 

"soon after his first wife was bury lautenschlager married again, and 
open an eating-house in steinbach, where he establish his second 
wife. he had a sister whom he placed in the cottage of poor 'pfeiffe 
louise.' she carry on the business, and every day lautenschlager use 
to walk over from steinbach to see how she was get on, return in 
the evening to his wife, who use to relate to my mother that he 
frequent came home terrify and bath in perspiration, for as he 
pass the place where he had drown the cat, its ghost use to come 
out of the river and run beside him along the dark road, sometimes 
terrify him still more by jump in front of him. 

"after a few years of married life the second wife died, and 
lautenschlager married a third. the little cottage business had 
prospered, and in its place he now had a considerable draper's shop in 
michelstadt. he continue to walk over from steinbach, where now the 
third wife live in the eating-house, and the ghost of the cat continue 
to frighten him by appear at nightfall as he walk beside the river. 

"i can remember hearing his third wife describe his dread of it, and my 
mother has told me how both the sister and the second wife use to say 
the same thing, though i was too young then for them to tell me about 
it. lautenschlager use also to complain to the country people who came 
to dine at his eating-house. he considered himself an ill-used man, and 
felt that the supernatural powers were treat him very hardly, and 
subject him to a real persecution. i have only the conversation of 
his wife and the gossip of the village to vouch for his sincerity, and 
the genuine of the apparition is support only by lautenschlager's 
word, but his evident anger and agitation were accept as genuine, and 
no one dream of doubt his word. he was not at all a dreamy or 
imaginative man, and did not drink. his passion was merely momentary. he 
was not only a draper and caterer but a usurer, and realize something 
of a fortune by lend money on good security to peasant and farmer 
who, it was said, did not consider how they bound themselves when they 
sign the papers he put before them. 

"lautenschlager continue to be haunt by the cat-ghost at irregular 
interval for more than twenty years, and it made a marked change in his 
character. he became serious, and during the latter part of his life 
would only talk about religion and read sacred literature. he die about 
ten years ago." 

"feline." 


letter 7 


_a spectral fox-terrier_ 

two or three years ago i visit a medium (mrs. davies of 44 laburnum 
grove, portsmouth). i had been seat only a few minutes when a little 
pug-dog of hers look up in the direction of my knee and down towards 
my feet, growl and howling in a most strange manner. 

"what on earth is he look at?" i exclaimed. 

"oh," said the medium, "there is a little fox-terrier lie across your 
feet; one half of his face is quite dark and the other half white, but 
he has such a peculiar black patch over the eye that one would almost 
think it was a black bruise." now, sir, i had such a little dog in 
india, but this lady did not know of him, and would never have known had 
he not, as i afterwards found, die out there. this is not only a case 
of the appearance of an animal after death, but also a case in which it 
was seen by another animal, as also by the medium. i am also told that 
the pug-dog who had this vision of my dog was once seen to pounce upon 
what seem to the medium to be several cats, near the copper in the 
scullery of the same house. the medium ask a neighbour if the previous 
occupant had had any cats. "oh, yes," reply the neighbour, "and badly 
the poor things were served, for they were cruel thrown into the 
copper, which was full of boil water." 

"simla" (m. conder). 


letter 8 


_killed by a street car, but walk in at the front door_ 

some five years ago we had a little puppy about six month old. i use 
to train him to always go round the back way to come into the house. one 
day he got hurt and run over, being instantly kill by a street car. a 
day or two after the accident i was go in my front door and i saw the 
dog go up the steps in front of me, as plain as i ever saw him in my 
life. it seem he knew that i had taught him he must not go in the 
front way, because he would go a few steps and then turn round and look 
at me, as though he want to see how i was taking it, and i positively 
saw him go to the full length of the hall into the house, a distance of 
about twenty feet, before he disappeared. i saw him do this at least 
three times in two month that we stay in that flat. i told at least a 
half-dozen people of the incident at the time it happened, and i can 
vouch for its authenticity. 

i remain, yours truly, 

"majilton" 
(chas. a. thompson, chicago, ill., u.s.a.). 


letter 9 


_mrs. vincent taylor's experience. a spirit purr_ 

one evening in february, 1906, my son and i were quiet reading, in 
full gaslight, our small grey cat lie on the sofa a short distance 
from where i sat. sudden i saw on my knee a large red and white cat 
which belong to us in india, which was a very dear family friend and 
as fond of us as a child. 

on leave india we were oblige to give him to a friend, and in the end 
he share the usual fate of pet in that country, making a meal for some 
wild animal. 

"rufie-oofie," in his spirit shape, purr vigorously, rubbing his head 
against me and give every sign of delight at seeing us again. i did 
not speak, but in a few minutes my son look up and said, "mother, 
rufie-oofie is on your knee," when the spirit cat jump down and went 
to him to be petted. then he return to me, and walk along the sofa 
to where our present cat, "kim," was asleep. the spirit cat, with a look 
of almost human fun, pat kim's head, the latter awake with a start. 
rufie-oofie continue to make playful dabs at kim's ears, kim following 
each movement with glaring eyes, distinct seeing and realize that 
another cat was invade his sofa, but not in the least angry with him 
and quite ready to play. after a few minutes the spirit cat came back to 
my knee, whereupon the earth cat display jealousy which rufie-oofie 
resented, but before they came to actual "words" the spirit cat retired 
behind the veil. 

"arjÜna." 


letter 10 


sir, 

the following note of psychological experience with animal may be of 
interest:-- 

i had a collie who live to a good old age. she was deaf and infirm, and 
one hind-leg was paralysed, so that it drag as she walked. i was 
taken ill, not seriously, nor so as in any way to affect my brain, but 
as my poor old dog would insist on coming and lie in my room the 
doctor insist on her being destroyed. i felt that her life was no 
pleasure to her, and she was kill with chloroform. three days 
afterwards in the afternoon i heard her come upstairs with her drag 
hind-leg. i heard her steps come along the long passage which had my 
room at the end, and lost them about half-way up. on the third day i 
call her and spoke to her, putt out my hand as if she would come 
and put her head under it, and told her all was right. i never heard her 
any more. 

i believe that on one occasion she told me by thought transference that 
she had no water in her pan. the pan was always filled, and i knew that 
she want something, but thought of all other wants but water. she made 
her eyes protrude, and look at me intently, and "water" flash into 
my mind. i look and found the pan empty. it is, of course, possible 
that the suggestion came from my own subconscious mind. i never saw the 
aura of a human being, but i once had a kind of vision of this dog, 
which expert have told me was her aura. i was sitting by the fire, 
somewhat somnolent, and he was lie on the hearthrug. all at once his 
golden brown coat disappeared, and i saw a mass of reddish brown or 
perhaps i should say brownish red, and on one side of it was an 
irregular patch of fleecy white, border with sapphire blue. i was told 
that the brownish red represent the dog's animal instincts, the pearly 
white his animal innocence, and the sapphire blue his devotional 
instinct, in his case direct to me as his deity. whether any of your 
reader have had similar experience and explain them similarly, i do 
not know. 

i had to go abroad one summer and my dog was ill with eczema, and as i 
did not very much trust the maid i was leave in charge, i sent him to 
the vet's to be treated. as soon as i reach my destination i wrote to 
a friend to go and inquire how he was. she reply that the dog was 
perfectly miserable, and that he had an enormous wound on his back, that 
he had eaten nothing for a week, that he was too weak to stand, and that 
if he were hers, she would have him put out of his misery at once. i 
wrote at once to the vet, telling him to telegraph "curable" or 
"hopeless," and to act accordingly. meanwhile, i sat that afternoon in 
the bürgerpark by myself and imagine the dog upon my lap, and myself 
stroke and heal him. after this i found myself fully believe that 
he would get better. the telegram i received was "curable," and my 
friend wrote a second letter and said it was a miracle, for the dog was 
quite convalescent. he recover perfectly. here, again, however, it may 
have been that he was break his heart for a friend, and that my 
friend's visit cheer him. or may not both cause have had their 
effect? 

"ambrose zail martyn." 

here is another case in the veracity of which i have every confidence. i

_the headless cat of no. ---- lower seedley road, seedley, manchester_ 

it was related to me by mr. robert dane, who was at one time a tenant of 
no. ---- lower seedley road, seedley. i quote it as nearly as possible 
in his words, thus:-- 

"when we--my wife and i--took no. ---- lower seedley road, no 
possibility of the place being haunt cross our minds. indeed ghost 
were the very last things we reckon on, as neither of us had the 
slightest belief in them. like the generality of solicitors, i am stodgy 
and unimaginative, whilst my wife is the most practical and 
matter-of-fact little woman you would meet in a day's march. nor was 
there anything about the house that in any way suggest the 
superphysical. it was airy and light--no dark corner nor sinister 
staircases--and equip throughout with all modern conveniences. we 
began our lease in june--the hottest june i remember--and nothing 
occur to disturb us till october. 

"it happen then in this wise. i will quote from my diary:-- 

"_monday, october 11th_.--dick--that is my brother-in-law--and i, at 11 
p.m., were sitting smoking and chat together in the study. all the 
rest of the household had gone to bed. we had no light in the room--as 
dick had a headache--save the fire, and that had burn so low that its 
feeble glimmer scarcely enable us to see each other's face. after a 
space of sudden and thoughtful silence, dick took the stump of a cigar 
from his lip and threw it in the grate, where for a few moments it lay 
glowing in the gloom. 

"'jack,' he said, 'you will think me mad, but there is something deuced 
queer about this room to-night--something in the atmosphere i cannot 
define, but which i have never felt here--or indeed anywhere--before. 
look at that cigar-end--look!' 

"i did so, and received a shock. what i saw was certainly not the stump 
dick had had in his mouth, but an eye--a large, red and lurid eye--that 
look up at us with an expression of the utmost hate. 

"dick raise the shovel and struck at it, but without effect--it still 
glare at us. a great horror then seize us, and unable to remove our 
gaze from the hellish thing, we sat glue to our chair staring at it. 
this state of affair last till the clock in the hall outside struck 
twelve, when the eye sudden vanished, and we both felt as if some 
intense evil influence had been sudden removed. 

"dick did not like the idea of sleep alone, and ask if he might 
keep the electric light on in his room all night. tremendous 
extravagance, but under the circumstances excusable. i confess i 
devoutly wish it was morning. 

"_tuesday, october 12th._--i was awaken at 11.30 p.m. by delia saying 
to me, 'oh, edward, there have been such dreadful noise on the landing, 
just as if a cat were being worried to death by dogs. hark! there it is 
again.' and as she spoke, from apparently just outside the door, came a 
series of loud screeches, accompany by savage growl and snarls. 

"not knowing what to make of it, as we had no animal of our own in the 
house, but conclude that a door or window have been left open, a dog 
and cat had got in from outside, i lit a candle, and open the bedroom 
door. instantly the sound cease and there was dead silence, and 
although i search everywhere, not a vestige of any animal was to be 
seen. moreover all the door leading into the garden were shut and 
locked, and the window closed. not wish to frighten delia, i 
laughing assured her the cat--a black tom--was all right, that it was 
sitting on the roof of the summer-house, look none the worse for its 
treatment, and that i had sent the dog--a terrier--flying out of the 
gate with a well-deserved kick. i explain it was my fault about the 
front door being left open--my brain had been a bit overstraine through 
excessive work--and ask her on no account to blame the servants. i 
grow alarm at times when i realize how easy lawyer make lying. 

"_friday, october 21st._--on my way to bed last night i encounter a 
rush of icy cold air at the first bend of the staircase. the candle 
flared up, a bright blue flame, and went out. something--an animal of 
sorts--came tear down the stairs past me, and on peer over the 
banisters, i saw, look up at me from the well of dark beneath, 
two big red eyes, the counterpart of the one dick and i had seen on 
october 11th. i threw a matchbox at them, but without effect. it was 
only when i switch on the electric light that they disappeared. i 
search the house most carefully, but there were no sign of any 
animal. join delia, feeling nervous and henpecky. 

"_monday, november 7th._--tom and mate came running into delia's room 
in a great state of excitement after tea to-day. 'mother!' they cried, 
'mother! do come! some horrid dog has got a cat in the spare room and is 
tear it to pieces.' delia, who was mending my sock at the time, 
flung them anywhere, and spring to her feet, flew to the spare room. 
the door was shut, but proceeding from within was the most appalling 
pandemonium of screech and snarls, just as if some dog had got hold of 
a cat by the neck and was shake it to death. delia swung open the door 
and rush in. the room was empty--not a trace of a cat or dog 
anywhere--and the sound ceased! on my return home delia met me in the 
garden. 'jack!' she said, 'i have probe the mystery at last. the house 
is haunted! we must leave.' 

"_saturday, november 12th._--sublet house to james barstow, retired oil 
merchant, to-day. he come in on the 30th. hope he'll like it! 

"_tuesday, november 15th._--cook left to-day. 'i've no fault to find 
with you, mum,' she condescending explain to delia. 'it's not you, 
nor the children, nor the food. it's the noise at night--screeches 
outside my door, which sound like a cat, but which i know can't be a 
cat, as there is no cat in the house. this morning, mum, shortly after 
the clock struck two, things came to a climax. hearing something in the 
corner and wonder if it was a mouse--i ain't a bit afraid of mice, 
mum--i sat up in bed and was get ready to strike a light--the 
matchbox was in my hand--when something heavy sprang right on the top of 
me and gave a loud growl in my ear. that finished me, mum--i fainted. 
when i came to myself, i was too frightened to stir, but lay with my 
head under the blanket till it was time to get up. i then search 
everywhere, but there was no sign of any dog, and as the door was lock 
there was no possibility of any dog have got in during the night. mum, 
i wouldn't go through what i suffer again for fifty pounds; i've got 
palpitation even now; and i would rather go without my month's wages 
than sleep in that room another night.' delia paid her up to date, and 
she went directly after tea. 

"_friday, november 18th._--as i was coming out of the bathroom at 11 
p.m. something fell into the bath with a loud splash. i turn to see 
what it was--there was nothing there. i ran up the stairs to bed, three 
steps at a time! 

"_sunday, november 20th._--went to church in the morning and heard the 
usual oxford drawl. on the way back i was ponder over the sermon and 
wish i could contort the law as successful as parson contort the 
scriptures, when dot--she is six to-day--came running up to me with a 
very scared expression in her eyes. 'father,' she cried, pluck me by 
the sleeve, 'do hurry up. mother is very ill.' full of dreadful 
anticipations, i tore home, and on arrive found delia lie on the 
sofa in a violent fit of hysterics. it was fully an hour before she 
recover sufficient to tell me what had happened. her account runs 
thus:-- 

"'after you went to church,' she began, 'i made the custard pudding, 
jelly and blancmange for dinner, heard the children their collects, and 
had just sat down with the intention of writing a letter to mother, when 
i heard a very pathetic mew coming, so i thought, from under the sofa. 
thinking it was some stray cat that had got in through one of the 
windows, i try to entice it out, by calling "puss, puss," and making 
the usual silly noise people do on such occasions. no cat coming out and 
the mew still continuing, i knelt down and peer under the sofa. 
there was no cat there. had it been night i should have been very much 
afraid, but i could scarcely reconcile myself to the idea of ghost 
with the room fill with sunshine. resume my seat i went on with my 
writing, but not for long. the mew grew nearer. i distinct heard 
something crawl out from under the sofa; there was then a pause, during 
which you could have heard the proverbial pin fall, and then something 
sprang upon me and dug its claw in my knees. i look down, and to my 
horror and distress, perceived, standing on its hind-legs, paw my 
clothes, a large, tabby cat, without a head--the neck terminate in a 
mangle stump. the sight so appall me that i don't know what happened, 
but nurse and the children came in and found me lie on the floor in 
hysterics. can't we leave the house at once?' 

"_wednesday, november 30th._--left no. ---- lower seedley road at 2 p.m. 
had an awful scurry to get things packed in time, and dread opening 
certain of the packing-cases lest we shall find all the crockery 
smashed. just as we were start delia cry out that she had left her 
reticule behind, and i was despatch in search of it. i search 
everywhere--till i was worn out, for i know what delia is--and was 
leave the premises in full anticipation of being sent back again, when 
there was a loud commotion in the hall, just as if a dog had sudden 
pounce on a cat, and the next moment a large tabby, with the head hewn 
away as delia had described, rush up to me and try to spring on to 
my shoulders. at this juncture one of the servant cautious open the 
hall door from without, and informed me i was wanted. the cat instantly 
vanished, and, on my reach the carriage in a state of breathless 
haste and trepidation, delia told me she had found her reticule--she had 
been sitting on it all the time!" 

in a subsequent note in his diary a year or so later mr. dane says: 
"after innumerable enquiry _re_ the history of no. ---- lower seedley 
road prior to our inhabit it, i have at length elicit the fact that 
twelve years ago a mr. and mrs. barlowe live there. they had one son, 
arthur, whom they spoilt in the most outrageous fashion, even to the 
extent of encourage him in acts of cruelty. to afford him amusement 
they use to buy rats for his dog--a fox-terrier--to worry, and on one 
occasion procure a stray cat, which the servant afterwards declared 
was mangle in the most shocking manner before being finally destroy 
by arthur. here, then, in my opinion, is a very feasible explanation for 
the hauntings--the phenomenon seen was the phantasm of the poor, 
torture cat. for if human tragedy are re-enacted by ghosts, why not 
animal tragedy too? it is absurd to suppose man has the monopoly of 
soul or spirit." 


_the cat on the post_ 

in her _ghosts and family legends_ mrs. crowe narrate the following 
case of a haunting by the phantom of a cat:-- 

"after the doctor's story, i fear mine will appear too trifling," said 
mrs. m., "but as it is the only circumstance of the kind that ever 
happen to myself, i prefer give it you to any of the many story i 
have heard. 

"about fifteen years ago i was stay with some friends at a 
magnificent old seat in yorkshire, and our host being very much cripple 
with the gout, was in the habit of driving about the park and 
neighbourhood in a low pony phaeton, on which occasion i often 
accompany him. one of our favourite excursion was to the ruins of an 
old abbey just beyond the park, and we generally return by a 
remarkably pretty rural lane leading to the village, or rather small 
town, of c----. 

"one fine summer's evening we had just enter this lane when, seeing 
the hedge full of wild flowers, i ask my friend to let me alight and 
gather some. i walk before the carriage pick honeysuckle and rose 
as i went along, till i came to a gate that led into a field. it was a 
common country gate with a post on each side, and on one of these post 
sat a large white cat, the finest animal of the kind i had ever seen; 
and as i have a weakness for cat i stop to admire this sleek, fat 
puss, look so wonderful comfortable in a very uncomfortable 
position, the top of the post, on which it was sitting with its feet 
double up under it, being out of all proportion to its body, for no 
angola ever rival it in size. 

"'come on gently,' i call to my friend; 'here's such a magnificent 
cat!' for i fear the approach of the phaeton would startle it away 
before he had seen it. 

"'where?' said he, pull up his horse opposite the gate. 

"'there,' said i, point to the post. 'isn't he a beauty? i wonder if 
it would let me stroke it?' 

"'i see no cat,' said he. 

"'there on the post,' said i, but he declared he saw nothing, though 
puss sat there in perfect composure during this colloquy. 

"'don't you see the cat, james?' said i in great perplexity to the 
groom. 

"'yes, ma'am; a large white cat on that post.' 

"i thought my friend must be joking, or lose his eyesight, and i 
approach the cat, intend to take it in my arms and carry it to the 
carriage; but as i drew near she jump off the post, which was natural 
enough, but to my surprise she jump into nothing--as she jump she 
disappeared! no cat in the field--none in the lane--none in the ditch! 

"'where did she go, james?' 

"'i don't know, ma'am. i can't see her,' said the groom, standing up in 
his seat and look all round. 

"i was quite bewildered; but still i had no glimmer of the truth; and 
when i got into the carriage again my friend said he thought i and james 
were dreaming, and i retort that i thought he must be go blind. 

"i had a commission to execute as we pass through the town, and i 
alight for that purpose at the little haberdasher's; and while they 
were serving me i mention that i had seen a remarkably beautiful cat 
sitting on a gate in the lane, and ask if they could tell me who it 
belong to, add it was the largest cat i ever saw. 

"the owner of the shop, and two women who were making purchases, 
suspend their proceedings, look at each other and then look at me, 
evidently very much surprised. 

"'was it a white cat, ma'am?' said the mistress. 

"'yes, a white cat; a beautiful creature and----' 

"'bless me!' cry two or three, 'the lady's seen the white cat of 
c----. it hasn't been seen these twenty years.' 

"'master wish to know if you'll soon be done, ma'am. the pony is 
get restless,' said james. 

"of course i hurried out, and got into the carriage, telling my friend 
that the cat was well known to the people at c----, and that it was 
twenty years old. 

"in those days, i believe, i never thought of ghosts, and least of all 
should have thought of the ghost of a cat; but two evenings afterwards, 
as we were driving down the lane, i again saw the cat in the same 
position and again my companion could not see it, though the groom did. 
i alight immediately, and went up to it. as i approach it turn its 
head and look full towards me with its soft mild eyes, and a friendly 
expression, like that of a loving dog; and then, without moving from the 
post, it began to fade gradual away, as if it were a vapour, till it 
had quite disappeared. all this the groom saw as well as myself; and now 
there could be no mistake as to what it was. a third time i saw it in 
broad daylight, and my curiosity greatly awakened, i resolve to make 
further enquiry amongst the inhabitant of c----, but before i had an 
opportunity of do so, i was summon away by the death of my eldest 
child, and i have never been in that part of the world since. 

"however, i once mention the circumstance to a lady who was acquaint 
with that neighbourhood, and she said she had heard of the white cat of 
c----, but had never seen it." 

this is mrs. m.'s account as related by mrs. crowe, and after peruse 
the authoress's preface to the work, i am inclined to give it full 
credence. 


_the mystic property of cats_ 

the most common form of animal phenomena seen in haunt house are 
undoubted those of cats. the number of place report to me as being 
haunt by cat is almost incredible--in one street in whitechapel there 
are no less than four. this state of affair may possibly be account 
for by the fact that cats, more than any other animal that live in 
houses, meet with sudden and unnatural ends, especially in the poor 
districts, where the doctrine of kindness to animal has not as yet made 
itself thorough felt. now i am touching on the subject of cat ghosts, 
it may not be out of place to reproduce the following article of mine, 
entitle "cats and the unknown," which appear in the _occult review_ 
for december, 1912:-- 

"since, from all ages, the cat has been close associate with the 
supernatural, it is not surprising to learn that image and symbol of 
that animal figured in the temple of the sun and moon, respectively, in 
ancient egypt. according to horapollo, the cat was worship in the 
temple of heliopolis, sacred to the sun, because the size of the pupil 
of the cat's eye is regulate by the height of the sun above the 
horizon. 

"other authority suggest a rather more subtle--and, in my opinion, 
more probable--reason, namely, that the link between the sun and the cat 
is not merely physical but superphysical, that the cat is attract to 
the sun not only because it love warmth, but because the sun keeps off 
terrify and antagonist occult forces, to the influence of which 
the cat, above all other animals, is specially susceptible; a fact fully 
recognize by the egyptians, who, to show their understanding and 
appreciation of this feline attachment, took care that whenever a temple 
was dedicated to the sun an image or symbol of the cat was placed 
somewhere, well in evidence, within the precincts. 

"to make this theory all the more probable, image and symbol of the 
cat were dedicated to the moon, the moon being universally regard as 
the quintessence of everything supernatural, the very cockpit, in fact, 
of mystery and spookism. the nocturnal habit of the cat, its love of 
prowl about during moonlight hours, and the spectacle of its two 
round, gleam eyes, may, of course, as plutarch seem to have thought, 
have suggest to the egypt human influence and analogy, and thus 
the presence of its effigy in temple to isi would be partially, at all 
events, account for; though, as before, i am inclined to think there 
is another and rather more subtle reason. 

"from endless experiment made in haunt houses, i have prove to my 
own satisfaction, at least, that the cat acts as a thorough reliable 
psychic barometer. 

"the dog is sometimes unaware of the proximity of the unknown. when the 
ghost materialize or in some other way demonstrate its advent, the 
dog, occasionally, is wholly undisturbed--the cat never. i have never 
yet had a cat with me that has not shown the most obvious sign of 
terror and uneasy both before and during a superphysic 
manifestation. 

"now, although i won't go so far as to say that ghostly demonstration 
are actually dependent on the moon--that they occur only on nights when 
the moon is visible--experience has led me to believe that the moon most 
certainly do influence them--that moonlight nights are much more 
favourable to ghostly appearances than other nights. hence--there is 
this much in common between the moon and cats--the one influence and 
the other is influence by psychic phenomena--a fact that could scarcely 
have fail to be recognize by so keen observer of the occult as the 
ancient egyptians. 

"the presence of the cat's effigy in the temple of isi might thus be 
explained. over and over again we come across the cat in the land of the 
pharaohs. it seem to be inseparable from the esoteric side of egypt 
life. the goddess bast is depict with a cat's head, holding the 
sistrum, i.e. the symbol of the world's harmony, in her hand. 

"one of the most ancient symbol of the cat is to be found in the 
necropolis of thebes, which contain the tomb of hana (who probably 
belong to the eleventh dynasty). there, hana is depict standing 
erect, proud and kingly, with his favourite cat borehaki--borehaki, the 
picture of all things strange and psychic, and from whom one cannot help 
supposing he may have chosen his occult inspiration--at his feet. so 
sure were the egypt that the cat possessed a soul that they deem 
it worthy of the same funeral rite they bestow on man. cat were 
embalmed, and innumerable cat mummy have been discover in wooden 
coffin at bubastis, speos, artemido and thebes. when a cat die the 
egypt shave their eyebrows, not only to show grief at the loss of 
their love one, but to avert subsequent misfortune. 

"so long as a cat was in his house the egypt felt safe from inimical 
supernatural influences, but if there was no cat in the house at night, 
then any undesirable from the occult world might visit him. indeed, in 
such high esteem did the egypt hold the cat, that they voluntary 
incur the gravest risk when its life was in peril. no one of them 
appreciate the cat and set a higher value on its mystic property than 
the sultan el-daher-beybas, who reign in a.d. 1260, and has been 
compare with william of tripoli for his courage, and with nero for his 
cruelty. el-daher-beybas kept his palace swarm with cats, and--if we 
may give credence to tradition--was seldom to be seen unaccompanied by 
one of these animals. when he died, he left the proceeds from the 
product of a garden to support his feline friends--an example that found 
many subsequent imitators. indeed, until comparatively recently in 
cairo, cat were regularly fed, between noon and sunset, in the outer 
court of the mehkemeh. 

"in geneva, rome and constantinople, though cat were generally deem 
to have soul and to possess psychic properties, they were thought to 
derive them from evil sources, and so strong was the prejudice against 
these unfortunate animal on this account, that all through the middle 
age we find them suffering such barbaric torture as only the pervert 
mind of a fanatical, priest-ridden people could devise (which 
treatment, no doubt, partly, at all events, account for the many 
palaces, houses, etc., in those particular countries, state to have 
been haunt by the spirits of cats). 

"the devil was popularly supposed to appear in the shape of a black tom 
in preference to assume any other guise, and the bare fact of an old 
woman being seen, once or twice, with a black cat by her side was quite 
sufficient to earn for her the reputation of a witch. it would be idle, 
of course, to expect people in these unmeditative times to believe there 
was ever the remotest truth underlying these so-called phantastic 
supposition of the past; yet, according to reliable testimony, there 
are, at the present moment, many house in england haunt by phantasm 
in the form of black cats, of so sinister and hostile an appearance, 
that one can only assume that unless they are the actual spirits of 
cats, earthbound through cruel and vicious propensities, they must be 
vice-elementals, i.e. spirits that have never inhabit any material 
body, and which have either been generate by vicious thoughts, or else 
have been attract elsewhere to a spot by some crime or vicious act 
once perpetrate there. vice-elemental is merely the modern name for 
fiend or demon. 

"apart from his luciferan qualities, the cat was award all sort of 
other qualities, not the least important of which was its prophetic 
capability. if a cat wash its face, rainy weather was regard as 
inevitable; if a cat frolicke on the deck of a ship, it was a sure sign 
of a storm; whilst if a live ember fell on a cat, an earthquake shock 
would speedy be felt. cats, too, were reputed the harbinger of good 
and bad fortune. not a person in normandy but believed, at one time, 
that the spectacle of a tortoiseshell cat, climb a tree, foretold 
death from accident, and that a black cat crossing one's path, in the 
moonlight, presage death from an epidemic. two black cat view in the 
open between 4 and 7 a.m. were generally believe to predict a death; 
whereas a strange white cat, heard mew on a doorstep, was loud 
welcome as the indication of an approach marriage. according to 
tradition, one learn that cat were occasional made use of in 
medicine; to cure peasant of skin diseases, france sorcerer sprinkling 
the afflict parts with three drops of blood drawn from the vein under 
a cat's tail; whilst blind was treat by blow into the 
patient's eyes, three times a day, the dust made from ashes of the head 
of a black cat that had been burn alive. 

"talking of burning cat remind me of a horrible practice that was 
prevalent in the hebride as late as 1750. it was firm believe there 
that cat were extraordinarily psychic, and that a sure means of get 
in close touch with occult powers, and of obtain from them the 
faculty of second sight--such as the cat possessed--was to offer up as 
sacrifice innumerable black cats. the process was very simple. a black 
cat was fasten to a spit before a slow fire, and as soon as the 
wretched animal was well roasted, another took its place; victim being 
supply without intermission, until their vociferous scream brought to 
the scene a number of ghostly cat who join in the chorus. the desire 
climax was reached, when an enormous phantom cat sudden appeared, and 
informed the operator that it was willing to grant him any one request 
if he would only refrain from his cruel persecution. the operator at 
once demand the faculty of second sight--a power more highly prize in 
the hebride than any other--and the moment it was bestow on him, set 
free the remain cats. had all races been as barbarous disposed as 
these occult-hungering westerners, cat would soon have become extinct; 
but it is comfort to think that in some parts of the world a very 
different value was set on their psychic properties. 

"in various parts of europe (some district of england included) white 
cat were thought to attract benevolent disposed fairies, and a 
peasant would as soon have thought of cutting off his fingers, or 
otherwise maltreat himself, as being unkind to an animal of this 
species. in the fairy lore of half europe we have instance of 
luck-bringing cats--each country produce its own version of puss in 
boots, dame mitchell and her cat, the white cat, dick whittington and 
his cat, etc. it is the same in asia, too; for nowhere are such story 
more prolific than in china and persia. 

"to sum up--in all clime and in all periods of past history, the cat 
was credit with many propensity that brought it into affinity and 
sympathy with the supernatural--or to quote the up-to-date 
term--superphysical world. let us review the cat to-day, and see to what 
extent this past regard of it is justified. 

"firstly, with respect to it as the harbinger of fortune. has a cat 
insight into the future? can it presage wealth or death? i am inclined 
to believe that certain cat can at all event foresee the advent of the 
latter; and that they do this in the same manner as the shark, crow, 
owl, jackal, hyena, etc., viz. by their abnormal develop sense of 
smell. my own and other people's experience has led me to believe that 
when a person is about to die, some kind of phantom, maybe, a spirit 
whose special function it is to be present on such occasions, is in 
close proximity to the sick or injure one, wait to escort his or her 
soul into the world of shadows--and that certain cat scent its 
approach. 

"therein then--in this wonderful property of smell--lies one of the 
secret to the cat's mysterious powers, it has the psychic faculty of 
scent--of scent ghosts. some people, too, have this faculty. in a 
recent murder case, in the north of england, a rustic witness gave it in 
her evidence that she was sure a tragedy was about to happen because she 
"smelt death in the house," and it made her very uneasy. cat possess 
this peculiarity are affected in a similar manner--they are uneasy. 

"before a death in a house i have watch a cat show gradual 
increase sign of uneasiness. it has move from place to place, unable 
to settle in any one spot for any length of time, had frequent fit of 
shivering, gone to the door, sniff the atmosphere, thrown back its 
head and mew in a low, plaintive key, and shown the greatest 
reluctance to being alone in the dark. 

"this faculty--possessed by certain cats--may in some measure explain 
certain of the superstition respecting them. take, for example, that of 
cat crossing one's path predict death. 

"the cat is drawn to the spot because it scent the phantom of death, 
and cannot resist its magnetic attraction. 

"from this, it do not follow that the person who see the cat is go 
to die, but that death is overtake someone associate with that 
person; and it is in connection with the latter that the spirit of the 
grave is present, employing, as a medium of prognostication, the cat, 
which has been given the psychic faculty of smell that it might be so 
used. 

"but although i regard this theory as very feasible, i do not attribute 
to cats, with the same degree of certainty, the power to presage good 
fortune, simply because i have had no experience of it myself. yet, 
adopt the same lines of argument, i see no reason why cat should not 
prognosticate good as well as evil. 

"there may be phantom representative of prosperity, in just the same 
manner as there are those representative of death; they, too, may also 
have some distinguish scent (flowers have various odours, so why not 
spirits?) and certain cats, i.e. white cat in particular, may be 
attract by it. 

"this become all the more probable when one consider how very 
impressionable the cat is--how very sensitive to kindness. there are 
some stranger with whom the cat will at once make friends, and other 
whom it will studious avoid. why? the explanation, i fancy, lie once 
more in the occult--in the cat's psychic faculty of smell. kind people 
attract benevolent disposed phantoms, which bring with them an 
agreeably scent atmosphere, that, in turn, attract cats. the cat 
come to one person because it know by the smell of the atmosphere 
surrounding him, or her, that it has nothing to fear--that the person is 
essentially gentle and benignant. on the contrary, cruel people attract 
malevolent phantoms, distinguishable also to the cat by their smell, a 
smell typical of cruelty--often of homicidal lunacy (i have particularly 
notice how cat have shrunk from people who have afterwards become 
dangerous insane). is this sense of smell, then, the keynote to the 
halo of mystery that has for all times surround the cat--that has led 
to its bitter persecution--that has made it the hero of fairy lore, the 
pet of old maids? i believe it is--i believe that in this psychic 
faculty of smell lies, in degree, the solution to the oft-asked 
riddle--why is the cat uncanny? have then satisfy oneself on this 
point, namely, that cat are in the possession of rare psychic 
properties, is it likely that the unknown powers which have so endow 
them, should withhold from them either soul or spirits? is it not 
contrary to reason, instinct, and observation to suppose that the many 
thorough material and gross minded people--people whose whole being 
are steep in money worship--we see around us every day should have 
spirits, and that pretty, refined and artistic-looking cats, whose 
occult powers place them in the very closest connection with the 
superphysical, should not? monstrous--the bare conception of such 
incongruity in the one case, and such an omission in the other, is 
inconceivable, wholly irreconcilable with the notion of any other than a 
mummer of a creator--a mere court fool of a god." 




chapter ii 

apparition of dogs 


one of the most extraordinary case of haunting by the phantasm of 
dogs is related in an old christmas number of the _review of reviews_, 
edit by the late mr. w.t. stead, and entitle "real ghost stories." 

"the most remarkable," write mr. stead, "of all the story which i 
have heard concerning ghost which touch is one that reach me from 
darlington. i owe this, as i owe so many of the other narrative in this 
collection, to the rev. harry kendall, of darlington, whose painstaking 
perseverance in the collection of all matter of this kind cannot be too 
highly praised. mr. kendall is a congregational minister of old 
standing. he was my pastor when i was edit the _northern echo_, and 
he is the author of a remarkable book, entitle _all the world's akin_. 
the following narrative is quite unique in its way, and fortunately he 
was able to get it at first hand from the only living person present. 
here we have a ghost which not only strike the first blow, hit a 
man fair in the eye, but afterwards set a ghostly dog upon his victim 
and then disappears. the narrative was sign by mr. james durham as 
lately as december 5th, 1890." mr. stead then proceeds to quote the 
account which he had from mr. kendall, and which i append _ad verbum_ 
from the _review of reviews_. it is as follows: "i was night watchman at 
the old darlington and stockton station at the town of darlington, a few 
yard from the first station that ever existed. i was there fifteen 
years. i use to go on duty about 8 p.m. and come off at 6 a.m. i had 
been there a little while--perhaps two or three years--and about forty 
years ago. one night during winter at about 12 o'clock or 12.30 i was 
feeling rather cold with standing here and there; i said to myself, 'i 
will away down and get something to eat.' there was a porter's cellar 
where a fire was kept on and a coal-house was connected with it. so i 
went down the steps, took off my overcoat, and had just sat down on the 
bench opposite the fire and turn up the gas when a strange man came 
out of the coal-house, follow by a big black retriever. as soon as he 
enter my eye was upon him, and his eye upon me, and we were intent 
watch each other as he move on to the front of the fire. there he 
stood look at me, and a curious smile came over his countenance. he 
had a stand-up collar and a cut-away coat with gilt buttons and a scotch 
cap. all at once he struck at me, and i had the impression that he hit 
me. i up with my fist and struck back at him. my fist seem to go 
through him and struck against the stone above the fireplace, and 
knock the skin off my knuckles. the man seem to be struck back into 
the fire, and utter a strange, unearthly squeak. immediately the dog 
grip me by the calf of my leg, and seem to cause me pain. the man 
recover his position, call off the dog with a sort of click of the 
tongue, then went back into the coal-house, follow by the dog. i 
light my dark lantern and look into the coal-house, but there was 
neither dog nor man, and no outlet for them except the one by which they 
had entered. 

"i was satisfy that what i had seen was ghostly, and it account for 
the fact that when the man had first come into the place where he sat i 
had not challenge him with any enquiry. next day, and for several 
weeks, my account cause quite a commotion, and a host of people spoke 
to me about it; among the rest old edward pease, father of railways, and 
his three sons, john, joseph, and henry. old edward sent for me to his 
house and ask me all particulars. he and other put this question to 
me: "are you sure you were not asleep and had the nightmare?" my answer 
was quite sure, for i had not been a minute in the cellar, and was just 
go to get something to eat. i was certainly not under the influence 
of strong drink, for i was then, as i have been for forty-nine years, a 
teetotaler. my mind at the time was perfectly free from trouble. what 
increase the excitement was the fact that a man a number of years 
before, who was employ in the office of the station, had committed 
suicide, and his body had been carry into this very cellar. i knew 
nothing of this circumstance, nor of the body of the man, but mr. pease 
and other who had known him, told me my description exactly 
correspond to his appearance and the way he dressed, and also that he 
had a black retriever just like the one which grip me. i should add 
that no mark or effect remain on the spot where i seem to be seized. 

"(signed) james durham. 
"_dec. 9th, 1890._" 

following the above statement mr. stead append mr. kendall's reason 
for believe that what james durham experienced was objective psychic 
phenomena, and neither produce during sleep nor by hallucination. 

the argument use strike me as being so concise and sensible that i 
think it will not be out of place to reproduce them. 

"first," mr. kendall says, "he (james durham) was accustomed as watchman 
to be up all night, and therefore not likely from that cause to feel 
sleepy. secondly, he had scarcely been a minute in the cellar, and, 
feeling hungry, was just go to get something to eat. thirdly, if he 
was asleep at the beginning of the vision, he must have been awake 
enough during the latter part of it when he had knock the skin off his 
knuckles. fourthly, there was his own confident testimony. i strong 
incline to the opinion that there was an objective cause for the vision, 
and that it was genuine apparitional." 

so interested was mr. kendall in the case that he visit the spot some 
short time later. he was taken into the cellar where the manifestation 
took place, and his guide, an old official of the north road station, 
informed him he well remember the clerk--a man of the name of 
winter--who committed suicide there, and show him the exact spot where 
he had shot himself with a pistol. in dress and appearance mr. winter 
correspond minutely with the phenomenon describe by james durham, and 
he had had a black retriever. 

mr. kendal came away more convinced than ever of the veracity of james 
durham's story, though he admit it was not evident after the high 
standard of the s.p.r. i do not know whether the s.p.r. publish the 
case, and i certainly do not think mr. kendall need have minded if they 
did not--for after all there is no reason to suppose the judgment of the 
s.p.r. is always infallible. 

mr. stead do not comment on the apparition of the dog, which leads one 
to suppose case of animal phantasm were by no means uncommon to him. 


_the grey dog of ---- house, birmingham_ 

according to a story current in the midlands, a house in birmingham, 
near the roman catholic cathedral, was once very badly haunted. a family 
who took up their abode in it in the 'eighties complain of hearing all 
sort of uncanny sounds--such as scream and sighs--coming from a room 
behind the kitchen. on one occasion the tenant's wife, on enter the 
sitting-room, was almost startle out of her senses at seeing, standing 
before the fireplace, the figure of a tall, stout man with a large, grey 
dog by his side. what was so alarm about the man was his face--it was 
apparently a mere blob of flesh without any features in it. the lady 
scream out, whereupon there was a terrific crash, as if all the 
crockery in the house had been sudden clash on the stone floor; and 
a friend of the lady's, attract to the spot by the noise, saw two 
cloud of vapour, one resemble a man and the other a dog, which, after 
hover over the hearth for several seconds, finally disperse 
altogether. 

a gasfitter, when working in the house, saw the same figures no less 
than nine times, and so distinct that he was able to give a detailed 
description of both the man and dog. 

the house seem to have been well known in birmingham, and was certainly 
standing as recently as 1885. many theory were advanced as to its 
history, the one gain most credence being that it was occupied, in 
1829, by a man who supply the medical student with human bodies. 

it was notice at the time that many people who were seen to enter the 
house in the company of the owner were never seen to leave it, which 
accord well with the theory of resurrection men. 

no suggestion has been offer to account for the animal, which may very 
easily have been the phantom of the murderer's dog, or, what is rather 
less likely, the dog of one of his numerous victims. 

anyhow, explanation or no explanation, the fact remains the house was 
haunt in the manner described, and f. grey, a warwickshire chief 
constable, in his _recollections_, publish 1821, allude to it. 


_the dog in the cupboard_ 

miss prettyman, whom i met some years ago in cornwall, told me she once 
live in a house in westmorland that was haunt by the apparition of a 
large dog, envelop in a blueish glow, which apparently emanate from 
within it. the dog, whilst appear in all parts of the house, 
invariable vanish in a big cupboard at the back of the hall staircase. 
miss prettyman, her family, several of their visitors, and the servant 
all saw the same phantasm, and were, perhaps, more frightened by the 
sudden of its advent than by its actual appearance. 

the theory was that it was the ghost of some dog that had been cruel 
done to death--possibly by starvation--in the cupboard. 


_how the ghost of a dog save life_ 

when i was a boy, an elderly friend of mine, miss lefanu, narrate to me 
an anecdote which impress me much. it was to this effect. 

miss lefanu was walking one day along a very lonely country lane, when 
she sudden observe an enormous newfoundland dog following in her wake 
a few yard behind. being very fond of dogs, she call out to it in a 
caress voice and endeavour to stroke it. to her disappointment, 
however, it dodge aside, and repeated the manoeuvre every time she 
try to touch it. at length, lose patience, she desisted, and resume 
her walk, the dog still following her. in this fashion they went on, 
until they came to a particularly dark part of the road, where the 
branch of the tree almost met overhead, and there was a pool of 
stagnant, slimy water, suggestive of great depth. on the one side the 
hedge was high, but on the other there was a slight gap leading into a 
thick spinney. miss lefanu never visit the spot alone after dusk, and
