had been warn against it even in the daytime. as she drew near to it, 
everything that she had ever heard about it flash across her mind, and 
she was more than once on the verge of turning back, when the sight of 
the big, friendly-looking dog plod behind, reassure her, she 
pressed on. just as she came to the gap, there was a loud snap of 
twigs, and, to her horror, two tramps, with singularly sinister faces, 
sprang out, and were about to strike her with their bludgeons, when the 
dog, utter a low, ominous growl, dashed at them. in an instant the 
expression of murderous joy in their eyes die out, one of abject terror 
took its place, and, drop their weapons, they fled, as if the very 
salvation of their soul depend on it. as may be imagined, miss lefanu 
lost no time in get home, and the first thing she did on arrive 
there was to go into the kitchen and order the cook to prepare, at once, 
a thorough good meal for her gallant rescuer--the newfoundland dog, 
which she had shut up secure in the back yard, with the laugh 
remark, "there--you can't escape me now." judge of her astonishment, 
however, when, on her return, the dog had gone. as the wall of the back 
yard were twelve feet high, and the door had been shut all the 
while--no one have pass through them--it was impossible for the 
animal to have escaped, and the only interpretation that could possibly 
be put on the matter was that the dog was superphysical--a conclusion 
that was subsequent confirmed by the experience of various other 
people. as the result of exhaustive enquiry miss lefanu eventually 
learned that many years before, on the very spot where the tramp had 
leap out on her, a pedlar and his newfoundland dog had been discover 
murdered. 

this story being true, then, there is one more link in the chain of 
evidence to show that dogs, as well as men, have spirits, and spirits 
that can, on occasion, at least, perform deed of practical service. 


_a precentor's story_ 

the late mr. w.t. stead, in his volume of _real ghost stories_, narrate 
the following, which by reason of its being witness by three people 
simultaneously, may be regard as highly evidential. 

in reply to mr. stead's request to hear the anecdote the precentor say 
(i quote him _ad verbum_): 

"i was walking, about nine years ago, one night in august, about ten 
o'clock, and about half a mile from the house where we are now sitting. 
i was go along the public road between the hamlet of mill of haldane 
and ballock. i had with me two young women, and we were leisurely 
walking along, when sudden we were startle by seeing a woman, a child 
about seven years old, and a newfoundland dog jump over the stone wall 
which was on one side of the road, and walk on rapid in front of us. i 
was not in the least frightened, but my two companion were very much 
startled. what bother me was that the woman, the child, and the dog, 
instead of coming over the wall naturally one after the other, as would 
have been necessary for them to do, had come over with a bound, 
simultaneous leap the wall, lighting on the road, and then hurry 
on without a word. leave my two companions, who were too frightened to 
move, i walk rapid after the trio. they walk on so quick that it 
was with difficulty that i got up to them. i spoke to the woman, she 
never answered. i walk beside her for some little distance, and then 
sudden the woman, the child, and the newfoundland dog disappeared. i 
did not see them go anywhere, they simply were no long there. i 
examine the road minutely, at the spot where they had disappeared, to 
see if it was possible for them to have gone through a hole in the wall 
on either side; but it was quite impossible for a woman and a child to 
get over a high dyke on either side. they had disappeared, and i only 
regret that i did not try to pass my stick right through their bodies, 
to see whether or not they had any resistance. finding they had gone, i 
return to my lady friends, who were quite unnerved, and who, with 
difficulty, were induce to go on to the end of their journey." 

one of his companions, mr. stead go on to explain, who heard him tell 
the story at the time, corroborate the fact that it had made a great 
impression on those who had seen it. nothing was ever ascertain as to 
any woman, child, or newfoundland dog that had ever been in the district 
before. when they got to ballock they enquire of the keeper of the 
bridge whether a woman, a child, and a dog had pass that way, but he 
had seen nothing. the apparition had disappear as sudden as it had 
appeared. mr. stead's article end here. of course, one can only surmise 
as to the nature of the phenomena. no member of the psychic research 
society could do more--and in the absence of any authentic history of 
the spot where the manifestation occurred, such a surmise can be of 
little value. since the phenomena were seen by three people at the same 
time, it is quite safe to assume they were objective, but it is 
impossible to lay down the law as to whether they were actual phantasm 
of the dead--of a woman, child, and newfoundland dog who had all three 
met with some violent end--or phantasm of three living beings, who, 
happening to think of that locality at the same time, had project 
their immaterial body there simultaneously. but whichever of these 
alternative be true, the same thing hold good in either case, viz. 
that the newfoundland dog had a spirit--and what apply to one dog 
should assured apply to the generality, if not, indeed, to all. 


_phantom dog seen on souter fell_ 

miss harriet martineau, in her _english lakes_, refer to certain 
strange phenomena seen from time to time on souter fell. 

in 1745, for example, a mr. wren and his servant saw, simultaneously, a 
man and dog pursue some horse along a razor-like ridge of rocks, on 
which it was obviously impossible for any ordinary being to gain a bare 
foothold, let alone walk. they watch the figures until the latter 
sudden vanished, when mr. wren and his servant, thinking, perhaps, the 
man, dog, and horse had really fallen over the cliff, went to look for 
them. they search elsewhere, but despite their vigilance, nothing was 
to be found, and convinced at last that what they had seen was something 
superphysical, they came away mystified, and no doubt somewhat 
frightened. 

there is no suggestion to make here other than the manifestation may 
have been the phantasm of a man, dog, and horse that at some former 
date had been killed, either accidental or purposely, in or near that 
spot. 


_the jump ghost_ 

mr. george sinclair, in his work _satan's invisible world discovered_, 
give a detailed account of haunting in a house in mary king's close, 
edinburgh. 

the house, at the time mr. sinclair writes, was occupy by mr. thomas 
coltheart, a law agent. seat one afternoon at home reading, mrs. 
coltheart was immeasurable startle at seeing, suspend in mid-air 
gaze at her, the head of an old man. she utter some sort of 
exclamation, most probably a cry, and the apparition at once vanished. 
some nights later, when in bed, both she and her husband saw the same 
head, which was presently join by the head of a child, and a long, 
naked arm, which try to catch hold of them. 

on another occasion, a member of the coltheart family was greatly 
alarm by the sudden appearance of a large dog, which leap on the 
chair by her side, and as sudden disappeared. 

every effort was made to lay the ghosts. ministers--and one know how 
pious scotch clergymen are--were call in, but their exhortations, 
instead of dispel or even minimize the phenomena, only increase 
them. it was a case of more prayers, more spooks; which state of 
affairs, however complimentary to the ministers' powers of address, was 
scarcely as comfort to the colthearts, who, unable to bear the 
strange sight and noise any longer, evacuate the premises. as no 
other tenant could be found, the house was eventually pull down, and 
a row of fine modern building now occupy the site. as the history of 
the place could never be trace with any degree of authenticity, one can 
do no more than speculate as to the cause of the disturbances, which, i 
am inclined to think, were due to the phantom of people and animal 
that had once actually live and die there. 


_dogs seen before a death_ 

mrs. crowe, in her _night side of nature_, mention the case of a young 
lady name p----, who saw a big black dog twice sudden appear and 
disappear by her side, immediately before the death of her mother. 

in _the unseen world_ a story is also told of the phantasm of a big 
black dog appear on the bed of a cornish child, doom to die shortly 
afterwards, the same dog invariable manifest itself before the death 
of any member of the child's family. 

there are so many case of a similar kind--one hear of them nearly 
everywhere one goes--that one is led to believe some of them, at least, 
must be true. there is no more reason to believe all ghost-story teller 
are liars, than there is to believe all parson are liars--and this 
being so, additional proof is afford of the continuation of the dog's 
life after death; for these family canine ghost are more than probably 
the phantasm of dogs that once belong to families--maybe century 
ago--and met their fate in some cruel and unnatural manner. 


_a dog scared by a canine ghost_ 

a friend of mine, edward morgan, had a terrier that was found one 
morning, poison in a big stone kennel. soon afterwards this friend 
came to me and said, "i have got a new dog--a spaniel--but nothing will 
induce it to enter the kennel in which poor zack was poisoned. come and 
see!" 

i did so, and what he said was true. mack (morgan gave all his dogs 
name that rhymed--zack, mack, jack, tack, and even whack and smack), 
when carry to the entrance of the kennel, resolute refuse to cross 
the threshold, barking, whining, and exhibit unmistakable symptom of 
fear. i knelt down, and peer into the kennel saw two luminous eyes 
and the distinct outline of a dog's head. 

"morgan!" i exclaimed, "the mystery is easily solved; there's a dog in 
here." 

"nonsense!" morgan cried, speak very excitedly. 

"but there is," i retorted, "see for yourself." 

morgan immediately bent down and poke his head into the kennel. 

"what rot," he said. "you're have me on, there's nothing here." 

"what!" i cried, "do you mean to say you can see no dog?" 

"no!" he replied, "there is none!" 

"let me look again!" i said, and kneel down, i peep in. 

"do you mean to say you can't see a dog's face and eyes look straight 
at us?" i asked. 

"no," he answered, "i can see nothing." and to prove to me the truth of 
what he said, he fetch a pole and rake about the kennel vigorous 
with it. we both, then, try to make mack enter, and morgan at last 
caught hold of him and placed him forcibly inside. mack's terror knew no 
limit. he gave one loud howl, and flying out of the kennel with his ear 
hanging back, tore past into the front garden, where we left him in 
peace. morgan was still sceptical as to there being anything wrong with 
the kennel, but two days later wrote to me as follows:-- 

"i must apologize for doubt you the other day. i have just had, what 
you declared you saw, corroborated. a friend of my wife's was calling 
here this afternoon, and, on hearing of mack's refusal to sleep in the 
kennel, at once said, 'i know what's the matter. it's the smell. mack 
scent the poison which was use to destroy zack. have the kennel 
thorough fumigated, and you'll have no more trouble.' at my wife's 
request she went into the yard to have a look at it, and the moment she 
bent down, she cry out like you did, 'why, there's a dog inside--a 
terrier!' my wife and i both look and could see nothing. the lady, 
however, persisted, and, on my hand her a stick, struck at the figure 
she saw. to her amaze the stick went right through it. then, and not 
till then, did we tell her of your experience. 'well!' she exclaimed, 
'i have never believe in ghosts, but i do so now. i am quite certain 
that what i see is the phantom of zack! how glad i am, because i am at 
last assured animal have spirits and can come back to us.'" 

in conclude the account of phantasm of dead dogs, let me quote two 
case taken from my work entitle _the haunt house of london_, 
publish by mr. eveleigh nash, of fawside house, king street, covent 
garden, london, w.c., in 1909. the case are these:-- 


_the phantom dachshund of w---- st., london, w._ 

in letter no. 1 my correspondent writes:-- 

"though i am by no means over-indulgent to dogs, the latter generally 
greet me very effusively, and it would seem that there is something in 
my individuality that is peculiarly attractive to them. this being so, i 
was not greatly surprise one day, when in the immediate neighbourhood 
of x---- street, to find myself persistent follow by a rough-haired 
dachshund wearing a gaudy yellow collar. i try to scare it away by 
shake my sunshade at it, but all to no purpose--it came resolute on; 
and i was beginning to despair of get rid of it, when i came to 
x---- street, where my husband once practised as an oculist. there it 
sudden alter its tactics, and instead of keeping at my heels, 
became my conductor, forging slow ahead with a gliding motion that 
both puzzle and fascinate me. i furthermore observe that 
notwithstanding the temperature--it was not a whit less than ninety 
degree in the shade--the leg and stomach of the dachshund were cover 
with mud and dripping with water. when it came to no. 90 it halted, and 
veer swift round, eye me in the strangest manner, just as if it 
had some secret it was burst to disclose. it remain in this 
attitude until i was within two or three feet of it--certainly not 
more--when, to my unlimited amazement, it absolutely vanished--melted 
away into thin air. 

"the iron gate leading to the area was closed, so that there was nowhere 
for it to have hidden, and, besides, i was almost bend over it at the 
time, as i want to read the name on its collar. there being no one 
near at hand, i could not obtain a second opinion, and so came away 
wonder whether what i had seen was actually a phantasm or a mere 
hallucination. no. 90, i might add, judge by the brass plate on the 
door, was inhabit by a doctor with an unpronounceable foreign name," 
etc. etc. 

i think one cannot help attach a great deal of importance to what 
this lady says, as her language is strict moderate throughout, and 
because she do not seem to have been bias by any special view on 
the subject of animal futurity. 

correspondent no. 2 (who, by the way, is a total stranger to the writer 
whose letter i have just quoted) is candidly devoted to dogs, regarding 
them as in every way on a par with, if not actually superior to, most 
human beings. still, notwithstanding this partiality, and consequent 
profusion of terms of endearment, which will doubtless prove somewhat 
nauseate to many, her letter is, in my opinion, valuable, because it 
not only refer to the phenomenon i have mentioned, but to a certain 
extent furnish a reason for its occurrence. the lady write as 
follows:-- 

"i once had a rough-haired dachshund, robert, whom i love devotedly. we 
were living at the time near h---- street, which always had a peculiar 
attraction for dear robert, who, i am now oblige to confess, had rather 
too much liberty--more, indeed, than eventually prove good for him. the 
servant complain that robert rule the house, and i believe what they 
said was true, for my sister and i idolize him, give him the very 
best of everything and never have the heart to refuse him anything he 
wanted. you will probably scarcely credit it, but i have sat up all 
night nursing him when he had a cold and was otherwise indisposed. can 
you therefore imagine my feelings when my darling was absent one day 
from dinner? such a thing had never happen before, for, fond of 
morning 'constitutionals' as poor robert was, he was always the soul of 
punctual at meal times. 

"neither my sister nor i would hear of eat anything. whilst he was 
missing, not a morsel did we touch, but slip on our hats, and 
bidding the servant do the same, we scour the neighbourhood instead. 
the afternoon pass without any sign of robert, and when bedtime came 
(he always slept in our room) and still no sign of our pet, i thought 
we should both have gone mad. of course, we advertised, select the 
most popular and, accordingly, the most likely papers, and we resort 
to other mediums, too, but, alas! it was hopeless. our darling little 
robert was irrevocably, irredeemable lost. for days we were utterly 
inconsolable, do nothing but mope morning, noon, and night. i cannot 
tell you how forlorn we felt, nor how long we should have remain in 
that state but for an incident which, although revealing the terrible 
manner of his death, gave us every reason to feel sure we were not 
part from him for all time, but would meet again in the great 
hereafter. it happen in this wise: i was walking along w---- street 
one evening when, to my intense joy and surprise, i sudden saw my 
darling standing on the pavement a few feet ahead of me, regarding me 
intent from out of his pathetic brown eyes. a sensation of extreme 
cold now stole over me, and i notice with something akin to a shock 
that, in spite of the hot, dry weather, robert look as if he had been 
in the rain for hours. he wore the bright yellow collar i had bought him 
shortly before his disappearance, so that had there been any doubt as to 
his identity that would have remove it instantly. on my calling to him, 
he turn quick round and, with a slight gesture of the head as if 
bidding me to follow, he glide forward. my natural impulse was to run 
after him, pick him up and smother him with kisses; but try as hard as i 
could, i could not diminish the distance between us, although he never 
appear to alter his pace. i was quite out of breath by the time we 
reach h---- street, where, to my surprise, he stop at no. 90 and, 
turning round again, gaze at me in the most beseech manner. i can't 
describe that look; suffice it to say that no human eyes could have been 
more expressive, but of what beyond the most profound love and sorrow i 
cannot, i dare not, attempt to state. i have ponder upon it through 
the whole of a mid-summer night, but not even the severest of my mental 
effort have enable me to solve it to my satisfaction. could i but do 
that, i feel i should have fathom the greatest of all mysteries--the 
mystery of life and death. 

"i do not know for how long we stood there look at one another, it 
may have been minutes or hours, or, again, but a few paltry seconds. he 
took the initiative from me, for, as i leap forward to raise him in my 
arms, he glide through the stone steps into the area. 

"convinced now that what i beheld was robert's apparition, i determined 
to see the strange affair through to the bitter end, and enter the 
gate, i also went down into the area. the phantom had come to an abrupt 
halt by the side of a low wooden box, and as i foolish made an 
abortive attempt to reach it with my hand, it vanish instantaneously. 
i search the area thoroughly, and was assured that there was no 
outlet, save by the steps i had just descended, and no hole, nor nook, 
nor cranny where anything the size of robert could be completely hidden 
from sight. what did it all mean? ah! i knew robert had always had a 
weakness for explore areas, especially in h---- street, and in the box 
where his wraith disappear i espy a piece of raw meat! 

"now there are ways in which a piece of raw meat may lie without 
arouse suspicion, but the position of this morsel strange suggest 
that it had been placed there carefully, and for assured no other 
purpose than to entice stray animals. resolve to interrogate the owner 
of the house on the subject, i rap at the front door, but was 
informed by the manservant, obviously a german, that his master never 
saw anyone without an appointment. i then did a very unwise thing--i 
explain the purpose of my visit to this man, who not only deny any 
knowledge of my dog, but declared the meat must have been thrown into 
the area by some passer-by. 

"'no one in dis house trow away gut meat like dat,' he explained, 'we 
eat all we can git here, we have nut for de animals. please go away 
at once, or de master will be very angry. he stand no nonsense from 
anyone.' 

"and as i had no alternative--for, after all, who would regard a ghost 
in the light of evidence?--i had to obey. i found out, however, from a 
medical friend that no. 90 was tenant by mr. k----, an anglo-german 
who was deem a very clever fellow at a certain london hospital, where 
he was often occupy in vivisection. 

"'i dare say,' my friend went on to remark, 'k---- do a little 
vivisect in his private surgery, by way of practice, and--well, you 
see, these foreign chaps are not so squeamish in some respects as we 
are.' 

"'but can't he be stopped?' i asked. 'it is horrible, monstrous that he 
should be allow to murder our pets.' 

"'you don't know for certain that he has,' was the reply, 'you only 
suppose so from what you say you saw, and evidence of that immaterial 
nature is no evidence at all. no, you can do nothing except to be extra 
careful in future, and if you have another dog make him steer clear of 
no. 90 h---- street.' 

"i was sensible enough to see that he was right, and the matter dropped. 
i soon notice one thing, however, namely, that there were no more 
pieces of meat tempting display in the box, so it is just possible 
k---- got wind of my enquiries, and thought it policy to desist from his 
nefarious practices. 

"poor robert! to think of him suffering such a cruel and ignominious 
death, and my being powerless to avenge it. surely if vivisection is 
really necessary, and the welfare of mankind cannot be advanced by any 
less barbarous system, why not operate on creature less deserving of 
our love and pity than dogs? on creature which whilst being near 
allied to man in physiology and anatomy, are at the same time far below 
the level of brute creation in character and disposition. 

"for example, why not experiment on wife-beaters and cowardly street 
ruffians, and, one might reasonably add, on all those 
pseudo-humanitarians who, by their constant petition to parliament for 
the abolition of the lash, encourage every form of blackguard and 
bestiality?" 

this conclude the letter of correspondent no. 2, and with the sentiment 
in the close paragraph i must say i heartily agree--only i should 
like to add a few more people to the list. 

one other case of haunting of this type is taken from my same work. 

"one all hallow e'en," wrote a mrs. sebuim, "i was stay with some 
friends in hampstead, and we amuse ourselves by working spells, to 
commemorate the night. there is one spell in which one walk alone down 
a path sow hempseed, and repeating some fantastic words; when one is 
supposed to see those that are destined to come into one's life in the 
near future. eager to put this spell to the test, i went into the garden 
by myself and, walking bold along a path, border on each side by 
evergreens, sprinkle hempsee lavishly. 

"nothing happening, i was about to desist, when sudden i heard a 
patter on the gravel, and turning round i beheld an ugly little 
black-and-tan mongrel running towards me, wag its stumpy tail. not 
at all prepossessed with the creature, for my own dogs are pure-bred, 
and thinking it must have stray into the grounds, i was about to 
drive it out, and had put down my hand to prevent it jump on my 
dress, when, to my astonishment, it had vanished. it literally melt 
away into fine air beneath my very eyes. not knowing what to make of the 
incident, but feeling inclined to attribute it to a trick of the 
imagination, i rejoin my friends. i did not tell them what had 
happened, although i made a memorandum of it in one of my innumerable 
notebooks. within six month of this incident i was greatly astonish 
to find a dog, corresponding with the one i have just described, running 
about on the lawn of my house in bath. how the animal got there was a 
complete mystery, and, what is stranger still, it seem to recognize 
me, for it rush towards me, frantic wag its diminutive tail. i 
had not the heart to turn it away, as it seem quite homeless, and so 
the forlorn little mongrel was permit to make its home in my 
house--and a very happy home it prove to be. for three years all went 
well, and then the end came swift and unexpectedly. i was in 
blackheath at the time, and the mongrel was in bath. it was all hallow 
e'en, but there was no hempsee sowing, for no one in the house but 
myself took the slightest interest in anything appertain to the 
superphysic or mystic. eleven o'clock came, and i retired to rest; my 
bed being one of those antique four-posters, hung with curtains that 
shine crimson in the ruddy glow of a cheerful fire. all my preparation 
complete, i had pull back the hangings, and was about to slip in 
between the sheets, when, to my unbounded amazement, what should i see 
sitting on the counterpane but the black-and-tan mongrel. it was he 
right enough, there could not be another such ugly dog, though, unlike 
his usual self, he evince no demonstration of joy. on the contrary, he 
appear downright miserable. his ear hung, his mouth dropped, and his 
bleare little eyes were watery and sad. 

"greatly perplexed, if not alarmed, at so extraordinary a phenomenon, i 
nevertheless felt constrained to put out my hand to comfort him--when, 
as i had half anticipated, he immediately vanished. two days later i 
received a letter from bath, and in a postscript i read that 'the 
mongrel' (we never call it by any other name) 'had been run over and 
kill by a motor, the accident occur on all hallow e'en, about 
eleven o'clock.' 'of course,' my sister wrote, 'you won't mind very 
much--it was so extremely ugly, and--well--we were only too glad it was 
none of the other dogs.' but my sister was wrong, for notwithstanding 
its unsightly appearance and hopeless lack of breed, i had grown to like 
that little black-and-tan more than any of my rare and choice pets." 

the following account, which conclude my note on haunting by dog 
phantasms, was sent me many years ago by a gentleman then living in 
virginia, u.s.a. it runs thus:-- 


_the strange disappearance of mr. jeremiah dance_ 

"twenty pound a year for a twelve-roomed house with large front lawn, 
good stabling and big kitchen gardens. that sound all right," i 
commented. "but why so cheap?" 

"well," the advertiser--mr. baldwin by name, a short, stout gentleman, 
with keen, glittering eyes--replied, "well, you see, it's a bit of a 
distance from the town, and--er--most people prefer being nearer--like 
neighbour and all that sort of thing." 

"like neighbours!" i exclaimed. "i don't. i've just seen about enough of 
them. drain all right?" 

"oh, yes! perfect." 

"water?" 

"excellent." 

"everything in good condition?" 

"first rate." 

"loneliness the only thing people object to?" 

"that is so." 

"then i'll oblige you to send someone to show me over the house, for i 
think it is just the sort of place we want. you see, after being bottle 
up in a theatre all the afternoon and evening, one likes to get away 
somewhere where it is quiet--somewhere where one can lie in bed in the 
morning inhale pure air and undisturbed by street traffic." 

"i understand," mr. baldwin responded, "but--er--it is rather late now; 
wouldn't you prefer to see over it in the morning? everything looks at 
its worst--its very worst--in the twilight." 

"oh, i'll make allowance for the dusk," i said. "you haven't got any 
ghost stow away there, have you?" and he went off into a roar of 
laughter. 

"no, the house is not haunted," mr. baldwin replied. "not that it would 
much matter to you if it were, for i can see you don't believe in 
spooks." 

"believe in spooks!" i cried. "not much. i would as soon believe in 
patent hair restorers. let me see over it at once." 

"very well, sir. i'll take you there myself," mr. baldwin replied, 
somewhat reluctantly. "here, tim--fetch the key of the crow's nest and 
tell higgin to bring the trap round." 

the boy he address flew, and in a few minutes the sound of wheels and 
the jingle of harness announce the vehicle was at the door. 

ten minutes later and i and my escort were bowling merry over the 
ground in the direction of the crow's nest. it was early autumn, and the 
cool evening air, fragrant with the mellow of the luscious virginia 
pippin, was tinge also with the sad inseparable from the demise of 
a long and glorious summer. evidence of decay and death were 
everywhere--in the brown fallen leaves of the oak and elms; in the bare 
and denude ditches. here a giant mill-wheel, half immerse in a dark, 
still pool, stood idle and silent; there a hovel, but recently inhabit 
by hop-pickers, was now tenantless, its glassless window board over, 
and a wealth of dead and rot vegetable matter in thick profusion 
over the tiny path and the single stone doorstep. 

"is it always as quiet and desert as this?" i ask of my companion, 
who continual cracked his whip as if he like to hear the 
reverberation of its echoes. 

"always," was the reply, "and sometimes more so. you ain't use to the 
country?" 

"not very. i want to try it by way of a change. are you well versed in 
the cry of birds? what was that?" 

we were fast approach an exceedingly gloomy bit of the road where 
there were plantation on each side, and the tree united their 
fantastic forked branch overhead. i thought i had never seen so 
dismal-looking a spot, and a sudden lower of the temperature made me 
draw my overcoat tight round me. 

"that--oh, a night bird of some sort," mr. baldwin replied. "an ugly 
sound, wasn't it? beastly things, i can't imagine why they were created. 
whoa--steady there, steady." 

the horse rear as he spoke, and taking a violent plunge forward, set 
off at a wild gallop. a moment later, and i utter an exclamation of 
astonishment. keeping pace with us, although apparently not moving at 
more than an ordinary walking pace, was a man of medium height, dress 
in a panama hat and albert coat. he had a thin, aquiline nose, a rather 
pronounced chin, was clean-shaven, and had a startling white 
complexion. by the side of him trot two poodles, whose close-cropped 
skin show out with remarkable perspicuity. 

"who the deuce is he?" i asked, raise my voice to a shout on account 
of the loud clatter made by the horse's hoof and the wheels. 

"who? what?" mr. baldwin shout in return. 

"why, the man walking along with us!" 

"man! i can see no man!" mr. baldwin growled. 

i look at him curiously. it may, of course, have been due to the 
terrific speed we were going, to the difficulty of holding in the horse, 
but his cheek were ashy pale, and his teeth chattered. 

"do you mean to say," i cried, "that you can see no figure walking on my 
side of the horse and actually keeping pace with it?" 

"of course i can't," mr. baldwin snapped. "no more can you. it's an 
hallucination cause by the moonlight through the branch overhead. 
i've experienced it more than once." 

"then why don't you have it now?" i queried. 

"don't ask so many questions, please," mr. baldwin shouted. "don't you 
see it is as much as i can do to hold the brute in? heaven preserve us, 
we were nearly over that time." 

the trap rose high in the air as he spoke, and then drop with such a 
jolt that i was nearly thrown off, and only save myself by the skin of 
my teeth. a few yard more the spinney ceased, and we were away out in 
the open country, plunge and galloping as if our very soul depend 
on it. from all side queer and fantastic shadow of objects, which 
certainly had no material counterpart in the moon-kissed sward of the 
rich, ripe meadows, rose to greet us, and fill the lane with their 
black-and-white wavering, ethereal forms. the evening was one of 
wonders for which i had no name--wonders associate with an icy that 
was far from agreeable. i was not at all sure which i like best--the 
black, stygian, tree-lined part of the road we had just left, or the 
wide ocean of brilliant moonbeam and streak suggestions. 

the figures of the man and the dogs were equally vivid in each. though i 
could no long doubt they were nothing mortal, they were altogether 
unlike what i had imagine ghosts. like the generality of people who are 
psychic and who have never had an experience of the superphysical, my 
conception of a phantasm was a "thing" in white that made ridiculous 
groan and still more ridiculous clank of chains. but here was 
something different, something that looked--save, perhaps, for the 
excessive pallor of its cheeks--just like an ordinary man. i knew it was 
not a man, partly on account of its extraordinary performance--no man, 
even if running at full speed, could keep up with us like that; partly 
on account of the unusual nature of the atmosphere--which was altogether 
indefinable--it brought with it; and also because of my own 
sensations--my intense horror which could not, i felt certain, have been 
generate by anything physical. 

i cogitate all this in my mind as i gaze at the figure, and in order 
to make sure it was no hallucination, i shut first one eye and then the 
other, covering them alternate with the palm of my hand. the figure, 
however, was still there, still pace along at our side with the 
regular swing, swing of the born walker. we kept on in this fashion till 
we arrive at a rusty iron gate leading, by means of a weed-covered 
path, to a low, two-storied white house. here the figures left us, and 
as it seem to me vanish at the foot of the garden wall. 

"this is the house," mr. baldwin panted, pull up with the greatest 
difficulty, the horse evince obvious antipathy to the iron gate. "and 
these are the keys. i'm afraid you must go in alone, as i dare not leave 
the animal even for a minute." 

"oh, all right," i said. "i don't mind, now that the ghost, or whatever 
you like to call it, has gone; i'm myself again." 

i jump down, and thread my way along the bramble-entangled path, 
reach the front door. on opening it, i hesitated. the big, 
old-fashioned hall, with the great, frown staircase leading to the 
gallery overhead, the many open door showing nought but bare, desert 
boards within, the grim passages, all moonlit and people only with 
queer flicker shadows, suggest much that was terrifying. i fancy 
i heard noises, noise like stealthy footstep moving from room to 
room, and tiptoe along the passage and down the staircase. once my 
heart almost stop beating as i saw what, at first, i took to be a 
white face peer at me from a far recess, but which i eventually 
discover was only a daub of whitewash; and, once again, my hair all 
but rose on end, when one of the door at which i was look swung open 
and something came forth. oh, the horror of that moment, as long as i 
live i shall never forget it. the something was a cat, just a rather 
lean but otherwise material, black tom; yet, in the state my nerves were 
then, it create almost as much horror as if it had been a ghost. of 
course, it was the figure of the walking man that was the cause of all 
this nervousness; had it not appear to me i should doubtless have 
enter the house with the utmost sang-froid, my mind set on nothing but 
the condition of the walls, drains, etc. as it was, i held back, and it 
was only after a severe mental struggle i summon up the courage to 
leave the doorway and explore. cautiously, very cautiously, with my 
heart in my mouth, i move from room to room, halting every now and then 
in dreadful suspense as the wind, sough through across the open land 
behind the house, blew down the chimney and set the window-frames 
jarring. at the commencement of one of the passage i was immeasurable 
startle to see a dark shape poke forward, and then spring hurried 
back, and was so frightened that i dare not advance to see what it was. 
moment after moment sped by, and i still stood there, the cold sweat 
ooze out all over me, and my eyes fixed in hideous expectation on the 
blank wall. what was it? what was hiding there? would it spring out on 
me if i went to see? at last, urge on by a fascination i found 
impossible to resist, i crept down the passage, my heart throb 
painful and my whole being overcome with the most sickly 
anticipations. as i drew near to the spot, it was as much as i could 
do to breathe, and my respiration came in quick jerk and gasps. six, 
five, four, two feet and i was at the dread angle. another step--taken 
after the most prodigious battle--and--nothing sprang out on me. i was 
confront only with a large piece of paper that had come loose from the 
wall, and flap backwards and forward each time the breeze from 
without rustle past it. the reaction after such an agony of suspense 
was so great, that i lean against the wall, and laugh till i cried. 
a noise, from somewhere away in the basement, calling me to myself, i 
went downstairs and investigated. again a shock--this time more sudden, 
more acute. pressed against the window-pane of one of the front 
reception-rooms was the face of a man--with corpse-like cheek and pale, 
malevolent eyes. i was petrified--every drop of my blood was congealed. 
my tongue glue to my mouth, my arms hung helpless. i stood in the 
doorway and stare at it. this went on for what seem to me an 
eternity. then came a revelation. the face was not that of a ghost but 
of mr. baldwin, who, get alarm at my long absence, had come to 
look for me. 

we left the premises together. all the way back to the town i 
thought--should i, or should i not, take the house? seen as i had seen 
it, it was a ghoulish-looking place--as weird as a paris catacomb--but 
then daylight make all the difference. view in the sunshine, it would 
be just like any other house--plain brick and mortar. i like the 
situation; it was just far enough away from a town to enable me to 
escape all the smoke and traffic, and near enough to make shop easy. 
the only obstacle were the shadows--the strange, enigmatic shadow i 
had seen in the hall and passages, and the figure of the walker. dare i 
take a house that knew such visitors? at first i said no, and then yes. 
something, i could not tell what, urge me to say yes. i felt that a 
very grave issue was at stake--that a great wrong connected in some 
manner with the mysterious figure await righting, and that the hand 
of fate pointed at me as the one and only person who could do it. 

"are you sure the house isn't haunted?" i demanded, as we slow roll 
away from the iron gate, and i lean back in my seat to light my pipe. 

"haunted!" mr. baldwin scoffed, "why, i thought you didn't believe in 
ghosts--laughed at them." 

"no more i do believe in them," i retorted, "but i have children, and we 
know how imaginative children are." 

"i can't undertake to stop their imaginations." 

"no, but you can tell me whether anyone else has imagine anything 
there. imagination is sometimes very infectious." 

"as far as i know, then, no; leastways, i have not heard tell of it." 

"who was the last tenant?" 

"mr. jeremiah dance." 

"why did he leave?" 

"how do i know? got tired of being there, i suppose." 

"how long was he there?" 

"nearly three years." 

"where is he now?" 

"that's more than i can say. why do you wish to know?" 

"why!" i repeated. "because it is more satisfactory to me to hear about 
the house from someone who has live in it. has he left no address?" 

"not that i know of, and it's more than two years since he was here." 

"what! the house has been empty all that time?" 

"two years is not very long. houses--even town houses--are frequent 
unoccupy for long than that. i think you'll like it." 

i did not speak again till the drive was over, and we drew up outside 
the landlord's house. i then said, "let me have an agreement. i've made 
up my mind to take it. three years and the option to stay on." 

that was just like me. whatever i did, i did on the spur of the moment, 
a mode of procedure that often led me into difficulties. 

a month later and my wife, children, servants, and i were all ensconce 
in the crow's nest. 

that was in the beginning of october. well, the month pass by, and 
november was fairly in before anything remarkable happened. it then came 
about in this fashion. 

jennie, my eldest child, a self-willed and rather bad-tempered girl of 
about twelve, evade the vigilance of her mother, who had forbidden her 
to go out as she had a cold, ran to the gate one evening to see if i was 
anywhere in sight. though barely five o'clock, the moon was high in the 
sky, and the shadow of the big tree had already commence their 
gambol along the roadside. 

jennie clamber up the gate as children do, and peer over, sudden 
espy what she took to be me, stride towards the house, at a swinging 
pace, and follow by two poodles. 

"poppa," she cried, "how cute of you! only to think of you bring home 
two doggies! oh, poppa, naughty poppa, what will mum say?" and climb 
over into the lane at imminent danger to life and limb, she tore 
frantic towards the figure. to her dismay, however, it was not me, 
but a stranger with a horrible white face and big glassy eyes which he 
turn down at her and stared. she was so frightened that she fainted, 
and some ten minutes later i found her lie out there on the road. from 
the description she gave me of the man and dogs, i felt quite certain 
they were the figures i had seen; though i pretended the man was a 
tramp, and assured her she would never see him again. a week passed, and 
i was beginning to hope nothing would happen, when one of the servant 
gave notice to leave. 

at first she would not say why she did not like the house, but when 
pressed made the following statement:-- 

"it's haunted, mrs. b----. i can put up with mice and beetles, but not 
with ghosts. i've had a queer sensation, as if water was fall down my 
spine, ever since i've been here, but never saw anything till last 
night. i was then in the kitchen get ready to go to bed. jane and 
emma had already gone up, and i was prepare to follow them, when, all 
of a sudden, i heard footsteps, quick and heavy, cross the gravel and 
approach the window. 

"'the boss,' say i to myself; 'maybe he's forgot the key and can't get 
in at the front door.' 

"well, i went to the window and was about to throw it open, when i got 
an awful shock. pressed against the glass, look in at me, was a 
face--not the boss's face, not the face of anyone living, but a horrid 
white thing with a droop mouth and wide-open, glassy eyes, that had 
no more expression in them than a pig. as sure as i'm standing here, 
mrs. b----, it was the face of a corpse--the face of a man that had die 
no natural death. and by its side, standing on their hind-legs, and 
staring in at me too were two dogs, both poodles--also no living things, 
but dead, horrible dead. well, they stare at me, all three of them, for 
perhaps a minute, certainly not less, and then vanished. that's why i'm 
leaving, mrs. b----. my heart was never overstrong. i always suffer 
with palpitations, and if i saw those head again, it would kill me." 

after this my wife spoke to me seriously. 

"jack," she said, "are you sure there's nothing in it? i don't think 
mary would leave us without a good cause, and the description of what 
she saw tally exactly with the figure that frightened jennie. jennie 
assure me she never said a word about it to the servants. they can't 
both have imagine it." 

i did not know what to say. my conscience prick me. without a doubt i 
ought to have told my wife of my own experience in the lane, and have 
consult her before taking the house. supposing she, or any of the 
children, should die of fright, it would be my fault. i should never 
forgive myself. 

"you've something on your mind! what is it?" my wife demanded. 

i hesitate a moment or two and then told her. the next quarter of an 
hour was one i do not care to recollect, but when it was over, and she 
had had her say, it was decided i should make enquiry and see if there 
was any possible way of get rid of the ghosts. with this end in 
view, i drove to the town, and after several fruitless effort was at 
length introduce to a mr. marsden, clerk of one of the banks, who, in 
reply to my questions, said: 

"well, mr. b----, it's just this way. i do know something, only--in a 
small place like this--one has to be so extra careful what one says. 
some years ago a mr. jeremiah dance occupy the crow's nest. he came 
here apparently a total stranger, and though often in the town, was only 
seen in the company of one person--his landlord, mr. baldwin, with 
whom--if local gossip is to be rely on--he appear to be on terms of 
the greatest familiarity. indeed, they were seldom apart, walk about 
the lane arm-in-arm, visit each other's house on alternate evenings, 
call each other "teddy" and "leslie." this state of things continue 
for nearly three years, and then people sudden began to comment on the 
fact that mr. dance had gone, or at least was no long visible. an 
errand-boy, return back to town, late one evening, swore to being 
pass on the way by a trap contain mr. baldwin and mr. dance, who 
were speak in very loud voices--just as if they were have a violent 
altercation. on reach that part of the road where the tree are 
thickest overhead, the lad overtook them, or rather mr. baldwin, 
prepare to mount into the trap. mr. dance was nowhere to be seen. and 
from that day to this nothing has ever been heard of him. as none of his 
friends or relations came forward to raise enquiries, and all his bill 
were paid--several of them by mr. baldwin--no one took the matter up. 
mr. baldwin pooh-poohed the errand-boy's story, and declared that, on 
the night in question, he had been alone in an altogether different part 
of the county, and knew nothing whatever of mr. dance's movements, 
further than that he had recently announce his intention of leave the 
crow's nest before the expiration of the three years' lease. he had not 
the remotest idea where he was. he claim the furniture in payment of 
the rent due to him." 

"did the matter end there?" i asked. 

"in one sense of the word, yes--in another, no. within a few week of 
dance's disappearance rumour got afloat that his ghost had been seen on 
the road, just where, you may say, you saw it. as a matter of fact, i've 
seen it myself--and so have crowd of other people." 

"has anyone ever spoken to it?" 

"yes--and it has vanish at once. i went there one night with the 
purpose of lay it, but, on its appear suddenly, i confess i was so 
startled, that i not only forgot what i had rehearse to say, but ran 
home, without utter as much as a word." 

"and what are your deduction of the case?" 

"the same as everyone else's," mr. marsden whispered, "only, like 
everyone else, i dare not say." 

"had mr. dance any dogs?" 

"yes--two poodles, of which, much to mr. baldwin's annoyance (everyone 
notice this), he use to make the most ridiculous fuss." 

"humph!" i observed. "that settle it! ghosts! and to think i never 
believe in them before! well, i am go to try." 

"try what?" mr. marsden said, a note of alarm in his voice. 

"try lay it. i have an idea i may succeed." 

"i wish you luck, then. may i come with you?" 

"thanks, no!" i rejoined. "i would rather go there alone." 

i said this in a well-lighted room, with the hum of a crowded 
thoroughfare in my ears. twenty minutes later, when i had left all that 
behind, and was fast approach the darkest part of an exceptional 
dark road, i wish i had not. at the very spot, where i had previous 
seen the figures, i saw them now. they sudden appear by my side, and 
though i was go at a great rate--for the horse took fright--they kept 
easy pace with me. twice i essay to speak to them, but could not 
ejaculate a syllable through sheer horror, and it was only by nerve 
myself to the utmost, and force my eyes away from them, that i was 
able to stick to my seat and hold on to the reins. on and on we dashed, 
until trees, road, sky, universe were obliterate in one blind 
whirlwind that got up my nostrils, choke my ears, and deaden me to 
everything, save the all-terrorizing, instinctive knowledge, that the 
figures by my side, were still there, stalk along as quiet and 
leisurely as if the horse had been go at a snail's pace. 

at last, to my intense relief--for never had the ride seem longer--i 
reach the crow's nest, and as i hurried dismount from the trap, 
the figures shot past me and vanished. once inside the house, and in the 
bosom of my family, where all was light and laughter, courage returned, 
and i upbraid myself bitter for this cowardice. 

i confessed to my wife, and she insist on accompany me the 
following afternoon, at twilight, to the spot where the ghost appear 
to originate. to our intense dismay, we had not been there more than 
three or four minutes, before dora, our youngest girl, a pretty, 
sweet-tempered child of eight, came running up to us with a telegram, 
which one of the servant had ask her to give us. my wife, snatch 
it from her, and reading it, was about to scold her severely, when she 
sudden paused, and clutch hold of the child with one hand, pointed 
hysterical at something on one side of her with the other. i looked, 
and dora looked, and we both saw, standing erect and staring at us, the 
spare figure of a man, with a ghastly white face and dull, lifeless 
eyes, clad in a panama hat, albert coat, and small, patent-leather 
boots; beside him were two glossy--abnormally glossy--poodles. 

i try to speak, but, as before, was too frightened to articulate a 
sound, and my wife was in the same plight. with dora, however, it was 
otherwise, and she electrify us by go up to the figure, and 
exclaiming: 

"who are you? you must feel very ill to look so white. tell me your 
name." 

the figure made no reply, but gliding slow forward, move up to a 
large, isolated oak, and point with the index finger of its left hand 
at the trunk of the tree, seemingly sank into the earth and vanish 
from view. 

for some seconds everyone was silent, and then my wife exclaimed: 

"jack, i shouldn't wonder if dora hasn't been the means of solve the 
mystery. examine the tree closely." 

i did so. the tree was hollow, and inside it were three skeletons! 

* * * * * 

here follow an extract from a local paper: 


"_sensational discovery in a wood near marytown_ 

"whilst explore in a wood, near marytown, the other evening, a party 
of the name of b---- discover three skeletons--a human being and two 
dogs--in the trunk of an oak. from the remnant of clothes still 
adhere to the human remains, the latter were prove to be those of an 
individual known as mr. jeremiah dance, whose strange disappearance from 
the crow's nest--the house he rent in the neighbourhood--some two 
years ago, was the occasion of much comment. on close examination, 
extraordinary to relate, the remains have been prove to be those of a 
woman; and from certain abrasion on the skull, there is little doubt 
she met with a violent end." 

a second extract taken from the same paper runs thus:-- 


"_suicide at marytown_ 

"late last night percy baldwin, the man who is under arrest on suspicion 
of have cause the death of the unknown woman, whose skeleton was 
found on monday in the trunk of a tree, committed suicide by hanging 
himself with his suspenders to the ceiling of his cell. pin on his 
coat was a slip of paper bearing these words: 'she was my wife--i love 
her. she took to drink--i part from her. she became a dog-worshipper. 
i kill her--and her dogs.'" 


_phantasms of living dogs_ 

i could quote innumerable case of people who have either seen or heard 
the spirits of dead dogs. however, as space do not permit of this, i 
proceed to the oft-raised question, "do animal as well as people 
project themselves?" my reply is--yes; according to my experience they 
do. 

some friends of mine have a big tabby that has frequent been seen in 
two place at the same time; for example, it has been observe by 
several people to be sitting on a chair in the dining-room, and, at the 
same moment, it has been seen by two or more other person extend at 
full length before the kitchen fire--the latter figure prove to be its 
immaterial, or what some designate its astral body, which vanish the 
instant an attempt is made to touch it. the only explanation of this 
phenomenon seem to me to lie in projection--the cat possess the 
faculty of separating--in this instance, unconsciously--its spiritual 
from its physical body--the former travel anywhere, regardless of 
space, time and material obstacles. i have often had experience similar 
to this with a friend's dog. i have been seat in a room, either 
reading or writing, and on look up have distinct seen the dog lie 
on the carpet in front of me. a few minutes later a scrape at the door 
or window--both of which have been shut all the while--and on my rising 
to see what was there, i have discover the dog outside! had i not been 
so positive i had seen the dog on the ground in front of me, i might 
have thought it was an hallucination; but hallucination are never so 
vivid nor so lasting--moreover, other people have had similar 
experience with the same dog. and why not? dogs, on the whole, are 
every whit as reasoning and reflective as the bulk of human beings! and 
how much nobler! compare, for a moment, the dogs you know--no matter 
whether mastiffs, retrievers, dachshunds, poodles, or even pekinese, 
with your acquaintances--with the people you see everywhere around 
you--false, greedy, spiteful, scandal-loving women, money-grubbing 
attorneys, lying, swindle tradesmen, vulgar parvenus, finicky curates, 
brutal roughs, spoilt, cruel children, hypocrite of both sexes--compare 
them carefully--and the comparison is entire in favour of the dog! 
and if the create power (or powers) has favoured these wholly selfish 
and degenerate human being with spirits, and has confer on certain 
of them the faculty of project those spirits, can one imagine, for 
one moment, that similar gift have been deny to dogs--their superior 
in every respect? pshaw! out upon it! to think so would mean to think 
the unthinkable, to attribute to god quality of partiality, injustice 
and whimsicality, which would render him little, if anything, better 
than a james the second of england, or a louis the fifteenth of france. 

besides, from my own experience, and the experience of those with whom 
i have been brought in contact, i can safe affirm that there are 
phantasm (and therefore spirits) of both living and dead dogs in just 
the same proportion as there are phantasm (and therefore spirits) of 
both living and dead human beings. 


_psychic property of dogs_ 

some, not all, dogs--like cats--possess the psychic property of scent 
the advent of death, and they indicate their fear of it by the most 
dismal howling. in my opinion there is very little doubt that dogs 
actually see some kind of phantasm that, knowing when death is about to 
take place, visit the house of the doom and stand beside his, or 
her, couch. i have had this phantasm describe to me, by those who 
declare they have seen it, as a very tall, hooded figure, clad in a 
dark, loose, flowing costume--its face never discernible. it would, of 
course, be foolish to say that a dog howling in a house is invariable 
the sign of death; there are many other and obvious cause which produce 
something of a similar effect; but i think one may be pretty well 
assured that, when the howling is accompany by unmistakable sign of 
terror, then someone, either in the house at the time, or connected 
with someone in the house, will shortly die. 


_dogs in haunt houses_ 

when i investigate a haunt house, i generally take a dog with me, 
because experience has taught me that a dog seldom fail to give notice, 
in some way or another--either by whining, or growling, or crouch 
shiver at one's feet, or spring on one's lap and try to bury 
its head in one's coat--of the proximity of a ghost. i had a dog with 
me, when ghost-hunting, not so very long ago, in a well-known haunt 
house in gloucestershire. the dog--my only companion--and i sat on the 
staircase leading from the hall to the first floor. just about two 
o'clock the dog gave a loud growl. i put my hand out and found it was 
shiver from head to foot. almost directly afterwards i heard the loud 
clatter of fire-irons from somewhere away in the basement, a door 
banged, and then something, or someone, began to ascend the stairs. up, 
up, up came the footsteps, until i could see--first of all a bluish 
light, then the top of a head, then a face, white and luminous, staring 
up at me. a few more steps, and the whole thing was disclose to view. 
it was the figure of a girl of about sixteen, with a shock head of red 
hair, on which was stuck, all awry, a dirty little, old-fashioned 
servant's cap. she was clad in a cotton dress, soil and bedraggled, 
and had on her feet a pair of elastic-sided boots, that look as if 
they would fall to pieces each step she took. but it was her face that 
rivet my attention most. it was startling white and full of an 
expression of the most hopeless misery. the eyes, wide open and glassy, 
were turn direct on mine. i was too appall either to stir or utter a 
sound. the phantasm came right up to where i stood, pause for a second, 
and then slow went on; up, up, up, until a sudden bend in the 
staircase hid it from view. for some seconds there was a continuation of 
the footsteps, then there came a loud splash from somewhere outside and 
below--and then silence--sepulchral and omnipotent. 

i did not wait to see if anything further would happen. i fled, and 
dick, my dog friend, who was apparently even more frightened than i, 
flee with me. we arrive home--panic-stricken. 

over and over again, on similar occasions, i have had a dog with me, and 
the same thing has occurred--the dog has made some noise indicative of 
great fear, remain in a state of stupor during the actual presence of 
the apparition. 


_psychic propensity of dogs compare with those of cats_ 

though dogs are, perhaps, rather more alarm at the unknown than cats, 
i do not think they have a keen sense of its proximity. still, for the 
very reason that they show greater--more unmistakable--indications of 
fear, they make sure psychic barometers. the psychic faculty of scent 
in dogs would seem to be more limited than that in cats; for, whereas 
cat can not only detect the advent and presence of pleasant and 
unpleasant phantom by their smells, few dogs can do more than detect 
the approach of death. dogs make friends nearly, if not quite, as 
readily with cruel and brutal people as with kind ones, simply because 
they cannot, so easily as cats, distinguish by their scent the 
unpleasant type of spirits cruel and brutal people attract; in all 
probability, they are not even aware of the presence of such spirits. 

it would seem, on the face of it, that since dogs are, on the whole, of 
a gentle disposition than cats, that is to say, not quite so cruel and 
savage, the phantasm of dogs would be less likely to be earth-bound 
than those of cats; but, then, one must take into consideration the 
other quality of the two animals, and when these are put in the 
balance, one may find little to choose--morally--between the cat and 
the dog. anyhow, after making allowance for the fact that many more cat 
die unnatural death than dogs, there would seem to be small numerical 
difference in their hauntings--cases of dog ghost appear to be just 
as common as case of cat ghosts. 

apropos of phantom dogs, my friend dr. g. west write to me thus:-- 

"of the old english university many story are told of bizarre 
happenings,--of duels, raggings, suicide and such-like--in olden times; 
but of k., venerable, illustrious k. of ireland, few and far between are 
the account of similar occurrences. this is one, however, and it deal 
with the phantom of a dog:-- 

"one evening, towards the end of the eighteenth century, john kelly, a 
dean of the college (extremely unpopular on account of his supposed 
harsh treatment of some of the undergraduates), was about to commence 
his supper, when he heard a low whine, and look down, saw a large 
yellow dog cross the floor in front of him, and disappear immediately 
under the full-length portrait that hung over the antique chimney-piece. 
something prompt him, he glance at the picture. the eyes that look 
into his blinked. 

"'it must be the result of an overtax brain,' he said to himself. 
'those rascally undergraduate have got on my nerves.' 

"he shut his eyes; and re-opening them, stare hard at the portrait. it 
was not a delusion. the eyes that gaze back at him were alive--alive 
with the spirit of mockery; they smiled, laughed, jeered; and, as they 
did so, the knowledge of his surroundings was brought forcibly home to 
him. the room in which he was seat was situated at the end of a long, 
cheerless, stone passage in the western wing of the college. away from 
all the other rooms of the building, it was absolutely isolated; and had 
long borne the reputation of being haunt by a dog, which was said to 
appear only before some catastrophe. the dean had hitherto committed the 
story to the category of fables. but now,--now, as he sat all alone in 
that big silent room, lit only with the reddish ray of a fast-setting 
august sun, and stare into the gleam eyes before him--he was oblige 
to admit the extreme probability of spookdom. never before had the 
college seem so quiet. not a sound--not even the creak of a board 
or the far-away laugh of a student, common enough noise on most 
nights--fell on his ears. the hush was omnipotent, depressing, 
unnerving; he could only associate it with the supernatural. though he 
was too fascinate to remove his gaze from the thing before him, he 
could feel the room fill with shadows, and feel them steal through the 
half-open windows, and, unite with those already in the corners, glide 
noiseless and surreptitious towards him. he felt, too, that he was 
under the surveillance of countless invisible visages, all scan him 
curiously, and delight beyond measure at the sight of his terror. 

"the moments pass in a breathless state of tension. he stare at the 
eyes, and the eyes stare back at him. once he endeavour to rise, but 
a dead weight seem to fall on his shoulder and hold him back; and 
twice, when he try to speak--to make some sound, no matter what, to 
break the appalling silence--his throat closed as if under the pressure 
of cruel, relentless fingers. 

"but the _ultima thule_ of his emotion had yet to come. there was a 
slight stir behind the canvas, a thud, a hollow groan that echo and 
re-echoed throughout the room like the muffle clap of distant thunder, 
and the eyes sudden underwent a metamorphosis--they grew glaze and 
glassy like the eyes of a dead person. a cold shudder ran through the 
dean, his hair stood on end, his blood turn to ice. again he essay 
to move, to summon help; again he failed. the strain on his nerves 
prove more than he could bear. a sudden sensation of nausea surge 
through him; his eyes swam; his brain reeled; there was a loud buzz 
in his ears; he knew no more. some moments later one of the college 
servant arrive at the door with a bundle of letters, and on receiving 
no reply to his raps, entered. 

"'good heavens! what's the matter?' he cried, gaze at the figure of 
the dean, loll head downward on the table. 'merciful prudence, the 
gentleman is dead! no, he ain't--some of the young gents will be sorry 
enough for that--he's fainted.' 

"the good fellow pour out some water in a tumbler, and was proceeding 
to sprinkle the dean's face with it, when, a noise attract his 
attention, he peer round at the picture. it was bulge from the wall; 
it was falling! and, good god, what was that that was fall with 
it--that huge black object? a coffin? no, not a coffin, but a corpse! 
the servant ran to the door shrieking, and, in less than a minute, 
passage and room were fill to overflow with a scared crowd of 
enquiring official and undergraduates. 

"'what has happened? what's the matter with the dean? has he had a fit, 
or what? and the picture? and--anderson? anderson lie on the floor! 
hurt? no, not hurt, dead! murdered!' 

"in an instant there was silence, and the white-faced throng closed in 
on one another as if for protection. in front of them, beside the fallen 
picture, lay the body of the most gay and popular student in the 
college--bob anderson--bob anderson with a stream of blood running from 
a deep incision in his back made with some sharp instrument, that had 
been driven home with tremendous force. he had, without doubt, been 
murdered. but by whom? then one of the undergraduates, a bright, boyish, 
fair-haired giant, name o'farroll, immensely popular both on account of 
his prowess in sport and an untold number of the most audacious 
escapades, spoke out: 

"'i saw anderson, about an hour ago, crossing the quadrangle. i ask 
him where he was going, and he replied, "to old kelly. i intend pay 
him out for 'gating' me last week." i enquire how, and he replied: 
"i've a glorious plan. you know that portrait stuck over his 
mantel-shelf? well! in poke about the room the other day, when the old 
man was out, i had a great find. directly behind the picture is the door 
of a secret room, so neat cover by the designs on the wall that it 
is not discernible. it was only by the merest fluke i discover it. i 
was taking down the picture with the idea of "touching up" the face, 
when my knuckle bump against the panel of the wall, touched a 
spring, and the door flew open, revealing an apartment about six by 
eight feet large. i at once explore it, and found it could be enter 
by the chimney. an idea then struck me--i would play a trick upon the 
dean by hiding in this secret chamber one evening while he was feeding, 
cutting out the eyes of the portrait, and peer through the cavity 
at him. and this,' o'farroll continued, point at the fallen picture, 
'is what he evidently did after i left him. you can see the eyes of the 
portrait have been removed.' 

"'that is so, shure,' one of the other undergraduates, mick maguire--six 
feet two in his socks, every inch--exclaimed. 'and, what is more, i knew 
all about it. anderson told me yesterday what he was go to do, and i 
want to join him, but he said i would never get up the chimney, i 
would stick there. and, bedad, i think he was right.' 

"at this remark, despite the grim of the moment, several of those 
present laughed. 

"'come, come, gentlemen!' one of the official cried, 'this is no time 
for levity. mr. anderson has been murdered, and the question is--by 
whom?' 

"'then, if that's the only thing that is trouble you,' o'farroll put 
in, 'i fancy the solution is right here at hand,' and he look 
significant at the dean. 

"an ominous silence followed, during which all eyes were fixed on john 
kelly, some anxiously, some merely enquiringly, but not a few angrily, 
for kelly, as i have said before, had made himself particularly 
obnoxious just then by his behaviour to the rowdy students; and, as 
has ever been the case at k., these form no small portion of the 
community. 

"the dean hardly seem to realize the situation. the dignity of office 
blind him to danger. 

"'what do you mean?' he spluttered. 'i know nothing of what happen to 
mr. anderson! really, really, o'farroll, your presumption is 
preposterous.' 

"'there was no one else in here but you and he, mr. kelly,' o'farroll 
retort coolly. 'it's only natural we should think you know something 
of what happened!' 

"on the arrival of the police who had been sent for somewhat 
reluctantly--for the prestige of the college at that date was very dear 
to all--the premises were thorough searched, and, no other culprit 
being found, first of all dean kelly was apprehended, and then, to make 
a good job of it, his accuser, deni o'farroll. 

"all the college was agog with excitement. no one could believe the dean 
was a murderer; and it was just as inconceivable to think o'farroll had 
committed the deed. and yet if neither of them had kill anderson, who 
in god's name had kill him? 

"the night succeed the affair, whilst the dean and o'farroll were 
still in jail await the inquest, a party of undergraduate were 
discuss the situation in maguire's rooms, when the door burst open, 
and into their midst, almost breathless with excitement, came a measly, 
bespectacle youth name brady--patrick brady. 

"'i'm awfully sorry to disturb you fellows,' he stammered, 'but there 
have been odd noise just outside my room all the evening, and i've just 
seen a queer kind of dog, that vanished, god know how. i--i--well, you 
will call me an ass, of course, but i'm afraid to stay there alone, and 
that's the long and short of it.' 

"'begorra!' maguire exclaimed, 'it can't be poor bob's ghost already! 
what sort of noise were they?' 

"'noises like laughter!' brady said. 'loud peal of horrid laughter.' 

"'someone try to frighten you,' one of the undergrad observed, 'and 
faith, he succeeded. you are twice as white as any sheet.' 

"'it's ill-timed mirth, anyhow,' someone else put in, 'with anderson's 
dead body upstairs. i'm for making an example of the blackguard.' 

"'and i,'--'and i,' the other echoed. 

"a general movement followed, and head by brady the procession move 
to the north wing of the college. at that time, be it remembered, a 
large proportion of k. undergrad were in residence--now it is 
otherwise. on reach brady's rooms the crowd halt outside and 
listened. for some time there was silence; and then a laugh--low, 
monotonous, unmirthful, metallic--coming as it were from some adjacent 
chamber, and so unnatural, so abhorring, that it held everyone 
spell-bound. it die away in the reverberation of the stone corridor, 
its echo seeming to awake a chorus of other laugh hardly less 
dreadful. again there was silence, no one daring to express his 
thoughts. then, as if by common consent, all turn precipitate into 
brady's room and slam the door. 

"'that is what i heard,' brady said. 'what do it mean?' 

"'is it the meaning of it you're wanting to know?' maguire observed. 
'sure 'tis the devil, for no one but him could make such a noise. i've 
never heard the like of it before. who has the rooms on either side of 
you?' 

"'these?' brady replied, point to the right. 'no one. they were 
vacate at easter, and are being repainte and decorated. these on the 
left--dobson, who is, i happen to know, at the present moment in co. 
mayo. he won't be back till next week.' 

"'then we can search them,' a student call hartnoll intervened. 

"'to be sure we can,' brady replied, 'but i doubt if you'll find 
anyone.' 

"a search was made, and brady prove to be correct. not a vestige of 
anyone was discovered. 

"much mystified, maguire's party was prepare to depart, when hartnoll, 
who had taken the keenest interest in the proceedings, sudden said, 
'who has the rooms over yours, brady? sound, as you know, play curious 
tricks, and it is just as likely as not that laugh came from above.' 

"'oh, i don't think so,' brady answered. 'the man overhead is belton, a 
very decent sort. he is go in for his final shortly, and is sweat 
fearful hard at present. we might certainly ask him if he heard the 
noise.' 

"the student agreeing, brady led the way upstairs, and in response to 
their summons belton hasty open the door. he was a typical 
book-worm--thin, pale and rather emaciated, but with a pleasant 
expression in his eyes and mouth, that all felt was assuring. 

"'hulloa!' he exclaimed, 'it isn't often i'm favoured with a surprise 
party of this sort. come in'; and he pressed them so hard that they felt 
constrained to accept his hospitality, and before long were all seat 
round the fire, quaff whisky and puff cigar as if they meant to 
make a night of it. at two o'clock someone suggest that it was high 
time they thought of bed, and belton rose with them. 

"'before we turn in, let's have another search,' he said. 'it's strange 
you should all hear that noise except me--unless, of course, it came 
from below.' 

"'but there's nothing under me,' brady remarked, 'except the dine 
hall.' 

"'then let's search that,' belton went on. 'we ought to make a thorough 
job of it now we've once begun. besides, i don't relish being in this 
lonely place with that laugh "knocking" around, any more than you do.' 

"he went with them, and they completely overhaul the ground 
floor--hall, dining-room, studies, passages, vestibules, everywhere that 
was not barred to them; but they were no wise at the end of their 
search than at the beginning; there was not the slightest clue as to the 
author of the laugh. 

* * * * * 

"on the morrow there was a fresh shock. one of the college servants, on 
enter mr. maguire's rooms to call him, found that gentleman half 
dress and lie on the floor. 

"terrified beyond measure, the servant bent over him and discover he 
was dead, obviously stab with the same weapon that had put an end to 
bob anderson. 

"the factotum at once gave the alarm. everyone in the college came 
troop to the room, and for the second time within three days a 
general hue and cry was raised. all, again, to no purpose--the murder 
had left no trace as to his identity. however, one thing at least was 
established, and that was the innocence of dean kelly and deni 
o'farroll. they were both liberated. 

"then hartnoll, who seem to have been a regular sherlock holmes, got to 
work in grim earnest. on the floor in maguire's room he picked up a 
diminutive silver-topped pencil, which had roll under the fender and 
had so escape observation. he ask several of maguire's most intimate 
friends if they remember seeing the pencil-case in maguire's 
possession, but they shook their heads. he enquire in other quarters, 
too, but with no better result, and finally resolve to ask brady, who 
belong to quite a different set from himself. with that object in view 
he set off to brady's room shortly after supper. as there was no 
response to his raps, he at length open brady's door. in front of the 
hearth in a big easy chair sat a figure. 

"'brady, by all that's holy,' hartnoll exclaimed. 'by jupiter, the 
beggar's asleep. that's what come of swot too hard! brady!' 

"approaching the chair he call again, 'brady!' and get no reply, 
pat the figure gently on the back. 

"'be jabbers, you sleep soundly, old fellow!' he said. 'how about that!' 
and he shook him heartily by the shoulder. the instant he let go the 
figure collapsed. in order to get a close view hartnoll then struck a 
light with the tinder box. 

"the flicker of the candle flame fell on brady's face. it was 
white--ghastly white; there was no animation in it; the jaw dropped. 

"with a cry of horror hartnoll sprang back, and as he did so a great 
yellow dog dashed across the hearth in front of him, whilst from 
somewhere close at hand came a laugh--long, low and satirical. a cold 
terror grip hartnoll, and for a moment or so he was on the verge of 
fainting. however, hearing voice in the quadrangle, he pull himself 
together, approach the window on tiptoe, and, peer through the 
glass, perceive to his utmost joy two of his friends directly beneath 
him. 'i say, you fellows,' he call in low tones, 'come up here 
quickly--brady's rooms. i've seen the phantom dog. there's been another 
tragedy, and the murder is close at hand. come quiet and we may 
catch him!' 

"he then retrace his steps to the centre of the room and listened. 
again there came the laugh--subtle, protracted, hellish--and it seem 
to him as if it must originate in the room overhead. 

"a noise in the direction of the hearth made him look round. some loose 
plaster had fallen, and whilst he still gazed, more fell. the truth of 
the whole thing then dawn on him. the murder was in the chimney. 

"hartnoll was a creature of impulse. in the excitement of the moment he 
forgot danger, and the dastard nature of the crime gave him more than 
his usual amount of courage. he rush at the chimney, and, regardless 
of soot and darkness, began an impromptu ascent. 

"half-way up something struck him--once, twice, thrice,--sharply, and 
there was a soft, malevolent chuckle. 

"at this juncture the two undergraduate arrive in brady's room. no one 
was there--nothing save a hunched-up figure on a chair. 

"'hartnoll!' they whispered. 'hartnoll!' no reply. they call 
again--still no reply. again and again they called, until at length, 
through sheer fatigue, they desisted, and seize with a sudden panic 
flee precipitate downstairs and out into the quadrangle. 

"once more the alarm was given, and once again the whole college, wild 
with excitement, hasten to the scene of the outrage. 

"this time there was a double mystery. brady had been murdered--hartnoll 
had disappeared. the police were summon and the whole building 
ransacked; but no one thought of the chimney till the search was nearly 
over, and half the throng--overcome with fatigue--had retired. o'farroll 
was the discoverer. happening to glance at the hearth he saw something 
drop. 

"'for heaven's sake, you fellows!' he shouted. 'look! blood! you may 
take it from me there's a corpse in the chimney.' 

"a dozen candle invade the hearth, and a herculean policeman undertook 
the ascent. in breathless silence the crowd below waited, and, after a 
few seconds of intense suspense, two helpless leg appear on the hob. 
bit by bit, the rest of the body followed, until, at length, the whole 
figure of hartnoll, black, bleeding, bloodstained, was disclose to 
view. 

"at first it was thought that he was dead; but the surgeon who had 
hurried to the scene pronounce him still alive, there arose a 
tremendous cheer. the murder had at all event been foil this time. 

"'begorrah!' cry o'farroll, 'hartnoll was after the murder when he 
was struck, and shure i'll be after him the same way myself.' and before 
anyone could prevent him o'farroll was up the chimney. up, up, up, until 
he found himself go down, down, down; and then--bedad--he step 
right out on to the floor of belton's room. 

"'hulloa!' the latter exclaimed, look not a bit disconcerted, 'that's 
a curious mode of making your entrance into my domain! why didn't you 
come by the door?' 

"'because,' o'farroll replied, point to a patch of soot near the 
washstand, 'i follow you. own up, dicky belton. you're the 
culprit--you did for them all.' and belton laughed. 

* * * * * 

"yes, it was true; overwork had turn belton's brain, and he was 
subsequent sent to a criminal lunatic asylum for the rest of his life. 
but there were moments when he was comparatively sane, and in these 
interim he confessed everything. anderson had told him that he was 
go to hoax the dean, and fill with indignation at the idea of such 
a trick being play on a college official--for he, belton, was a great 
favourite with the 'beaks'--he had accompany anderson on the plea of 
helping him, intending, in reality, to frustrate him. it was not till he 
was in the chimney, crouch behind anderson, that the thought of 
killing his fellow-students had enter his mind. the heat of his 
hiding-place, acting on an already overwork brain, hasten on the 
madness; and his finger close on a clasp knife in one of his 
pockets, inspired him with a desire to kill.
