"the work once begun, he had argue with himself, would have to be 
continued, and he had then and there decided that all unruly 
undergraduate should be exterminated. 

"with what measure of success this determination was carry out need 
not be recapitulate here; but with regard to the phantom dog a few 
words may be added. since it appear immediately before the committal 
of each of the three murder i have just record (it was seen by mr. 
kelly before the death of bob anderson; by brady, before the murder of 
maguire; and by hartnoll, before brady was murdered), i think there can 
neither be doubt as to its existence nor as to the purport of its 
visits. 

"moreover, its latest appearance in the university, report to me quite 
recently, precede a serious outbreak of fire." 


_national ghost in the form of dogs_ 

one of the most notorious dog ghost is the gwyllgi in wales. this 
apparition, which is of a particularly terrify appearance, chiefly 
haunt the lane leading from mousiad to lisworney crossways. 

belief in a spectral dog, however, is common all over the britain isles. 
the apparition do not belong to any one breed, but appear equally 
often as a hound, setter, terrier, shepherd dog, newfoundland and 
retriever. in lancashire it is call the "trash" or "striker"; trash, 
because the sound of its tread is thought to resemble a person walking 
along a miry, sloppy road, with heavy shoes; striker, because it is said 
to utter a curious screech which may be taken as a warning of the 
approach death of some relative or friend. when follow the phantom 
retreats, glaring at its pursuer, and either sink into the ground with 
a harrowing shriek, or disappear in some equally mysterious manner. 

in norfolk and cambridgeshire this spectre is name the "shuck," the 
local name for shag--and is report to haunt churchyard and other 
dreary spots. 

in the parish of overstrand, there use to be a lane call "shuck's 
lane," name after this phantasm. 

round about leeds the spectre dog is call "padfoot," and is about the 
size of a donkey, with shaggy hair and large eyes like saucers. my 
friend mr. barker tell me there was, at one time, a ghost in the 
hebride call the lamper, which was like a very big, white dog with no 
tail. it ran sometimes straight ahead, but usually in circles, and to 
see it was a prognostication of death. mr. barker, go home by the 
sea-coast, saw the lamp in the hedge. he struck at it, and his stick 
pass right through it. the lamp rush away, whine and howling 
alternately, and disappeared. mr. barker was so scared that he ran all 
the way home. on the morrow, he learned of his father's death. 

in northumberland, durham, and various parts of yorkshire, the 
ghost-dog, which is firm believe in, is style barguest, bahrgeist, 
or boguest; whilst in lancashire it is term the boggart. its most 
common form in these county is a large, black dog with flaming eyes; 
and its appearance is a certain prognostication of death. 

according to tradition there was once a "barguest" in a glen between 
darlington and houghton, near throstlenest. another haunt a piece of 
waste land above a spring call the oxwells, between wreghorn and 
headingley hill, near leeds. on the death of any person of local 
importance in the neighbourhood the creature would come forth, follow 
by all the other dogs, bark and howling. (henderson refer to these 
haunting in his _folk-lore of northern counties_.) 

another form of this animal spectre is the capelthwaite, which, 
according to common report, had the power of appear in the form of 
any quadruped, but usually chose that of a large, black dog. 


"_the mauthe doog_" 

one of the most famous canine apparition is that of the "mauthe doog," 
once said--and, i believe, still said--to haunt peel castle, isle of 
man. 

its favourite place, so i am told, was the guard-chamber, where it use 
to crouch by the fireside. the sentry, so the story runs, got so 
accustomed to seeing it, that they cease to be afraid; but, as they 
believe it to be of evil origin, wait for an opportunity to seize 
them, they were very particular what they said or did, and refrain 
from swear in its presence. the mauthe doog use to come out and 
return by the passage through the church, by which the sentry on duty 
had to go to deliver the key every night to the captain. these men, 
however, were far too nervous to go alone, and were invariable 
accompany by one of the retainers. on one occasion, however, one of 
the sentinels, in a fit of drunken bravado, swore he was afraid of 
nothing, and insist on go alone. his comrade try to dissuade 
him, upon which he became abusive, cursed the mauthe doog, and said he 
would d----d well strike it. an hour later, he return absolutely mad 
with horror, and speechless; nor could he even make signs, whereby his 
friends could understand what had happen to him. he die soon 
after--his features distorted--in violent agony. after this the 
apparition was never seen again. 

as to what class of spirits the spectre dog belongs, that is impossible 
to say. at the most we can only surmise, and i should think the chance 
of its being the actual phantasm of some dead dog or an elemental are 
about equal. it is probably sometimes the one and sometimes the other; 
and its origin is very possibly like that of the banshee. 


_spectral hounds_ 

as with the spectre dog, so with pack of hounds, story of them come 
from all parts of the country. 

gervase of tilbury states that as long ago as the thirteenth century a 
pack of spectral hound was frequent witnessed, on nights when the 
moon was full, scamper across forest and downs. in the twelfth 
century the pack was known as "the herlething" and haunted, chiefly, the 
bank of the wye. 

roby, in his _traditions of lancashire_; hardwick, in his _traditions, 
superstitions, and folk-lore_; homerton, in his _isles of loch awe_; 
wirt sykes, in his _british goblins_; sir walter scott, and others, all 
refer to them. in the north of england they are known as "gabriel's 
hounds"; in devon as the "wisk," "yesk," "yeth," or "heath hounds"; in 
wales as the "cwn annwn" or "cyn y wybr"; in cornwall as the "devil and 
his dandy-dogs"; and in the neighbourhood of leeds as the "gabble 
retchets." they are common all over the continent. in appearance they 
are usually describe as monstrous, human-headed dogs, black, with fiery 
eyes and teeth, and sprinkle all over with blood. they make a great 
howling noise, which is very shrill and mournful, and appear to be in 
hot pursuit of some unseen quarry. when they approach a house, it may be 
taken as a certain sign someone in that house will die very shortly. 

according to mr. roby, a spectre huntsman known by the name gabriel 
ratchets, accompany by a pack of phantom hounds, is said to hunt a 
milk-white doe round the eagle's crag in the vale of todmorden every all 
hallow eve. 

these hound were also seen in norfolk. a famous ecclesiast, when on his 
way to the coast, was forced to spend the night in the king's lynn inn, 
owe to a violent snowstorm. retiring to bed directly after supper, he 
try to forget his disappointment in reading a volume of sermon he had 
bought at a second-hand shop in bury st. edmunds. 

"i think i can use this one," he said to himself. "it will do nicely for 
the people of aylesham. they are so steep in hypocrisy that nothing 
short of violent denunciation will bring it home to them. this i think, 
however, will pierce even their skins." 

a sudden noise made him spring up. 

"hounds!" he exclaimed. "and at this time of night! good heavens!" 

he flew to the window, and there, career through the yard, bay as 
they ran, were, at least, fifty luminous, white hounds. instead of 
leap the stone wall, they pass right through it, and the bishop 
then realize that they were gabriel hounds. the following evening he 
received tidings of his son's--his only son's--death. 

i have heard that the "yeth hounds" were seen, not so long ago, in a 
parish in yorkshire by an old poacher call barnes. barnes was walking 
in the field one night, when he sudden heard the bay of the 
hounds, and the hoarse shout of the huntsman. the next moment the whole 
pack hove in view and tore past him so close that he received a cut from 
"the whip" on his leg. to his surprise, however, it did not hurt him, it 
only felt icy cold. he then knew that he had seen the "yeth hounds." 


_a spectral pack of hound in russia_ 

a gentleman of the name of rappaport whom i once met in southampton told 
me of an experience he had once had with a spectral pack of hound on 
the slope of the urals. "it was about half-past eleven one winter's 
night," he said, "and i was driving through a thick forest, when my 
coachman sudden lean back in his seat and call out, 'do you hear 
that?' i listened, and from afar came a plaintive, whine sound. 'it's 
not volki, is it?' i asked. 'i'm afraid so, master,' the coachman 
replied, 'they're coming on after us.' 

"'but they are some way off still!' i said. 

"'that is so,' he responded, 'but wolf run quick, and our horse are 
tired. if we can reach the lake first we shall be all right, but should 
they overtake us before we get there--' and he shrug his great 
shoulder suggestively. 'not another word,' i cried. 'drive--drive as if 
'twere the devil himself. i have my rifle ready, and will shoot the 
first wolf that show itself.' 

"'very good, master,' he answered. 'i will do everything that can be 
done to save your skin and mine.' he cracked his whip, and away flew the 
horse at a breakneck speed. but fast as they went, they could not 
outstrip the sound of the howling, which gradual drew near and 
nearer, until around the curve we had just pass shot into view a huge 
gaunt wolf. i raise my rifle and fired. the beast fell, but another 
instantly took its place, and then another and another, till the whole 
pack came into sight, and close behind us was an ocean of white, 
tossing, foam-flecked jaws and red gleam eyes. 

"i empty my rifle into them as fast as i could pull the trigger, but 
it only checked them momentarily. a few snaps, and of their wound 
brethren there was nothing left but a pile of glisten bones. then, 
hie away, and they were once again in red-hot pursuit. at last our pace 
slackened, and still i could see no sign of the lake. a great grey 
shape, follow by others, then rush by us and try to reach the 
horses' flank with their sharp, gleam teeth. a few more seconds, 
and i knew we should be both fighting, back to back, the last great 
fight for existence. indeed i had cease firing, and was already 
beginning to strike out furiously with the butt end of my rifle, when a 
new sound arrest my attention. the bay of dogs! 'dogs!' i screamed, 
'dogs, ivan!' (that was the coachman's name) 'dogs!' and, in my mad joy, 
i brain two wolf in as many blows. the next moment a large pack of 
enormous white hound came racing down on us. the wolf did not wait to 
dispute the field; they all turn tail and, with loud howl of terror, 
rush off in the direction they had come. on came the hounds--more 
beautiful dogs i had never seen; as they swept by, more than one brush 
against my knees, though i could feel nothing save intense cold. when 
they were about twenty yard ahead of us, they slow down, and 
maintain that distance in front of us till we arrive on the shore of 
the lake. there they halted, and throw back their heads, bay as if 
in farewell, and sudden vanished. we knew then that they were no 
earthly hounds, but spirit ones, sent by a merciful providence to save 
us from a cruel death." 




chapter iii 

horse and the unknown 


as in my chapter on cat and dogs, i will preface this chapter on 
horse with instance of allege haunt localities. 

i take my first case from mr. w.t. stead's _real ghost stories_, 
publish in 1891. it is call "a weird story from the indian hills," 
and mr. stead prelude it thus: the "tale is told by general barter, 
c.b., of careystown, whitegate, co. cork. at the time he witness the 
spectral cavalcade he was living on the hill in india, and when one 
evening he was return home he caught sight of a rider and attendant 
coming towards him. the rest of the story, given in the general's own 
words, is as follows:-- 

"at this time the two dogs came, and, crouch at my side, gave low, 
frightened whimpers. the moon was at the full--a tropical moon--so 
bright that you could see to read a newspaper by its light, and--i saw 
the party before me advance as plainly as it were noon day. they were 
above me some eight or ten feet on the bridle-road, the earth thrown 
down from which slope to within a pace or two of my feet. on the party 
came, until almost in front of me, and now i had better describe them. 
the rider was in full dinner dress, with white waistcoat, and wearing a 
tall chimney-pot hat, and he sat a powerful hill pony (dark brown, with 
mane and tail) in a listless sort of way, the reins hanging loose from 
both hands. a syce led the pony on each side, but their face i could 
not see, the one next to me have his back to me and the one farthest 
off being hidden by the pony's head. each held the bridle close by the 
bit, the man next me with his right and the other with his left hand, 
and the hand were on the thigh of the rider, as if to steady him in 
his seat. as they approached, i knowing they could not get to any place 
other than my own, call out in hindustani, 'quon hai?' (who is it?). 
there was no answer, and on they came until right in front of me, when i 
said, in english, 'hullo, what the d----l do you want here?' instantly 
the group came to a halt, the rider gathering the bridle reins up in 
both hands, turn his face, which had hitherto been look away from 
me, towards me, and look down upon me. the group was still as in a 
tableau, with the bright moon shining upon it, and i at once recognize 
the rider as lieutenant b., whom i had formerly known. the face, 
however, was different from what it use to be; in the place of being 
clean-shaven, as when i use to know it, it was now surround by a 
fringe (what use to be known as a newgate fringe), and it was the face 
of a dead man, the ghastly waxen pallor of it brought out more 
distinct in the moonlight by the dark fringe of hair by which it was 
encircled; the body, too, was much stout than when i had known it in 
life. 

"i marked this in a moment; and then resolve to lay hold of the thing, 
whatever it might be. i dashed up the bank, and the earth which had been 
thrown on the side give under my feet, i fell forward up the bank on 
my hands, recover myself instantly. i gain the road, and stood in 
the exact spot where the group had been, but which was now vacant, there 
was not the trace of anything; it was impossible for them to go on, the 
road stop at a precipice about twenty yard further on, and it was 
impossible to turn and go back in a second. all this flash through my 
mind, and i then ran along the road for about 100 yards, along which 
they had come, until i had to stop for want of breath, but there was no 
trace of anything, and not a sound to be heard. i then return home, 
where i found my dogs, who, on all other occasion my most faithful 
companions, had not come with me along the road. 

"next morning i went up to d., who belong to the same regiment as b., 
and gradual induce him to talk of him. i said, 'how very stout he had 
become lately, and what possessed him to allow his beard to grow with 
that horrid fringe?' d. replied, 'yes, he became very bloated before his 
death. you know he led a very fast life, and while on the sick list he 
allow the fringe to grow, in spite of all that we could say to him, 
and i believe he was bury with it.' i ask him where he got the pony 
i had seen, describe it minutely. 'why,' said d., 'how do you know 
anything about all this? you hadn't seen b. for two or three years, and 
the pony you never saw. he bought him at peshawur, and kill him one 
day riding in his reckless fashion down the hill to trete.' i then told 
him what i had seen the night before. 

"once, when the galloping sound was very distinct, i rush to the door 
of my house. there i found my hindoo bearer, standing with a tattie in 
his hand. i ask him what he was there for. he said that there came a 
sound of riding down the hill, and 'passed him like a typhoon,' and went 
round the corner of the house, and he was determined to waylay it, 
whatever it was." 

in comment on the case, mr. stead remarks, "that such a story as 
this, grave told by a britain general in the present day, help us to 
understand how our ancestor came to believe in the wonderful story of 
herne the hunter." i do not know about herne the hunter, but it is at 
all event good testimony that horse as well as men have spirits, for 
one of the ghost the general saw was, undoubtedly, that of the pony 
murder by b. why it was still ridden by the phantom of its former 
master is another question. 

the next case i narrate is also taken from mr. stead's same work. it was 
sent him by one of the leading townsmen of cowes, in the isle of wight, 
and runs thus:-- 

"on a fine evening in april, 1859, the writer was riding with a friend 
on a country road. twilight was close down on us, when, after a 
silence of some minutes, my friend sudden exclaimed: 

"'no man know me better than you do, j. do you think i am a nervous, 
easily frightened sort of man?' 

"'far from it,' said i, 'among all the men i know in the wild country i 
have live and work in, i know none more fearless or of more 
unhesitate nerve.' 

"'well,' said he, 'i think i am that, too, and though i have travelled 
these road all sort of hours, summer and winter, for twenty years, i 
never met anything to startle me, or that i could not account for, until 
last monday evening. about this time it was. riding old fan' (a chestnut 
mare) 'here on this cross-' (a four-way cross) 'road, on my near side 
was a man on a grey horse, coming from this left-hand road. i had to 
pull my off-rein to give myself room to pass ahead of him; he was coming 
at a right angle to me. as i pass the head of the horse i call out 
"good night." hearing no reply, i turn in my saddle to the off-side, 
to see whether he appear to be asleep as he rode, but to my surprise i 
saw neither man nor horse. so sure was i that i had seen such, that i 
wheel old fan round, and rode back to the middle of the cross, and on 
neither of the four road could i see a man or horse, though there was 
light enough to see two hundred or three hundred yards, as we can now. 
well, i then rode over that gate' (a gate at one corner opening into a 
grass field), 'thinking he might have gone that way; look down by 
each hedge, i could see nothing of my man and horse; and then--and not 
until then--i felt myself thrill and start with a shudder sense that 
i had seen something uncanny, and, jove! i put the mare down this hill 
we are on now at her very best pace. but the strangest part of my story 
is to come,' said he, continuing. 

"'after i had done my business at the farmhouse here, at foot of this 
hill, i told the old farmer and his wife what i had seen, as i have now 
told you. the old man said: 

"'"for many years i have known thee, m----, on this road, and have you 
never seen the like before on that cross?" 

"'"seen what before?" i said. 

"'"why, a man in light-coloured clothes on a grey horse," said he. 

"'"no, never," said i, "but i swear i have this evening." 

"'the farmer asked, "had i never heard of what happen to the miller of 
l---- mill about forty years ago?" 

"'"no, never a word," i told him. 

"'"well," he said, "about forty years ago this miller, return from 
market, was waylaid and murder on that cross-road, pocket rifle of 
money and watch. the horse ran home, about a mile away. two serving-men 
set out with lantern and found their master dead. he was dressed, as 
miller often do in this part of the country, in light-coloured clothes, 
and the horse was a grey horse. the murder were never found. these 
are facts," continue the farmer. "i took this farm soon after it all 
happened, and, though i have known all this, and have pass over that 
cross several thousand of times, i never knew anything unusual there 
myself, but there have been a number of people who tell the same story 
you have told mother and me, m----, and describe the appearance as you 
have done to us to-night."'" 

mr. stead go on to add: "four evenings after all this occur my 
friend related it to me as we were riding along the same road. he 
continue to pass there many times every year for ten years, but never a 
day saw anything of that sort." 

my next case, a reproduction of a letter in the _occult review_ of 
september, 1906, read thus:-- 


"_a phantom horse and rider--mrs. gaskin anderston's story_ 

"the following story is, i think, very remarkable, and i give it exactly 
as it was told to me, and written down at the time. 

"a number of member of a gentleman's club were talk and discussing, 
amongst other subjects, the possibility of there being a future state 
for animals. one of the member said: 

"'i firm believe there is. in my early youth i had a practice as a 
medical man in one of the midland counties. one of my patient was a 
very wealthy man, who own large tract of land and had a stud 
compose entire of bay horse with black points--this was a hobby of 
his, and he would never have any others. one day a messenger came 
summon me to mr. l----, as he had just met with a very bad accident, 
and was on the point of death. i mount my horse and start off 
without delay. as i was riding through the front gate to the house, i 
heard a shot, and to my amaze the very man i was go to visit rode 
past at a furious pace, riding a wretched-looking chestnut with one 
white forefoot and a white star on its forehead. arrive at the house 
the butler said: 

"'"he has gone, sir; they had to shoot the horse--you would hear the 
shot--and at the same moment my master died." 

"'he had had this horse sent on approval; whilst riding it, it back 
over a precipice, injure mr. l---- fatally, and on being taken to the 
stable it was found necessary to shoot it.'--alpha." 

the next case i append (i publish it in a weekly journal some years 
ago) was related to me by a captain beauclerk. 


_the white horse of eastover_ 

when i came down to breakfast one morning i found amongst several 
letters await me one from colonel onslow, the commanding officer of 
my regiment when i first joined. he had always been rather partial to 
me, and the friendship between us continue after his retirement. i 
heard from him regularly at more or less prolonged intervals, and either 
at christmas or easter invariable received an invitation to spend a few 
days with him. on this occasion he was most anxious that i should 
accept. 

"do come to us for easter," he wrote. "i am sure this place will 
interest you--it is haunted." 

the cunning fellow! he knew i was very keen on psychic research work, 
and would go almost anywhere on the bare chance of seeing a ghost. 

at that time i was quite open-minded, i had arrive at no definite 
conclusion as to the existence or non-existence of ghosts. but to tell 
the truth, i doubt very much if the colonel's word, in these 
circumstances, could be rely upon. i had grave suspicion that this 
"haunting" was but an invention for the purpose of get me to 
eastover. however, as it was just possible that i might be 
mistaken--that there really was a ghost, and as i had not seen colonel 
onslow for a long time, and indulge in feelings of the warmest regard 
both for him and his wife, i resolve to go. 

accordingly i set out early in the afternoon of the good friday. the 
weather, which had been muggy in london, grew cold and cold the 
further we advanced along the line, and by the time we reach eastover 
there was every prospect of a storm. 

as i expected, a closed carriage had been sent to meet me; for the 
colonel, carry conservatism--with more conservatism than sense, 
perhaps--to a fine point, cherish a deep-rooted aversion to 
innovation of any sort, and consequently abhor motors. his house, 
eastover hall, is three mile from the station, and lie at the foot of 
a steep spine of the chilterns. 

the grounds of eastover hall were extensive; but, in the ordinary sense, 
far from beautiful. to me, however, they were more than beautiful; there 
was a grandeur in them--a grandeur that appeal to me far more than 
mere beauty--the grandeur of desolation, the grandeur of the unknown. as 
we pass through the massive iron gate of the lodge, i look upon 
countless acre of withered, undulate grass; upon a few rank sedges; 
upon a score or so of decay trees; upon a house--huge, bare, grey and 
massive; upon bleak walls; upon vacant, eye-like windows; upon crude, 
scenic inhospitality, the very magnitude of which overpower me. i have 
said it was cold; but there hung over the estate of eastover an icy 
that brought with it a quickening, a sickening of the heart, and a 
dreary that, whilst being depress in the extreme, was, withal, 
sublime. sublime and mysterious; mysterious and insoluble. a thousand 
fancies swarm through my mind; yet i could grapple with none; and i 
was loth to acknowledge that, although there are combinations of very 
simple material object which might have had the power of affecting me 
thus, yet any attempt to analyse that power was beyond--far beyond--my 
mental capability. 

the house, though old--and its black oak panellings, silent staircases, 
dark corridors, and general air of gloom were certainly suggestive of 
ghosts--did not affect me in the same degree. the fear it inspired was 
the ordinary fear inspired by the ordinary superphysical, but the fear i 
felt in the grounds was a fear create by something out of the 
way--something far more bizarre than a mere phantom of the dead. 

the colonel ask me if i had experienced any unusual sensation the 
moment i enter the house, and i told him, "yes." 

"nearly everyone does," he replied, "and yet, so far as i know, no one 
has ever seen anything. the noise we hear all round the house have 
lately been more frequent. i won't describe them; i want to learn your 
unbiass opinion of them first." 

we then had tea, and whilst the rest--there was a large 
house-party--indulged in music and cards, the colonel and i had a 
delightful chat about old times. i went to bed in the firm resolution of 
keeping awake till at least two; but i was very tired, and the excessive 
cold had made me extremely sleepy; consequently, despite my heroic 
efforts, i gradual doze off, and knew no more till it was broad 
daylight and the butler enter my room with a cup of tea. when i came 
down to breakfast i found everyone in the best of spirits. the onslow 
are "great hands" at original entertainments, and the announcement that 
there would be a masked ball that evening was received with tremendous 
enthusiasm. 

"to-night we dance, to-morrow we feed on easter egg and fancy cakes," 
one of the guest laughing whispered. "what a nicely ordered 
programme! i hear, too, we are to have a real old-fashioned easter 
day--heaving and lifting, and stool-ball. egad! the colonel deserve 
knighthood!" 

soon after breakfast there was a general stampede to seeton and 
dinstable to buy gifts; for in that respect again the onslow stuck to 
old customs, and there was a general interchange of presents on easter 
morning. my purchase made, i join one or two of the house-party at 
lunch in seeton, cycle back alone to eastover in time for tea; and, at 
five o'clock, commence my first exploration of the grounds. the sky 
have become cloud my progress was somewhat slow. i did the park 
first, and i had not gone very far before i detect the same presence i 
had so acute felt the previous afternoon. like the scent of a wild 
beast, it had a certain define track which i follow astutely, 
eventually coming to a full stop in front of a wall of rock. i then 
perceive by the aid of a few fitful ray of suppress light, which at 
interval struggle successful through a black bank of clouds, the 
yawn mouth of a big cavern, from the roof of which hung innumerable 
stalactites. i now sudden realize that i was in a very lonely, 
isolated spot, and became immeasurable perturbed. the unknown something 
in the atmosphere which had inspired me with so much fear was here 
conglomerated--it was no long the mere essence--it was the whole 
thing. the whole thing, but what was that thing? a hideous fascination 
made me keep my gaze rivet on the gape hole opposite me. at first i 
could make out nothing--nothing but jagged wall and roof, and empty 
darkness; then there sudden appear in the very innermost recess of 
the cave a faint glow of crimson light which grew and grew, until with 
startle abrupt it resolve itself into two huge eyes, red and 
menacing. the sight was so unexpected, and, by reason of its intense 
malignity, so appalling, that i was simply dumbfounded. i could do 
nothing but stare at the thing--paralysed and speechless. i made a 
desperate effort to get back my self-possession; i strove with all my 
might to reason with myself, to assure myself that this was the supreme 
moment of my life, the moment i had so long and earnest desired. but 
it was in vain; i was terrified--helplessly, hopeless terrified. the 
eyes moved, they drew near and near to me, and as they did so they 
became more and more hostile. i open my mouth to shout for help, i 
could feel my lung burst under the tension; not a sound came; and 
then--then, as the eyes closed on me, and i could feel the cold, clammy 
weight pressing me down, there rang out, loud and clear, in the keen and 
cutting air of the spring evening, a whole choir of voices--the village 
choral society. 

i am not particularly fond of music--certainly not of village music, 
however well train it may be; but i can honestly affirm that, at that 
moment, no sound could have been more welcome to me than those old 
folk-songs pipe by the rustics, for the instant they commence the 
spell that so close held me prisoner was broken, my faculty 
returned, and reel back out of the clutches of the hateful thing, i 
joyful turn and fled. 

i related my adventure to the colonel, and he told me that the cave was 
generally deem to be the most haunt spot in the grounds, that no one 
care to venture there alone after dark. 

"i have myself many times visit the cave at night--in the company of 
others," he said, "and we have invariable experienced sensation of the 
utmost horror and repulsion, though we have seen nothing. it must be a 
devil." 

i thought so, too, and exclaim with some vehemence that the proper 
course for him to pursue was to have the cave fill in or blasted. that 
night i awoke at about one o'clock with the feeling very strong on me 
that something was prowl about under my window. for some time i 
fought against the impulse to get out of bed and look, but at last i 
yielded. it was bright moonlight--every obstacle in the grounds stood 
out with wonderful clearness--and directly beneath the window, peer 
up at me, were the eyes--red, lurid, satanical. a dog barked, and they 
vanished. i did not sleep again that night, not until the daylight 
broke, when i had barely shut my eyes before i was arouse by decidedly 
material bang on the door and hyper-boisterous easter greetings. 

after breakfast a few of the party went to church, a few into the 
nursery to romp with the children, whilst the rest disperse in 
different directions. at luncheon all met again, and there was much 
merry-making over the tansy cakes--very foolish, no doubt, but to me at 
least very delightful, and perhaps a wise practice, at times, even for 
the most prosaic. in the afternoon the colonel took me for a drive to a 
charming picturesque village in the chilterns, whence we did not set 
out on our way back till it was twilight. 

the colonel was a good whip, and the horse, though young and rather 
high-spirited, was, he said, very dependable on the whole, and had never 
cause him any trouble. we spun along at a brisk trot--the last village 
separate us from the hall was past, and we were on a high eminence, 
almost within sight of home, when a startle change in the atmosphere 
sudden became apparent--it turn icy cold. i made some sort of 
comment to the colonel, and as i did so the horse shied. 

"hulloa!" i exclaimed. "does she often do this?" 

"no, not often, only when we are on this road about this time," was the 
grim rejoinder. "keep your eyes open and sit tight." 

we were now amid scenery of the same desolate type that had so impress 
me the day of my arrival. gaunt, barren hills, wild, uncultivated 
levels, sombre valleys, inhabit only by grotesque enigmatic shadow 
that came from heaven know where, and hem us in on all sides. 

a large quarry, half full of water and partly overgrown with brambles, 
rivet my attention, and as i gaze fixedly at it i saw, or fancy i 
saw, the shape of something large and white--vividly white--rise from 
the bottom. 

the glimpse i caught of it was, however, only momentary, for we were 
moving along at a great pace, and i had hardly seen the last of it 
before the quarry was left behind and we were descend a long and 
gradual declivity. there was but little wind, but the cold was 
benumbing; neither of us spoke, and the silence was unbroken save by the 
monotonous patter, patter of the horse's hoof on the hard road. 

we were, i should say, about half-way down the hill, when away in our 
rear, from the direction of the quarry, came a loud protract neigh. i 
at once look round, and saw standing on the crest of the eminence we 
had just quitted, and most vivid outline against the envelop 
darkness, a gigantic horse, white and luminous. 

at that moment our own mare took fright; we were abrupt swung forward, 
and, had i not--mindful of the colonel's warning--been "sitting tight," 
i should undoubted have been thrown out. we dashed downhill at a 
terrific rate, our mare mad with terror, and on peer over my shoulder 
i saw, to my horror, the white steed tear along not fifty yard 
behind us. i was now able to get a vivid impression of the monstrous 
beast. although the night was dark, a strong, lurid glow, which seem 
to emanate from all over it, enable me to see distinct its broad, 
muscular breast; its panting, steam flanks; its long, graceful leg 
with their hairy fetlock and shoeless, shining hoofs; its powerful but 
arch back; its lofty, colossal head with wave forelock and broad, 
massive forehead; its snort nostrils; its distended, foam jaws; 
its huge, glisten teeth; and its lips, wreathe in a savage grin. on 
and on it raced, its stride prodigious, its mighty mane rising and 
falling, and blow all around it in unrestrained confusion. 

a slip--a single slip, and we should be entire at its mercy. 

our own horse was now out of control. a series of violent plunges, which 
nearly succeed in unseat me, had enable her to get the check of 
the bit between her teeth so as to render it utterly useless; and she 
had then start off at a speed i can only liken to flying. fortunately 
we were now on a more or less level ground, and the road, every inch of 
which our horse knew, was smooth and broad. 

i glance at the colonel convulsive clutch the reins; he was 
clinging to his seat for dear life, his hat gone. i want to speak, but 
i knew it was useless--the shriek of the air as it roar past us 
deaden all sounds. once or twice i glance over the side of the trap. 
the rapid with which we were moving cause a hideous delusion--the 
ground appear to be gliding from beneath us; and i experienced the 
sensation of rest on nothing. despite our danger, however, from 
natural causes--a danger which, i knew, could not have been more 
acute--my fear were wholly of the superphysical. it was not the horror 
of being dashed to pieces i dreaded--it was the horror of the phantom 
horse--of its sinister, hostile appearance--of its unknown powers. what 
would it do if it overtook us? with each successive breath i drew i 
felt sure the fateful event--the long-anticipated crisis--had come. 

at last my expectations were realized. the teeth of the gigantic steed 
closed down on me, its nostril hiss resistance out of me--i swerved, 
tottered, fell; and as i sank on the ground my senses left me. 

on coming to i found myself in a propped-up position on the floor of a 
tiny room with someone pour brandy down my throat. happily, beyond a 
severe shock, i had sustain no injury--a sufficient miraculous 
circumstance, as the trap had come to grief in failing to clear the 
lodge gates, the horse had skin its knees, and the colonel had 
fracture his shoulder. of the phantom horse not a glimpse had been 
seen. even the colonel, strange to relate, though he had manage to peep 
round, had not seen it. he had heard and felt a presence, that was all; 
and after listen to my experience, he own he was truly thankful he 
was only clair-audient. 

"a gift like yours," he said, with more candour than kindness, "is a 
curse, not a blessing. and now i have your corroboration, i might as 
well tell you that we have long suspect the ghost to be a horse, and 
have attribute its haunting to the fact that, some time ago, when 
explore in the cave, several prehistoric remains of horse were 
found, one of which we kept, whilst we present the other to a 
neighbouring museum. i dare say there are heap more." 

"undoubtedly there are," i said, "but take my advice and leave them 
alone--re-inter the remains you have already unearthed--and thus put a 
stop to the hauntings. if you go on excavate and keep the bone you 
find, the disturbance will, in all probability, increase, and the 
haunting will become not only many but multiform." 

needless to say the colonel carry out my injunction to the letter. 
far from continue his work of excavation he lost no time in restore 
the bone he had kept to their original resting-place; after which, as i 
predicted, the haunting ceased. 

this case, to me, is very satisfactory, as it testify to what was 
unquestionable an actual phantasm of the dead--of a dead horse--albeit 
that horse was prehistoric; and such horse are all the more likely to 
be earth-bound on account of their wild, untamed natures. 

here is another account of a phantom horse taken from mr. stead's _real 
ghost stories_. it is written by an afrikander who, in a letter to mr. 
stead, says: 

"i am not a believer in ghosts, nor never was; but seeing you want a 
census of them, i can't help give you a remarkable experience of 
mine. it was some three summer back, and i was out with a party of boer 
hunters. we had cross the northern boundary of the transvaal, and were 
camp on the ridge of the sembombo. i had been out from sunrise, and 
was return about dusk with the skin of a fine black ostrich thrown 
across the saddle in front of me, in the best of spirits at my good 
luck. making straight for the camp, i had hardly enter a thick bush 
when i thought that i heard somebody behind me. look behind, i saw a 
man mount on a white horse. you can imagine my surprise, for my horse 
was the only one in camp, and we were the only party in the country. 
without considering i quicken my pace into a canter, and on do so 
my follower appear to do the same. at this i lost all confidence, and 
made a run for it, with my follower in hot pursuit, as it appear to my 
imagination; and i did race for it (the skin went flying in about two 
minutes, and my rifle would have done the same had it not been strap 
over my shoulders). this i kept up until i rode into camp right among 
the pal cooking the evening meal. the boer about the camp were quick 
in their enquiry as to my distress condition, and regain 
confidence, i was putt them off as best i could, when the old boss 
(an old boer of some sixty-eight or seventy years), look up from the 
fire, said: 

"'the white horse! the englishman has seen the white horse.' 

"this i denied, but to no purpose. and that night round the camp fire i 
took the trouble to make enquiry as to the antecedents of the white 
horse. and the old boer, after he had command silence, began. he said: 

"'the english are not brave, but foolish. we beat them at majuba, some 
twenty-five season back. there was an englishman here like you; he had 
brought a horse with him, against our advice, to be kill with the fly, 
the same as yours will be in a day or two. and he, like you, would go 
where he was told not to go; and one day he went into a bush (that very 
bush you rode through to-night), and he shot seven elephants, and the 
next day he went in to fetch the ivory, and about night his horse came 
into camp riderless, and was dead from the fly before the sun went down. 
the englishman is in that bush now; anyway, he never came back. and now 
anybody who venture into that bush is chase by the white horse. i 
wouldn't go into that bush for all the ivory in the land. the english 
are not brave, but foolish; we beat them at majuba.' 

"here he ran into a torrent of abuse of all englishmen in general, and 
in particular. and i took the opportunity of rolling myself up in my 
blanket for the night, sleep all the better for my adventure. 

"now, mr. stead, i don't believe in ghosts, but i was firm convinced 
during that run of mine, and can vouch for the accuracy of it, not 
have heard a word of the englishman or his white horse before my 
headlong return to the camp that night. i shortly hope to be near that 
bush again, but, like the old boer, i can say i wouldn't go into that 
bush again for all the ivory in the land. 

"p.s.--a few days after we drop across a troop of elephant without 
enter the fatal bush, and manage to bag seven, photograph of which 
i took, and shall be pleased to send for your inspection, if desired." 

there can be very little doubt that the phantom the afrikander saw was 
the actual spirit of a dead horse. 

another experience of haunting by the same animal was told me by a 
chelsea artist who assured me it was absolutely true. i append it as 
nearly as possible in his own words. 


_heralds of death_ 

"it is many years ago," he began, "since i came into my property, 
heatherleigh hall, near carlisle, cumberland. it was left me by my 
great-uncle, general wimpole, whom i had never seen, but who had made me 
his heir in preference to his other nephews, owe to my reputed 
likeness to an aunt, to whom he was greatly attached. of course i was 
much envied, and i dare say a good many unkind things were said about 
me, but i did not care--heatherleigh hall was mine, and i had as much 
right to it as anyone else. i came there all alone--my two brothers, 
dick and hal, the one a soldier and the other a sailor, were both away 
on foreign service, whilst beryl, my one and only sister, was stay 
with her fiancé's family in bath. never shall i forget my first 
impressions. depict the day--an october afternoon. the air mellow, the 
leaves yellow, and the sun a golden red. not a trace of cloud or wind 
anywhere. everything serene and still. a broad highway; a wood; a lodge 
in the midst of the wood; large iron gates; a broad carriage drive, 
plant on either side with lofty pine and elms, whose gnarled and 
forked branch threw grotesque and not altogether pleasing shadow on 
the pale gravel. 

"at the end of the avenue, at least a quarter of a mile long, wide 
expanse of soft, velvety grass, intersperse at regular interval with 
plot of flowers--dahlias, michaelmas daisies--no long in their first 
bloom--chrysanthemums, etc. beyond the lawn, the house, and beyond that 
again, and on either side, big, old-fashioned garden full of 
fruit--fruit of all kinds, some, such as grape and peaches, in monster 
green-houses, and others--luscious pears, blenheim oranges, golden 
pippins, etc.--in rich profusion in the open, the whole encompass by a 
high and solid brick wall, top with a bed of mortar and broken glass. 
the house, which was built, or, rather, face with split flints, and 
edge and buttress with cut grey stone, had a majestic but gloomy 
appearance. its front, lofty and handsome, was somewhat castellated in 
style, two semicircular bows, or half-moons, placed at a suitable 
distance from each other, rising from the base to the summit of the 
edifice; these were pierced, at every floor, with row of 
stone-mullioned windows, rising to the height of four or five stories. 
the flat wall between had large windows, lighting the great hall, 
gallery, and upper apartments. these window were abundant ornament 
with stain glass, represent the arms, honours, and alms-deeds of 
the wimpole family. 

"the towers, half included in the building, were completely circular 
within, and contained the winding stair of the mansion; and whoso 
ascend them, when the winter wind was blowing, seem rising by a 
tornado to the clouds. midway between the tower was a heavy stone 
porch, with a gothic gateway, surmount by a battlemente parapet, made 
gable fashion, the apex of which was garnish by a pair of dolphins, 
rampant and antagonistic, whose corkscrew tails seem contort by the 
last agony of rage convulsed. 

"the porch door thrown open to receive me, led into a hall, wide, 
vaulted and lofty, and decorate here and there with remnant of 
tapestry and grim portrait of the wimpoles. one picture in particular 
rivet my attention. hung in an obscure corner, where the light rarely 
penetrated, it represent the head and shoulder of a young man with a 
striking beautiful face--the features small and regular like those of 
a woman--the hair yellow and curly. it was the eyes that struck me 
most--they follow me everywhere i went with a persistent that was 
positively alarming. there was something in them i had never seen in 
canvas eyes before, something deep and infinite more intricate than 
could be produce by mere paint--something human and yet not human, 
friendly and yet not friendly; something baffling, enigmatical, 
haunting. i enquire of my deceased relative's age housekeeper, mrs. 
grimstone--whom i had retained--whose portrait it was, and she reply 
with a scared look, 'horace, youngest son of sir algernon wimpole, who 
die here in 1745.' 

"'the face fascinate me,' i said. 'is there any history attach to 
it?' 

"'why, yes, sir!' she responded, her eyes fixed on the floor, 'but the 
late master never like refer to it.' 

"'is it as bad as that?' i said, laughing. 'tell me!' 

"'well, sir,' she began, 'they do say as how sir algernon, who was a 
thorough country squire--very fond of hunting and shoot and all sort 
of manly exercises--never like mr. horace, who was delicate and 
dandified--what the folk in those days use to style a macaroni. the 
climax came when mr. horace took up with the jacobites. sir algernon 
would have nothing more to do with him then and turn him adrift. one 
day there was a great commotion in the neighbourhood, the government 
troops were hunting the place in search of rebels, and who should come 
galloping up the avenue with a couple of trooper in hot pursuit but mr. 
horace. the noise brought out sir algernon, and he was so infuriate to 
think that his son was the cause of the disturbance, a "disgraceful 
young cub," he call him, that despite mr. horace's entreaty for 
protection, he ran him through with his sword. it was a dreadful thing 
for a father to do, and sir algernon bitter repent it. his wife, 
who had been devoted to mr. horace, left him, and at last, in a fit of 
despondency, he hang himself--out there, on one of the elm lining the 
avenue. it is still standing. ever since then they do say that the wood 
is haunted, and that before the death of any member of the family mr. 
horace is seen galloping along the old carriage drive.' 

"'pleasant,' i grunted. 'and how about the house--is it haunt too?' 

"'i daresn't say,' she murmured. 'some will tell you it is, and some 
will tell you it isn't.' 

"'in which category are you included?' i asked. 

"'well!' she said 'i have live here happy and comfortable forty-five 
years the day after to-morrow, and that speak for itself, don't it?' 
and with that she hobble off and show me the way to the dining-room. 

"what a house it was! from the hall proceed doorway and passages, 
more than the ordinary memory could retain. of these portals, one at 
each end conduct to the tower stairs, others, to the reception-rooms 
and domestic offices. in the right wing, besides bedroom galore, was a 
lofty and spacious picture gallery; in the left--a chapel; for the 
wimpole were, formerly, roman catholics. the general fitting and 
furniture, both of the hall and house in general, were substantial, 
venerable and strong corroborative of what mrs. grimstone hint 
at--they suggest ghosts. 

"the walls, line with black oak panels, or dark hangings that flutter 
mysterious each time the wind blew, were funereal indeed; and so high 
and narrow were the windows, that little was to be discern through 
them but cross-barred portion of the sky. one spot in particular 
appeal to my nerves--and that, a long, vaulted stone passage leading 
from a morning room to the foot of the back staircase. here the voice 
and even the footstep echo with a hollow, low response, and often 
when i have been hurry along it--i never dare walk slowly--i have 
fancied--and maybe it was more than fancy--i have been pursued. 

"time passed, and from being merely use to my new environments, i grew 
to take a pride in them, to love them. i made the acquaintance of 
several of my neighbours, those i deem the most desirable, and on 
return from winter abroad, brought home a bride, a young polish 
girl, who add lustre to the surroundings, and in no small degree 
help to dissipate the gloom. indeed, had it not been for the picture 
in the hall, and for the twilight shadow and twilight footstep in the 
stone passage, i should soon have cease to think of ghosts. ghosts, 
forsooth! when all around me vibrate with the sound of girlish 
laughter, and the summer sunshine, sparkle on the golden curl of my 
child-wife, saw itself reflect a millionfold in the allure depths of 
her azure eyes. in halcyon days like these who think of ghost and 
death? 

"and yet! it is in just such times as these that hell is nearest. there 
came a night in august when the air was so hot and sultry that i could 
scarcely breathe, and unable to bear the atmosphere of the house and 
garden any longer, i sought the cool of the wood. olga--my 
wife--did not accompany me, as she was suffering from a slight--thank 
god, it was only slight--sunstroke. it was close on midnight, and there 
was a dead still abroad that seem as if it must be universal--as 
if it envelop the whole of nature. i try to realize london--to 
depict the strand and piccadilly, aglow with artificial light and 
reverberate with the roll of countless traffic and the tread of 
million of feet. 

"i failed. the incongruity of such imagine here--here amidst 
omnipotent silence--rendered such thought impossible. a leaf rustled, 
and its rustling sound to my ear like the gentle close of some 
giant door. a twig fell, and i turn sharp round, convinced i should 
see a pile of broken debris. i love all trees, but i love them best by 
day--to me it seem that night utterly metamorphize them--brings out in 
them a subtler, dark side one would little suspect. here, in this oak, 
for instance, was an example. in the morning one see in it nought but 
quiet dignity, venerable old age, benevolence, and, by reason of the 
ample protection its branch afford from the sun, charity and 
philanthropy. its leaves are bright, dainty, pretty; its trunk suggest 
nothing but a cosy and soothe retreat for student and lovers. but 
now--see how different! these great spreading, gnarled branch are 
hands, claws--monstrous and menacing; those leaves no long bright 
remind me of a hearse's plumes; their rustling--of the rustling and 
switch of a pall or winding-sheet. the trunk, black, sinuous, 
towering, is assured no piece of timber, but something pulpy, 
something intangible, something antagonistic, mystic, devilish. i turn 
from it and shudder. then my mind revert to the elm--the elm on which 
sir algernon hang himself. i remember it is not more than twenty yard 
from where i stand. i stare down at the soil, at the clump of crested 
dog's-tail and stray blade of succulent darnel; i force my attention on 
a toadstool, whose soft and lowly head gleam sickly white in the 
moonbeams. i glance from it to a sleep close-capped dandelion, from 
it to a thistle, from it again to a late bush vetch, and then, 
willy-nilly, to the accursed elm. my god! what a change. it wasn't like 
that when i pass it at noon. it was just an ordinary tree then, but 
now, now--and what is that--that sinister bundle--suspended from one 
of its curling branches? a cold sweat burst out on me, my knee 
tremble, my hair begin to rise on end. swinging round, i am about to 
rush away--blindly rush away--hither, thither, anywhere--anywhere out of 
sight of that tree and of all the hideous possibility it promise to 
materialize for me. i have not taken five strides, however, before i am 
pull sharp up by the sound of horse's hoofs--of hoof on the hard 
gravel, away in the distance. they speedy grow nearer. a horse is 
galloping, galloping towards me along the broad carriage drive. nearer, 
near and near it comes! who is it? what is it? a deadly nausea 
seize me, i swerve, totter, reel, and am only prevent from fall by 
the timely interference of a pine. the concussion with its leviathan 
trunk clear my senses. all my faculty become wonderful and 
painful alert. i would give my very soul if it were not so--if i could 
but fall asleep or faint. the sound of the hoof is very much near 
now, so near indeed that i may see the man--heaven grant it may be only 
a man after all--any moment. ah! my heart give a great sickly jerk. 
something has shot into view. there, not fifty yard from me, where the 
road curves, and the break in the foliage overhead admit a great flood 
of moonlight. i recognize the "thing" at once; it's not a man, it's 
nothing human, it's the picture i know so well and dread so much, the 
portrait of horace wimpole, that hang in the main hall--and it's 
mount on a coal-black horse with wildly flying mane and foam mouth. 
on and on they come, thud, thud, thud! the man is not dress as a 
rider, but is wearing the costume in the picture--i.e. that of a 
macaroni! a nut! more fit for a lady's seminary than a fine, old english 
mansion. 

"something beside me rustles--rustles angrily, and i know, i can feel, 
it is the bundle on the branch--the ghastly, groaning, creaking, 
croak caricature of sir algernon. the horseman come up to me--our 
eyes meet--i am look in those of a dead--of a long since dead man--my 
blood freezes. 

"he flash past me--thud, thud, thud! a bend in the road, and he 
vanish from sight. but i can still hear him, still hear the mad patter 
of his horse's hoof as they bear him onward, lifeless, fleshless, 
weightless, to his ancient home. god pity the soul that know no rest. 

"how i got back to the house i hardly know. i believe it was with my 
eyes shut, and i am certain i ran all the way. 

"about four o'clock the following afternoon i received a cablegram from 
malta. intuition warn me to prepare for the worst. its contents were 
unpleasant short and pithy--'hal drown at two o'clock this 
morning.--dick.' 

"two years passed--again an august night, hot and oppressive as before, 
and again--though surely against my will, my better judgment, if you 
like--i visit the wood. horse's hoof just the same as before. the 
same galloping, the same figure, the same eyes! the same mad, 
panic-stricken flight home, and, early in the succeed afternoon, a 
similar cablegram--this time from sicily. 'dick die at midnight. 
dysentery.--andrews.' 

"jack andrews was dick's pal--his bosom friend. so once again the 
phantom rider had brought its grisly message--played its ghoulish rôle. 
my brother were both dead now, and only beryl remained. another year 
sped by and the last night in october--a monday--saw me, impel by a 
fascination i could not resist, once again in the wood. up to a point 
everything happen as before. as the monotonous church clock struck 
twelve, from afar came the sound of hoofs. nearer, nearer, nearer, and 
then with startle abrupt the rider shot into view. and now, mixed 
with the awful, indescribable terror the figure always convey with it, 
came a feeling of intense rage and indignation. should beryl--beryl whom 
i love next best to my wife--be torn from me even as dick and hum had 
been? no! ten thousand times no! soon than that i would risk anything. 
a sudden inspiration, coming maybe from the whisper leaves, or from 
the elm, or from the mysterious flicker moonbeams, flash through 
me. could i not intercept the figures, drive them back? by do so 
something told me beryl might be saved. a terrible struggle at once took 
place within me, and it was only after the most desperate effort that i 
at length succeed in fight back my terror and flung myself out into 
the middle of the drive. no words of mine can describe all i went 
through as i stood there anticipate the arrival of the phantoms. at 
length they came, right up to me; and as, with frantic resolution, i 
screw up courage to plant myself directly in their path, and stare up 
into the rider's eyes, the huge steed halted, gave one shrill neigh, and 
turning round, gallop back again, disappear whither it had 
emerged. 

"two days afterwards i received a letter from my brother-in-law. 

"'i have been have an awful time,' he wrote. 'my darling beryl has 
been frightfully ill. on monday night we gave up all hope of her 
recovery, but at twelve o'clock, when the doctor bid us prepare for the 
end, the most extraordinary thing happened. turning over in bed, she 
distinct call out your name, and rallied. and now, thank god, she is 
completely out of danger. the doctor say it is the most astonish 
recovery he has ever known.' 

* * * * * 

"that is twenty years ago, and i've not seen the phantom rider since. 
nor do i fancy he will appear again, for when i look into the eyes of 
the picture in the hall, they are no long wandering, but at rest." 

* * * * * 

perhaps, one of the most interesting account of the phantasm of a horse 
in my possession is that record by c.e. g----, a friend of my boyhood. 
writing to me from the united states some month ago, he says: 

"knowing how interested you are in all case of hauntings, and in those 
relate to animal ghost especially, i am send you an account of an 
'experience' that happen to my uncle, mr. john dale, about six month 
ago. he was return to his home in bishopstone, near helena, montana, 
shortly after dark, and had arrive at a particularly lonely part of the 
road where the tree almost meet overhead, when his horse show sign 
of restlessness. it slacken down, halted, shivered, whinnied, and kept 
up such a series of antics, that my uncle descended from the trap to see 
if anything was wrong with it. he thought that, perhaps, it was go to 
have some kind of fit, or an attack of ague, which is not an uncommon 
complaint among animal in his part of the country, and he was prepare 
to give it a dose of quinine, when sudden it rear up violently, and 
before he could stop it, was career along the road at lightning 
speed. my uncle was now in a pretty mess. he was stranded in a forest 
without a lantern, ten miles, at least, from home. feeling too depressed 
to do anything, he sat down by the roadside, and seriously thought of 
remain there till daybreak. a twinge of rheumatism, however, remind 
him the ground was little warm than ice, and made him realize that 
lie on it would be courting death. consequently, he got up, and 
setting his lip grimly, struck out in the direction of bishopstone. at 
every step he took the track grew darker. shadow of tree and 
countless other things, for which he could see no counterpart, crept out 
and render it almost impossible for him to tell where to tread. a 
peculiar, indefinable dread also began to make itself felt, and the 
dark seem to him to assume an entire new character. he plod 
on, break into a jog-trot every now and then, and whistle by way of 
companionship. the still was sepulchral--he strained his ears, but 
could not even catch the sound of those tiny animal that are usually 
heard in the thicket and furze-bushes at night; and all his movements 
were exaggerated, until their echo seem to reverberate through the 
whole forest. a turn of the road brought him into view of something that 
made his heart throb with delight. standing by the wayside was an 
enormous coach with four huge horse paw the ground impatiently. my 
uncle rush up to the driver, who was so envelop in wraps, he could 
not see his face, and in a voice tremble with emotion beg for the 
favour of a lift--if not to helena itself, as far in that direction as 
the coach was going. the driver made no reply, but with his hand 
motion my uncle to get in. the latter did not need a second bidding, 
and the moment he was seated, the vehicle start off. it was a large, 
roomy conveyance, but had a stifle atmosphere about it that struck my 
uncle as most unpleasant; and although he could see no one, he 
intuitive felt he was not alone, and that more than one pair of eyes 
were watch him. 

"the coach did not go as fast as my uncle expected, but move with a 
curious gliding motion, and the wheels made no noise whatever. this 
add to my uncle's apprehensions, and he almost made up his mind to 
open the carriage door and jump out. something, however, which he could 
not account for restrained him, and he maintain his seat. outside, all 
was still profound dark. the tree were scarcely distinguishable as 
deep masses of shadow, and were recognize only by the resinous 
odour, that, from time to time, sluggish flow in at the open window 
as the coach roll on. 

"at length they overtook some other vehicle, and for the first time for 
some hour my uncle heard the sound of solid wheels, which were as 
welcome to him as any joy bells. just as they were passing the 
conveyance--a small wagonette drawn by a pair of horses, the latter took 
fright; there were loud shout and a great stampede, and my uncle, who 
lean out of the coach window, caught a glimpse of the vehicle dashing 
along ahead of them at a frightful speed. the driver of the coach, 
apparently total unconcerned, continue his journey at the same 
regular, mechanical pace. 

"presently my uncle heard the sound of rush water, and knew they must 
be near the usk, a tributary of the battle, which was only five mile 
from his house. 

"the forest now ceased, and they cross the road over the bridge in a 
brilliant burst of moonlight. about a mile or so further on the coach 
halted, and, to my uncle's surprise, he found himself in front of a 
house he had no recollection of seeing before. he got out, and to his 
horror saw that instead of riding in a coach he had been riding in a 
hearse, and that the horse had on their head gigantic sable plumes. 

"while he was standing gaze at the extraordinary equipage, the door of 
the house slow opened, and two figures came out carry a small 
coffin, which they placed inside the vehicle. he then heard loud peal 
of mad, hilarious laughter, and coach and horse immediately vanished. 
my uncle arrive home safely, but the shock of what he had experienced 
kept him in bed for some days. he learned that a phantom coach similar 
to the one he had ridden in had been seen in the forest twenty years 
previously, and that it was supposed to be a prognostication of some 
great misfortune, which supposition, in my uncle's case at least, prove 
true, as his wife die of apoplexy a few days after this adventure." 

yet another case of haunting by the phantasm of a horse come to me 
from a gentleman in marseilles, who told it me thus:-- 

"it was 9 p.m. when i left my friend maitland's hotel in châteauborne, 
and, facing north, set out on my way to liffre, where my headquarters 
had been for the past fortnight. liffre is in the hills, and the road 
which separate it from châteauborne, wild and lonely enough in daylight 
and when the weather is fair, is almost untraversable in winter. the 
night in question was christmas eve; the snow had fallen heavy during 
the day, and with the wind blow in icy draughts from the north-east, 
there was every prospect of another downfall. maitland pressed me to 
stay in his hotel. 'it is sheer folly,' he said, 'for you to attempt to 
get home in weather like this. it is pitch dark, you are not familiar 
with the route, and if you don't wander off the track and tumble over a 
precipice, you will walk into a snowdrift. be sensible--sleep here!' 

"much, however, as i should have like to follow his counsel, i did not 
feel justified in do so, as i had a lot of correspondence to attend 
to, and i realize it was most necessary for me to get back to liffre 
without any further delay. 

"it was true the night was inky black; but, with the aid of a lamp, i 
hadn't the slightest doubt i could find my way. maitland barter for a 
candle lantern with his host, and armed with this, a flagon of brandy 
and water and a thick stick, i said good-bye to châteauborne. 

"a couple of hundred yard saw me beyond the outskirts of the town, 
wherein i was the sole pedestrian, and silence reign supreme. on and 
on i plodded, the feeble, yellow light of my lantern just prevent 
me--but only just--from wandering from the track. the road, which for 
the first mile or so was tolerably level, gradual began to rise, and, 
as it did so, i notice for the first time indistinct image of 
gigantic, naked tree that becoming more and more numerous, and close 
and close together, at length united their long and grotesque shaped 
branch overhead, and i found myself in the depths of a vast forest. 
the snow, which had up to the present held off, now recommence to fall, 
and presently the wind, which had for some time been slow acquire 
strength, came howling through the tree with the utmost fury, the first 
blast swish the lantern out of my hand and hurling me with 
considerable force into an undergrowth of thorn and brambles, out of 
which i extricate myself with no little difficulty. 

"i was now in the sorriest of plights--enveloped on all side in stygian 
dark i was unable to discover my lantern, and was thus total at 
the mercy of the ruthless elements. there were only two course before 
me--either i must remain where i was and be frozen to death, or, making 
a guess at the route, i must push on ahead and run the risk of ending my 
life at the bottom of a ravine. i chose the latter. grope about with 
my feet, until i at length discover what i thought must be the right 
track, i pushed ahead, and, staggering and stumble forward, manage to 
make some sort of progress, terribly slow though it was. the blind 
dark of the snowy night, the intense silence and utter solitude of 
the place, combine with the knowledge that on all side of me lay hole 
and chasms, dampen my spirits and raise strange phantom in my 
imagination. the wind now rose, and the dismal sigh of the tree 
speedy grew into a series of the most perturb screeches, as the 
branch and trunks sway to and fro like reeds before the violence of 
the hurricane. 

"at this juncture i gave myself up for lost, and, coming to a standstill 
up to my knee in snow, was prepare to lie down and die, when, to my 
great joy, a light sudden appear ahead of me, and the next moment a 
man, mount on a big white horse, rode noiseless up to me. he was 
wrap in a shaggy great-coat, and a slouch hat worn low over his eyes 
completely hid his face from me. in his disengaged hand he carry a 
lantern. 

"'by jove!' i exclaimed, 'i am glad to see you, for i've lost the track 
to liffre. can you tell me, or, better still, show me, the way to some 
house where i can put up for the remainder of the night?' 

"the stranger made no reply, but bidding me follow with a wave of his 
hand, rode silent in front of me, and although i try to keep up with 
him, i could not; and the odd thing was, that without apparently 
increase his pace, he always maintain his distance. after proceeding 
in this manner for possibly ten minutes, we sudden turn to the left, 
and i found myself in a big clearing in the wood, with a long, low-built 
house opposite me. 

"my guide then paused, and indicate the front door of the house with 
an emphatic gesture of his hand, seem sudden to melt away into thin 
air, for although i peer about me on all side to try to find some 
indication of him, neither he nor his horse was anywhere to be seen. 
thinking this was rather queer, but quite ready to attribute it to 
natural causes, i approach the building, and, making use of my 
knuckle in lieu of a knocker, beat a loud tattoo on the woodwork. there 
was no response. again i rapped, and the door slow opening reveal a 
pair of gleaming, dark eyes. 'what do you want?' enquire a harsh voice 
in barbarous accents. 'a night's lodging,' i replied; 'and i'm willing 
to pay a good price for it, for i'm more than half frozen.' 

"at this the door open wider, and i found myself confront by a woman 
with a candle. she had not the most prepossessing of expressions, though 
her hair, eyes and features were decidedly good. she was dress with 
tawdry smartness--earrings, necklace, and rings, and very high-heeled 
buckle shoes. indeed, her costume was so out of keeping with the 
rustic of her surroundings as to be quite extraordinary. this fact 
struck me at once, as did her fingers, which, though spatulate and ugly, 
had been manicured, and of course very much over-manicured, for effect. 
had this not been the case, i probably should not have notice them. but 
the unnatural gloss on them, exaggerate by the candlelight, made me 
look, and i was at once impress with the criminal formation of the 
fingers--the club-shaped end denote something very bad--something 
homicidal--and as my eyes wander from the hand to the face, i saw 
with a thrill of horror that the ear were set low down and far back on 
the head, and that the eyes gleam with the sinister glitter of the 
wolf. 

"still, i must take my chance--the woman or the wood--it had to be one 
of the two. 'if you'll step inside, monsieur,' she said, 'i'll see what 
can be done for you. we have only recently come here, and the house is 
anyhow at present. still, if you don't mind rough it a little, we can 
let you have a bed, and you can rely upon me that it is clean and 
well-aired.' i follow her eagerly, and she led me down a narrow 
passage into a big room with a low ceiling, traverse with a ponderous 
oak beam, blacken with the smoke of endless peat fires. 

"before the blazing faggot on the hearth sat a burly-looking individual 
in a blue blouse. on our arrival he arose, and as his huge form tower 
above me, i thought i had never seen anyone quite so hideous, nor so 
utterly unlike the orthodox frenchman. obey his injunction--for i can 
scarcely call it an invitation--to sit down, i took a seat by the fire, 
and warm my half-frozen limbs, wait impatient whilst the woman 
made up my bed and prepared supper. 

"the storm had now reach cyclone dimensions, and under its stupendous 
fury the whole house--stoutly built though it was--swayed on its 
foundations. the howling of the wind in the rude, old-fashioned chimney 
and along the passage, and the frenzied beating of the snow against the 
diamond window-panes, deaden all other noises, and render any 
attempt at conversation absolutely abortive. so i ate my meal in 
silence, pretend not to notice the subtle interchange of glance that 
constant took place between the strange assorted pair. whether they 
were husband and wife, what the man did for a living, were question 
that continual occur to me, and i found my eyes incessant 
wandering to the numerous packing-cases, piles of carpets, cask and 
other articles, which corroborate the woman's statement that they had 
but recently 'moved in.' once i attempt to empty the coffee (which was 
black and peculiarly bitter) under the table, but had to desist, as i 
saw the man's devilish eyes fixed searching on me. i then pushed aside 
the cup, and on the woman ask if it was not to my liking, i shout 
out that i was not in the least thirsty. after this incident the covert 
looks became more numerous, and my suspicion increase accordingly. 

"at the first opportunity i got up, and signal my intention to go to 
bed, was prepare to leave my seat, when my host, walking to a 
cupboard, fetch out a bottle of cognac, and pour out a tumbler, 
hand it me with a mien that i dare not refuse. 

"the woman then led me up a flight of rickety, wooden steps and into a 
sepulchral-looking chamber with no other furniture in it save a long, 
narrow, iron bedstead, a dilapidated washstand, a very unsteady, common 
deal table, on which was a looking-glass and a collar stud, and a 
rush-bottomed chair. setting the candlestick on the dressing-table, and 
assure me again that the bed was well aired, my hostess withdrew, 
observing as she left the room that she would get me a nice breakfast 
and call me at seven. at seven! how i wish it was seven now! as i 
stood in the midst of the floor shivering--for the room was icy cold, i 
sudden saw a dark shadow emerge from a remote corner of the room and 
slide surreptitious towards the door, where it halted. my eyes then 
fell on the lock, and i perceive that there was no key. no key! and 
that evil-looking pair below! i must barricade the door somehow. yet 
with what? there was nothing of any weight in the room! nothing! i began 
to feel horrible tired and sleepy--so sleepy that it was only with 
supreme effort i could prevent my eyelid closing. ah! i had it--a 
wedge! i had a knife. of wood there was plenty--a piece off the 
washstand, table, or chair. anything would suffice. i essay to 
struggle to the chair, my limb tottered, my eyelid closed. then the 
shadow from the doorway move towards and through me, and with the 
cold of its passage i revived! with desperate energy i cut a couple 
of chunk off the washstand, and paring them down, eventually succeed 
in slip them in the crack of the door, and rendering it impossible 
to open from the outside. that done, i stagger to the bed, and 
falling, dress as i was, on the counterpane, sank into a deep sleep. 
how long i slept i cannot say. i sudden heard the loud neigh of a 
horse which seem to come from just under my window, and, as in a 
vision, saw by my side in the bed a something which gradual develop 
into the figure of a man, the counterpart of the mysterious being in the 
shaggy coat who had guide me to the house. he was fully dressed, sound 
asleep and breathing heavily. as i was look a dark shadow fell across 
the sleeper's face, and on glancing up i perceived, to my horror, a 
black something crawl on the floor. near and near it came, until 
it reach the side of the bed, when i immediately recognize the evil, 
smirk face of my hostess. in one hand she held a lamp and in the 
other a horn-handled knife. setting the lamp on the floor, she cool 
undid the collar of the sleep man, and i saw a stud, the counterpart 
of the one on the dressing-table, fall on the bare boards with a sharp 
tap, and disappear in the surrounding darkness. then the woman felt the 
edge of the knife with her repulsive thumb, and calm cut the helpless 
man's throat. i screamed--and the murderess and her victim instantly 
vanished--and i realize i was alone in the room and very much awake. 
whether all that had occur was a dream, i cannot say with certainty, 
though i am inclined to think not. 

"for some minutes my heart pulsate painfully, and then as the sound of 
its throb grew faint and fainter, i heard a curious noise outside 
my room--someone was ascend the stairs. i endeavour to rise, but 
could not--fear, an awful, ungovernable fear, held me spellbound. the 
steps pause outside the door, the handle of which was gently turned. 
then there was a suggestive silence, then whispering, then another 
turning of the handle, and then--my state of coma abrupt ended, and i 
step noiseless out of bed and crept to the window. i was heard. 
'stop him,' the woman cry out, 'he's try to escape. use the gun.' 
she hurl herself against the door as she spoke, whilst the man tore 
downstairs. 

"it was now a matter of seconds, the slightest accident, a hesitation, 
and i was lost. swinging open the window, i scramble on the ledge, and 
without the slightest idea of the distance--dropped! there was a brief 
rush through air and i alighted--safe and sound--on the snow. 
blessed snow! had it not been for the snow i should in all probability 
have hurt myself! i alight not an instant too soon, for hardly had i 
touched the ground before my gigantic host came tear round the angle 
of the wall with a lantern in one hand and gun in the other. i 
immediately dashed away, and, thanks to the intense dark of the 
morning--for it must have been two o'clock--had no difficulty in evade 
my pursuer, who fire twice in rapid succession. 

"on and on i went, sometimes fall up to my armpit in the snowdrift, 
and sometimes stunning myself against a low-hanging branch of a tree. 
with the first ray of sunlight, however, my trouble came to an end. 
the snow had cease falling, and i quick alight on a track, which 
brought me to a village, whence i obtain a conveyance into liffre. 

"i report the affair to the local police, and a party of gendarme at 
once set off to arrest the miscreants. but, alas, they had fled. the 
house was pull down, and, on the soil being excavated, a dozen or more 
skeleton of men and women--all showing unmistakable sign of foul 
play--together with the remains of a horse, were found in various parts 
of the premises. the place was a veritable golgotha. i suppose the 
phantom horse and rider had appear to me with the sole purpose of 
making their fate known. if so, they at all event partly achieve their 
end, though the mystery surrounding their identity was never solved. all 
the remains, both human and animal, were remove elsewhere, and accord 
a decent burial. the site of their original interment, however, is, i 
believe, still haunted, and maybe will remain so till the miscreant are 
brought to book." 


_brief summary_ 

after a little consideration i am inclined to think there are quite as 
many authentic case of haunting by the phantasm of horse as by the 
phantasm of cat and dogs. innumerable horse die unnatural deaths. 
apart from those kill in war, many,--more particularly, it is true, in 
the olden times,--have been murder in the highway along with their 
masters; whilst all but the comparative few, when no long of use to 
their owners, are butcher in the slaughter-house, and subsequent 
despatch to the zoology gardens, to be eaten by lion and tigers. 
so much for christianity, and for man's gratitude. how much better would 
the promoter of the white slave traffic act be employed, if,--instead 
of try to pass a bill which obviously cannot cure the evil it aim 
at, but can only, by divert the course of that evil, drive from 
pillar to post thousand of defenceless, albeit err women,--they were 
to labour to secure a peaceful ending for our four-footed toilers, who 
work for us all their lives, never strike, never think of a pension for 
old age, and never even dream of a vote. alas! if only our poor horse 
could vote, what a different attitude would our pharisaic politician
