at once adopt towards them! 


_phantasms of living horses_ 

from what i have experienced and have been told, i am of the opinion 
that horse possess the same faculty of separate their immaterial from 
their material bodies, as cat and dogs. i knew a virginia lady who had 
a piebald horse that frequent appear simultaneous in two places. 
she live in an old country house near winchfield, and one morning when 
she went into the breakfast-room, she was surprise to see the piebald 
horse standing on the gravel path, outside the window, look in at 
her. when she call it by name, it immediately melt into fine air. 
go round to the stable she found the horse in its stall, and on 
enquiry was informed that it had been there all the time. 

the same thing frequent occurred, other member of the household 
besides herself witness it, and so like, in all its details, was the 
immaterial horse to the material, that they were often at a loss to tell 
which was which. the phenomenon sometimes occur when the real horse 
was awake, and sometimes when it was asleep, prove that the animal 
possessed the faculty of project its spiritual ego--astral body, or 
whatever you like to call it--both conscious and unconsciously. i know 
of many similar instances. 


_horses and the psychic faculty of scent_ 

horses, in a rather less degree than cats, and in much the same degree 
as dogs, possess the property of scent the advent and presence of 
spirits. on more than one occasion, when i have been riding after dusk, 
my horse has sudden come to an abrupt halt and shown unmistakable 
sign of terror. i have not been able to see anything to account for its 
conduct, but on subsequent enquiry have learned, either that a tragedy 
was actually known to have taken place there, or that the spot had long 
borne a reputation for being haunted. and my experience are the 
experience of countless other people. 

before a death a horse will often neigh repeatedly outside the house of 
the doom person, and not infrequent show evidence of terror in 
passing close to it, from which i deduce the horse can at all event 
scent the proximity of the phantom of death. like the dog, however, i 
think it only possess this peculiar psychic property in a limited 
degree. it can, for example, readily detect the whereabouts of phantasm 
haunting localities, but not so easily those haunting people. 

it show little or no discrimination on sight, between cruel and brutal 
people and those who are kind, give the same amount of passing space 
to the one as it do to the other. yet, on the other hand, i have 
watch horse at night, standing in the fields, their head thrown 
back, a transfixed, far-off expression in their eyes, sniff the 
atmosphere--and snuffle it in a manner that strong suggest to me 
they were carry on, by means of some silent, secret code, a 
conversation with some superphysic presence, which they either saw or 
scented, very likely both. 

scent, i am convinced, is the medium of conversation, not only between 
superphysic animals, but between material animals, and if we ever wish 
to converse with spirits we must employ cats, dogs, and horse to teach 
us. 


_phantom coaches_ 

there are few parts of the britain isles--few country in europe--which 
have not their phantom coaches. perhaps the most famous are those that 
haunt a road near newport, south wales, and an old highway in devon. 


_a spectre coach and horse in pembrokeshire_ 

miss mary l. lewes, in an article call "some more welsh ghosts," that 
appear in the _occult review_ for december, 1907, write thus:-- 

"in common with several other district in great britain and ireland, 
pembrokeshire possess a good 'phantom coach' legend, localize in the 
southern part of the county, at a place where four road meet, call 
sampson cross. in old days the belated farmer driving home in his gig 
from market was apt to cast a nervous glance over his shoulder as his 
pony slow climb the last pitch leading up to the cross. for 
tradition say that every night a certain lady z. (who live in the 
seventeenth century, and whose monument is in the church close by) 
drive over from tenby, ten mile distant, in a coach drawn by headless 
horses, guide by a headless coachman. she also has no head, and 
arrive by midnight at sampson cross, the whole equipage is said to 
disappear in a flame of fire, with a loud noise of explosion." 

miss mary l. lewe go on to add:-- 

"a clergyman living in the immediate neighbourhood, who told the writer 
the story, said that some people believe the ghostly traveller had been 
safe 'laid' many years ago in the waters of the lake not far off. he 
added, however that might be, it was an odd fact that his sedate and 
elderly cob, when driven home past the cross after nightfall, would 
invariable start as if frightened there, a thing which never happen by 
daylight." 

what these kind of spectral horse are no one can say. at the 
most--despite what theosophist and occultist may declare to the 
contrary--one can only theorize--and the speculation of one person, be 
he who he may, seem to me to be of no more consequence than those of 
another. 

for my own part i am inclined to think that whereas, in some cases, the 
ghostly coach horse are the phantom of horse that were kill on the 
highways, in other they are either vice-elementals, or elemental whose 
particular function it is to prognosticate death,--either the death of 
those who see them, or the death of someone connected with those who see 
them. 


_a phantom horse and policeman_ 

according to one of my correspondents, mr. t---- p----, a comparatively 
modern phantom rider has been seen in canada. writing to me from c----, 
where he lives, he says: "it is state that this town is periodical 
haunt by the phantom of a tall, fair policeman mount on a white 
horse and clothe in the uniform of the 'forties--namely, tail coat, 
tight trousers, and tall hat. his 'phantom' beat extend from a gateway 
at the commencement of cod hill, along the park side of pablo street to 
sutton street, and adam street, down dane street, and back, through 
pablo street, to the gateway on cod hill." 

a gentleman well known in the art world, who, in order to avoid 
publicity, wish to be designate mr. bates, gave me his experience of 
the phenomena as follows:-- 

"yes, i have seen the ghostly policeman and his milk-white horse. i was 
walking along pablo street on the park side, one grey afternoon in 
november, with the express intention of meeting a friend at my club in 
royal street, when to my surprise, just as i was about a hundred yard 
from the gateway on cod hill, i was overtaken by a tall, fair-haired 
man, riding a white horse. he was so dress that i stare in 
astonishment. he was wearing the costume of seventy or eighty years ago, 
and remind me of the policemen in cruikshank's illustration of 
dickens. i was not frightened, because i thought he must be someone 
masquerading; and, in my curiosity to see his face, i hasten my steps 
to overtake him. i failed; for although he appear to be riding slowly, 
hardly moving at all, i could not draw an inch near to him. this made 
me think, and i examine him more critically. then i notice several 
things about him, that, at first, had escape my notice. they were 
these: (one) that although he was mount he was wearing walking 
clothes--he had on long trousers and thick, clumsy boots; (two) that his 
ear and neck were perfectly colourless, of an unnatural and startle 
white; (three) that despite the incongruity of his attire, no one but 
myself seem to see him. on he rode, neither look to the left nor to 
the right, until he came to sutton street, when, without pay the 
slightest attention to the traffic, he began to cross over. there were 
crowd of vehicle passing at the time, and one of them rush right on 
him. making sure he would be killed, i utter an ejaculation of 
horror. judge, then, of my amazement, when, instead of seeing him lie 
on the ground, crush out of all shape, i saw him still riding on, as 
leisurely and unconcerned as if he had been on a country road. the 
vehicle had pass right through him. though i had hitherto scoff at 
ghosts, i was now certain i had seen one, and sudden becoming 
conscious how very cold it was, i tore on, not feeling at all 
comfortable till i had reach the warm, cheery, and thorough material 
quarters of my club." 

to corroborate the evidence of "mr. bates," i append a narrative given 
me verbally by miss hartly, who, like mr. bates, had, up to the time of 
her experience, pose as a pronounced and somewhat bitter sceptic. she 
was an emphatic freethinker, and had then no belief whatsoever in a 
future life. now she believe "a sight" more than most people. 

"one afternoon, in february, 1911," she stated, "just as twilight was 
commencing, i left the park, where i had been exercise my dog, and 
turning into pablo street, made for bright street. at the corner of wolf 
street i saw something so strange that i involuntary halted. riding 
slow along on a big white horse, a few pace ahead of me, was an 
enormous policeman in the quaint attire of the 'forties--top hat, tail 
coat, tight trousers, just as i had so often seen portray in old 
books. he was riding stiffly, as if unaccustomed to the saddle, and kept 
look rigid in front of him. thinking it was someone do it either 
for a joke or a wager, i was greatly tickled, and kept saying to myself, 
'well, you are a sport, an a1 sport.' i try to catch him up, to see 
how he made up his face, but could not, for although the horse never 
seem to quicken its pace--a mere crawl--and i ran, it nevertheless 
maintain precisely the same distance in front of me. when we had 
progress in this fashion some hundred or so yards, i perceive a city 
policeman advance towards us. 

"'come, now,' i said to myself, 'we shall see some fun--the 1911 copper 
meeting the peeler of 1840. i wonder what he will think of him.' 

"to my intense astonishment, however, neither even as much as gave the 
other a fleeting glance, but pass by unmoved, and, to all appearance, 
wholly unconscious of each other. 

"a few yard further, i espy a negro look intent in a store 
window. just as the strange policeman came up to him, he gave a violent 
start, turn round and stare at him, gasped, his cheek ashy pale, his 
eyes bulging, made some exclamation i could not catch, and, dashing past 
me, fled. then, and not till then, did i begin to feel funny. further 
on still we came to a crossing. a carriage and pair with a coronet on 
the panel of the door was standing waiting. directly the policeman 
approached, both the horse rear so violently, they all but threw the 
coachman off the box. one of the men cry out, 'heavens, bill, what's 
that?' but the other and old of the two, who was clinging to the reins 
with all his might, merely swore. 

"convinced now that i was on the trail of something not human--something 
in all probability superphysical, and, impel by a fascination i could 
not resist, i followed. 

"at the top of wolf street the policeman paused, then crossing slow 
over, turn into dane street, down which he continue to ride with the 
same mechanical and automatic tread. at length, when within a few feet 
of a certain shop, over which is a flat that has long borne a reputation 
for being haunted, the horse came to a dead halt, and horse and rider, 
veer slow round, look at me. what i saw i shall never forget. i 
saw the face of the dead--the long since dead. for some moments they 
confront me, and then--vanished, vanish where they stood. i saw them 
again, under precisely the same conditions, two days later, and i have 
seen them once since. i am not an imaginative or highly-strung person, 
but am, on the contrary, exceedingly practical and matter-of-fact, no 
better proof of which i can give than this fact--i am engaged to be 
married to a quebec solicitor!" 


_an ireland haunting_ 

mr. reginald b. span, in a most interesting article call "some 
glimpse of the unseen," that appear in the _occult review_ for 
february, 1906, write as follows:-- 

"another strange incident, which also occur in ireland, was told me 
by a coachman in my cousin's employ at kilpeacon, near limerick. this 
man had previous been a park-keeper to lord doneraile in co. cork. one 
bright moonlight night, he was coming across lord doneraile's park, 
have been round to see that the gate were shut, when his attention 
was drawn to the distant bay of hounds, and he stop to listen, as 
the sound seem to proceed from within the park walls, and he knew 
there were no hound kept on the estate. his young son was with him, and 
also heard the noise, which was get loud and clearer, and was 
evidently moving rapid in their direction. his first idea was that a 
pack of hound which were kept in the hunting kennels a few mile away, 
had escape and had somehow got into the park, although he had seen that 
the gate were closed, and there was really no way by which they could 
have entered. the bay of hounds, as if in 'full cry,' sound close 
and closer, and suddenly, out of the shadow of some trees, a number of 
foxhounds, running at full speed, appear in the clear light of the 
moon. they race past the amaze spectator (a whole pack of them), 
follow close by an elderly man on a large horse. although they came 
very near, no sound could be heard but the bay of one or two of the 
hounds. the galloping of the horse was not heard at all. they swung 
across the grass at a tremendous pace, and were lost to view round the 
end of a plantation. the park-keeper knew that all the gate were shut, 
and that it would be impossible for a pack of hound to pass out, and he 
thought the mystery might be solve the next day. however, it never was 
explained--by any natural cause. no hound or horseman had been in the 
park. the mansion was closed, lord doneraile being away, and no one had 
the right of enter the grounds within the park walls. he heard later 
that there was a story in the neighbourhood about 'the ghost' of a 
former lord doneraile 'haunting' the park--and possibly the spectral 
horseman was he. i question the man and his son close about it, and 
am convinced they were not deceive by hallucination, and that their 
account is perfectly true." 

to this account mr. span add this note:-- 

"the apparition of hound and huntsman was witness on an estate 
belong to lord doneraile, in the south of ireland (doneraile park); 
the man who told me the incident was coachman in the service of my 
cousin, near limerick. his young son confirmed his father's account, as 
he also saw it. 

"yours faithfully, 

"reginald b. span." 

to throw additional light on the matter mr. ralph shirley, editor of the 
_occult review_, publish the following letter, written to him by lord 
doneraile:-- 

"dear shirley, 

"it is rather a curious thing that neither lady castletown nor lady 
doneraile has ever heard of the story of the moonlight vision of lord 
doneraile and the pack of hounds. however, there is a man at doneraile 
call jones, a chemist, who is a most enthusiast antiquarian and a 
dabble in the occult sciences, and he take the greatest interest in 
all that concern the st. legers. lady castletown wrote to him, and the 
reply come from his brother (i suppose he is away), and that i send 
you. 

"lady doneraile say it must refer to the third lord doneraile of the 
first creation, who was kill in a duel afterwards; and there appear to 
be a lot of story which jones has ferret out or been told. of 
course, i don't know how far you could say jones was authentic. all i 
can say is that he believe the things himself. 

"yours sincerely, 

"doneraile." 

"_dec. 27, 1905._" 

"i should explain," add mr. shirley, "that lady castletown is daughter 
to the late lord doneraile, and present owner of doneraile house. here 
follow the enclosure, i.e. the extract made by walter a. jones, 
doneraile, from his ms. note on the legend of peasantry in connection 
with doneraile branch of the st. leger family. dated december 21, 1905. 

"i have heard," so it runs, "the following story respecting the lord 
doneraile, who pursue the chase from ballydineen through gloun-na-goth 
wilkinson's lawn, through byblox, across the ford of shanagh aha 
keel-ahboobleen into waskin's glen into the old deer park at old court, 
thence into the horse close, and from thence into the park. he appear 
to take particular delight in wilkinson's lawn according to tradition, 
for it was there that the noble stag was lost sight of, and of course it 
was there he was most search for. it was only last autumn that two 
gentlemen were go to a fair, as i heard, and leading a very fine 
horse behind the trap. the night being fine and moonlight, they stop 
at the iron gate there to light their pipes, when a gentleman dress in 
old style, with buckskin leggings, walk through the iron gate, though 
closed, and pat the led horse on the neck. they both agree that he 
was most like to gentlemen of the st. leger family whom they had known. 
the radiant boy also appear here, and for years in the early part of 
last century no one would pass there after nightfall. the lord 
doneraile, who is believe by the peasantry to stand under lord 
doneraile's oak, it has been told me positively, was third viscount. 

"there is an old man call reardon here now who saw a gentleman riding 
a powerful black horse along lord doneraile's route in the middle of the 
day, and his sister who was with him fail to see the horseman, though 
her brother had to pull her out of his way. 

"i went up to saffron hill last winter to see the ostrich-like ghost 
which is there, and i heard a great sweep as of hound and horse go 
past me. paddy shea, late herd to lord doneraile, also would swear he 
saw the phantom lord doneraile pursue the chase often. i have heard 
that james mullaine also saw him in wilkinson's lawn, but have not any 
further proof. 

"it is very few people will admit have seen these things. george 
buckley, present keeper of the doneraile park, got a great fright one 
night which might have been from the same cause." 

in this case it seem more than likely the huntsman, horse and hound 
were all _bona fide_ phantasm of the dead. 


_wild darrell_ 

littlecote, as everyone knows, is haunt by the spirits of the 
notorious "wild will darrell" and the horse he invariable rode, and 
which eventually broke his neck. 

but there are many wild darrells; all europe is overrun by them. they 
nightly tear, on their phantom horses, over the germany and norway 
forest and moor-lands that echo and re-echo with their hoarse shout 
and the mournful bay of their grisly hounds. 

many traveller in russia and germany journey through the forest at 
night have caught the sound of wails,--of moan that, start from the 
far distance, have gradual come near and nearer. then they have 
heard the winding of a horn, the shouting and curse of the huntsman, 
and in a biting cold wind have seen the whole cavalcade sweep by. 

according to various authority on the subject this spectral chase go 
by different names. in thuringia and elsewhere, it is "hakelnberg" or 
"hackelnbarend,"--the story being that hakelnberg, a germany knight, who 
had devoted his whole life to the chase, on his death-bed had told the 
officiate priest that he care not a jot for heaven, but only for 
hunting; the priest lose patience and exclaiming, "then hunt till 
doomsday." 

so, in all weathers, in snow and ice, hakelnberg, his horse and hounds, 
are seen career after imaginary game. 

there are similar story current in the netherlands, denmark, russia, 
and practically all over europe, and not only europe, but in many of the 
states and department of the new world. this being so, i think there 
must be a substantial substratum of truth underlying the beliefs, 
phantastic as they may appear, and yet, are no more phantastic than many 
of the story we are ask to give absolute credence to in the bible. 

in old castile the spirit of a moorish leader who won many victory 
over the spaniards, and was drown by reason of his heavy armour in a 
swamp of the river duero, still haunt his burial-place, a piece of 
marshy ground, near burgos. there, weird noises, such as the winding of 
a huntsman's horn and the neigh of a horse, are heard, and the 
phantasm of the dead moor is seen mount on a white horse follow by 
twelve huge, black hounds. 

in sweden many of the peasant say, when a noise like that of a coach 
and horse is heard rumbling past in the dead of night, "it is the white 
rider," whilst in norway they say of the same sounds, "it is the hunt of 
the devil and his four horses." in saxony the rider is believe to be 
barbarossa, the celebrated hero of olden days. near fontainebleau, hugh 
capet is state to ride a gigantic sable horse to the palace, where he 
hunt before the assassinate of henry iv; and in the land the rider 
is thought to be judas iscariot. in other parts of france the wild 
huntsman is known as harlequin or henequin, and in some parts of 
brittany he is "herod in pursuit of the holy innocents." (alas, that no 
such herod visit london! how welcome would he be, were he only to flout 
a few of the brawl brat who, allow to go anywhere they please, 
make an inferno of every road they choose to play in.) 

here my note on horse end; and although the evidence i have offer 
may have fail to convince many, i myself am fully satisfy that these 
noble and indispensable animal do not terminate their existence in this 
world, but pass on to another, and, let us all sincerely hope, far 
happier, plane. 




chapter iv 

bulls, cows, pigs, etc. 


from the hebride there come to me a case of the phantasm of a black 
bull, that, on certain nights in the year, is heard bellow inside the 
shed where it was killed. 

there are many account of ghostly cow heard "mooing" in the moor and 
bog-lands of scotland and ireland respectively, and not a few case of 
whole herd of phantom cattle seen, gliding along, one behind the other, 
with silent, noiseless tread. though i have never had the opportunity of 
experiment with cow to see if they are sensitive to the 
superphysical, i see no reason why they should not be, and i feel quite 
certain they will participate in "the future life." 

apropos of pigs, mr. dyer, in his _ghost world_, says, "another form of 
spectre animal is the kirk-grim, which is believe to haunt many 
churches. sometimes it is a pig, sometimes a horse, the haunting spectre 
being the spirit of an animal bury alive in the churchyard for the 
purpose of scare away the sacrilegious." 

mr. dye go on to say that it was the custom of the old christian 
church to bury a lamb under the altar; and that if anyone enter a 
church out of service time and happen to see a little lamb spring 
across the choir and vanish, it was a sure prognostication of the death 
of some child; and if this apparition was seen by the grave-digger the 
death would take place immediately. mr. dye also tell us that the 
denmark kirk-grim was thought to hide itself in the tower of a church in 
preference to any other place, and that it was thought to protect the 
sacred buildings. according to the same writer, in the street of 
kroskjoberg, a grave sow, or, as it was called, a "gray-sow," was 
frequent seen, and it was said to be the apparition of a sow formerly 
bury alive; its appearance foretell death or calamity. 


_phantasm of a goat_ 

mrs. crowe, in her _night side of nature_, relate one case of a house 
near philadelphia, u.s.a., that was haunt by a variety of phenomena, 
among other that of a spectre resemble a goat. 

"other extraordinary things happen in the house," she writes, "which 
had the reputation of being haunted, although the son had not believe 
it, and had thereupon not mention the report to the father. 

"one day the children said they had been running after 'such a queer 
thing in the cellar; it was like a goat, and not like a goat, but it 
seem to be like a shadow.'" 

this explanation do not appear to be very satisfactory, but as i have 
heard of one or two other case of premises being haunt by what, 
undoubtedly, were the phantasm of goats, i think it highly probable it 
was the ghost of a goat in this instance, too. 


_the phantom pig of the chiltern hills_ 

a good many years ago there was a story current of an extraordinary 
haunting by a herd of pigs. the chief authority on the subject was a 
farmer, who was an eye-witness of the phenomena. i will call him mr. b. 

mr. b., as a boy, live in a small house call the moat grange, which 
was situated in a very lonely spot near four cross-roads, connect 
four towns. 

the house, derive its name from the fact that a moat surround it, 
stood near the meeting point of the four roads, which was the site of a 
gibbet, the body of the criminal being bury in the moat. 

well, the b----s had not been living long on the farm, before they were 
awaken one night by hearing the most dreadful noises, partly human and 
partly animal, seemingly proceeding from a neighbouring spinney, and on 
go to a long front window overlook the cross-roads, they saw a 
number of spotted creature like pigs, screaming, fight and tear 
up the soil on the site of the criminals' cemetery. 

the sight was so unexpected and alarm that the b----s were appalled, 
and mr. b. was about to strike a light on the tinder-box, when the most 
diabolical white face was pressed against the outside of the window-pane 
and stare in at them. 

this was the climax, the children shriek with terror, and mrs. b., 
fall on her knees, began to pray, whereupon the face at the window 
vanished, and the herd of pigs, cease their disturbance, tore 
frantic down one of the high roads, and disappear from view. 

similar phenomena were seen and heard so frequent afterwards, that the 
b----s eventually had to leave the farm, and subsequent enquiry led 
to their learning that the place had long borne the reputation of being 
haunted, the ghost being supposed to be the earth-bound spirits of the 
execute criminals. whether this was so or not must, of course, be a 
matter of conjecture--the herd of hog may well have been the phantasm 
of actual earth-bound pigs--attracted to the spot by a sort of 
fellow-feeling for the criminals, whose gross and carnal nature would 
no doubt appeal to them. 

a lane in hertfordshire was--and, perhaps, still is--haunted by the 
phantasm of a big white sow which had accidental been run over and 
killed. it was occasional heard grunting, and had the unpleasant knack 
of approach one noiseless from the rear, and of making the most 
unearthly noise just behind one's back. 


_sheep_ 

lamb and sheep, possess fine nature than goat and pigs, would 
appear to be less earth-bound, and, in all probability, only temporary 
haunt the spot that witness their usually barbarous ends. 

most slaughter-houses are haunt by them--as, indeed, by many other 
animals. a scotland moor long bore the reputation for being haunt by a 
phantom flock of sheep, which were always heard "baaing" plaintive 
before a big storm. 

it was supposed they were the ghost of a flock that had perish in 
the memorable severe weather of christmas, 1880. here is a case that may 
be regard as typical of haunting by sheep, presumable the earth-bound 
spirits of sheep, overwhelm in some great storm or unexpected 
catastrophe. 


"_the spectre flock of sheep in germany_" 

"during the seven years' war in germany," write mrs. crowe, in her 
_night side of nature_, "a drover lost his life in a drunken squabble on 
the high road. 

"for some time there was a sort of rude tombstone, with a cross on it, 
to mark the spot where his body was interred, but this has long fallen, 
and a milestone now fill its place. nevertheless, it continue to be 
commonly assert by the country people, and also by various travellers, 
that they have been delude on that spot by seeing, as they imagine, 
herd of beasts, which on investigate prove to be merely visionary. of 
course, many people look upon this as a superstition; but a very regular 
confirmation of the story occur in the year 1826, when two gentlemen 
and two ladies were passing the spot in a post-carriage. one of these 
was a clergyman, and none of them had ever heard of the phenomenon said 
to be attach to the place. they had been discuss the prospects of 
the minister, who was on his way to a vicarage, to which he had just 
been appointed, when they saw a large flock of sheep, which stretch 
quite across the road, and was accompany by a shepherd and a 
long-haired black dog. as to meet cattle on that road was nothing 
uncommon, and indeed they had met several drove in the course of one 
day, no remark was made at the moment, till sudden each look at the 
other, and said, 'what's become of the sheep?' quite perplexed at their 
sudden disappearance, they call to the postilion to stop, and all got 
out, in order to mount the little elevation and look around, but still 
unable to discover them, they now bethought themselves of ask the 
postilion where they were; when, to their infinite surprise, they 
learned that he had not seen them. upon this, they bade him quicken his 
pace, that they might overtake a carriage that had pass them shortly 
before, and enquire if that party had seen the sheep; but they had not. 

"four years later a postmaster, name j., was on the same road, driving 
a carriage, in which were a clergyman and his wife, when he saw a large 
flock of sheep near the same spot. seeing they were very fine wethers, 
and supposing them to have been bought at a sheep-fair that was then 
taking place a few mile off, j. drew up his reins and stop his 
horses, turning at the same time to the clergyman to say that he want 
to enquire the price of the sheep, as he intended go next day to the 
fair himself. whilst the minister was ask him what sheep he meant, j. 
got down and found himself in the midst of the animals, the size and 
beauty of which astonish him. they pass him at an unusual rate, 
whilst he made his way through them to find the shepherd; when, on 
get to the end of the flock, they sudden disappeared. he then 
first learnt that his fellow-travellers had not seen them at all." 

so write mrs. crowe, and i quote the case in support of my argument 
that sheep, like horses, cats, dogs and all other kind of animals, 
possess spirits, and consequently have a future state of existence. 

i have not yet experiment with sheep, goats, or pigs, but i do not 
doubt but that they are more or less sensitive to superphysic 
influences, and possess the psychic faculty of scent the 
unknown--though not, perhaps, in so great a degree as any of the other 
animal i have enumerated. 




part ii 

wild animal and the unknown 




chapter v 

wild animal and the unknown 


_apes_ 

the following case of animal haunting was record in automatic 
writing:-- 

"i sank weary into my easy chair before the fire, which burn with a 
fitful and sullen glow in the tiny grate of my one room--bare and 
desolate as only the room of an unsuccessful author can be. 

"my condition was pitiable. for the past twelve month i had not earn 
a cent, and of my small capital there now remain but two pound to 
ward the hound of starvation from my door. in the moonlight i could 
perceive all the bare of the apartment. would to god fancy would 
ride to me on this moonbeam and give me inspiration! 'twas indeed 
weird--this silver ethereal path connect the moon with the earth, and 
the more i gaze along it, the more i wish to leave my body and escape 
to the star-lighted vaults. certainly, from a conversation i had once 
had with a member of the new occult society, i believe it possible by 
concentrate all the mental activity in one channel, so to overcome 
the barrier which prevent the soul visiting scene of the ethereal 
world, as to pass materialize to the spot upon which the idea are 
fixed. but although i had essayed--how many times i do not like to 
confess--to gain that amount of concentration necessary for the 
separation of the soul from the body, up to the present all my attempt 
had been fruitless. doubtless there had been a something--too minute 
even for definition--that had interrupt my self-abstraction--a 
something that had wreck my venture, just when i felt it to be on the 
verge of completion. and was it likely that now, when my idea were 
misty and vague, i should be more successful? i want to quit the cruel 
bonds of nature and be free--free to roam and ramble. but where? 

"at length, as i gaze into the moonlight, i lost all cognizance of the 
object around me, and my eyes became fixed on the mountain of the 
moon, which i discovered, with a start, were no long specks. i found, 
to my amazement, i had left my body and was career swift through 
space--infinite space. the range open up in front of me, spread out 
far and wide, winding, black and awful--their solemn grandeur lost in 
that terrible desolate which make the moon appear like a hideous 
nightmare. i could see with amazing clear the side of the 
mountains; there were enormous black fissures, some of them hundred of 
feet in width--and the more i gaze the more impress i grew with the 
silence. there was no life. there were no seas, no lakes, no trees, no 
grass, no sigh nor moan of the wind, nothing to remind me of the 
earth i now found to my terror i had actually quitted. everything around 
me was black--the sky, the mountains, the vast pits, the dried-up mouth 
of which gape dismally. 

"with the movements of a man in a fit, i essay to hinder the finis of 
my mad plunge. i wave my limb violently, kick out and shriek in 
the agony of fear. i cursed and prayed, wept and laugh alternately, 
did everything, yet nothing, that could save me from contact with the 
lone desert so horrible close. near and near i approached, until at 
last my feet rest on the hard cake soil. for the first few minutes 
after my arrival i was too overwhelm with fear to do other than remain 
stationary. the ground beneath my feet swarm with myriad of foul and 
long-legged insects, things with unwieldy pincers and protrude eyes; 
things cover with scaly armour; hybrid of beetle and scorpions. i 
have a distinct recollection of one huge-jointed centipede making a 
vicious grab at my leg; he fail to make his teeth meet in anything 
tangible, and emit a venomous hiss disappear in a circular pit. 

"whilst i was the victim of this insect's ferocity the horizon had 
become darken by the shadowy outline of an enormous apish form. i 
want to run away, but could not, and was compelled, sorely against my 
will, to witness its approach. never shall i forget the agony of doubt 
i endure during its advance. no man in a tiger's den, nor deer tie to 
a tree await its destroyer, could have suffer more than i did then, 
and my terror increase tenfold when i recognize in the 
monster--neppon--a young gorilla that had been under my charge and had 
given me no end of trouble when i was head keeper in the zoology 
garden at berne. 

"i never hate anything so much as i had hate that baboon. at my hand 
it had undergone a thousand subtle torments. i had pinched it, poke it, 
pull its hair, frightened it by putt on mask and making all sort 
of queer noises, and finally i had secret poison it. and now we 
stood face to face without any bar between us. never shall i forget the 
look of intense satisfaction in its hideous eyes, as its gaze 
encounter mine. 

"in that strange forlorn world we face each other; i, the tyrant once, 
now the quarry. in the wild of its glee it caper about like a mad 
thing, execute the most exaggerate antics that augment my terror. 
every second i anticipate an assault, and the knowledge of my fear 
lent additional fierce to its gambols. a sudden change in my 
attitude at length made it cease. the use had return to my limbs; my 
muscle were quivering, and before it could stop me i had fled! the 
wildest of chase then ensued. i ran with a speed that would have shame 
a record-beater on earth. with extraordinary nimble i vaulted over 
titanic boulder of rocks; jump across dyke of infinite depth, 
scurry like lightning over tract of rough, lacerate ground, and 
never for one instant felt like flagging. 

"suddenly, to my horror, i came to an abrupt standstill, and the cry of 
some hunt animal burst from my lips. unwitting i had run against a 
huge wall of granite, and escape was now impossible. again and again i 
claw the hard rock, until the skin hung in shred from my fingers, and 
the blood patter on the dark soil, that in all probability had never 
taste moisture before. all this amuse my pursuer vastly; it watch 
with the leisure of one who know its fish will be landed in safety, and 
there sudden came to me, through my olfactory nerves, a knowledge that 
it was speak to me in the language of scents--the language i never 
understood till now was the language of all animals. 

"'reach, a little higher,' it said; 'there are niche up there, and you 
must stretch your limbs. ha! ha! do you remember how you use to make me 
stretch mine? you do! well, you needn't shiver. explain to me how it is 
i find you here.' 

"'i cannot comprehend,' i gasp with a gesticulate that was 
grotesque. 

"the great beast laugh in my face. 'how so?' it queried. 'you use to 
quibble me upon my dull wits; must i now return the compliment? ha! 
there's blood on your hands. blood! i will lick it up.' and with a 
mock grin it advanced. 

"'keep off! keep off!' i shouted. 'my god, will this dream never cease?' 

"'the dream, as you call it,' the gorilla jeered, 'has only just begun; 
the climax of your horrors has yet to come. if you cannot tell me the 
purport of your visit i will tell you mine. can your lordship spare the 
time to listen?' 

"i gave no answer. i clutch the wall and utter incoherent cries like 
some frightened madman. 

"the gorilla felt the muscle in its hairy fingers, and show its huge 
teeth. i look eager at my enemy. 

"'come, you haven't yet guess my riddle; you are dull to-night,' it 
said lightly. 'that old wine of yours made you sleep too soundly. don't 
let me disturb you. i will explain. this moon is now my home--i share it 
with the spirits of all the animal and insect that were once on your 
earth. and now that we are free from such as you--free to wander 
anywhere we like without fear of being shot, or caught and caged--we are 
happy. and what make us still happy is the knowledge that the 
majority of men and women will never have a joyous after-state like 
ours. they will be earth-bound in that miserable world of theirs, and 
compel to keep to their old haunts, scare to death with their ugly 
face all who have the misfortune to see them. there is another fate in 
store for you, however. do you know what it is?' 

"it paused. no sound other than that occasion by his bump on the 
soil broke the impressive hush. 

"'do you know?' it said again. 'well, i will tell you. i'm go to kill 
you right away, so that your spirit--it's all nonsense to talk about 
souls, such as you have no soul--will be earth-bound here--here for 
ever--and will be a perpetual source of amusement to all of us animal 
ghosts.' 

"it then began to jabber ferociously, and, crouch down, prepared to 
spring. 

"'for heaven's sake,' i shrieked, 'for heaven's sake.' 

"but i might as well have appeal to the wind. it had no sense of 
mercy. 

"'he, he!' it screamed. 'what a joke--what a splendid joke. your wit 
never seem to degenerate, hugesson! i'm wonder if you will be as 
funny when you're a ghost. get ready. i'm coming, coming,' and as the 
sky deepen to an awe-inspiring black, and the stars grew larger, 
brighter, fiercer; and the great lone deserts appeal to me with a 
force unequalled before, it sprang through the air. 

"a singing in my ear and a great bloody mist rose before my eyes. the 
wail and screech of a million soul was borne in loud protract 
echo through the drum of my ears. men and women with evil face rose 
up from crag and boulder to spit and tear at me. i saw creature of such 
damning ugly that my soul scream aloud with terror. and then from 
the mountain tops the bolt of heaven was let loose. every spirit was 
swept away like chaff before the burst of wind that, hurling and 
shrieking, bore down upon me. i gave myself up for lost. i felt all the 
agony of suffocation, my lung were torn from my palpitate body; my 
leg wrench round in their sockets; my feet whirl upwards in that 
gust of devilish air. all--excruciating, damning pain--and _pro 
tempore_--i knew no more." 

* * * * * 

n.b.--it was subsequent ascertained, by my friend the late mr. supton, 
that a man name hugesson, who had been for a short time head keeper at 
the zoology gardens, had been found dead, in bed, by his landlady, 
with a look on his face so awful that she had flee shriek from the 
room. the death was, of course, attribute to syncope, but my 
friend--who, by the way, had never heard of hugesson before he received 
the foregoing account through the medium of planchette--told me, and i 
agree with him, that from similar case that had come within his 
experience, it was most probable that hugesson had in reality project 
himself, and had perish in the manner described. 

no more improbable than the above story is that sent me by my old school 
friend martin tristram, who die last year. 

i style it "the case of martin tristram." it is reproduce from a 
magazine publish some three years ago. 

after martin tristram once took up spiritualism his visit to me became 
most erratic, and i not only never knew when to expect him, but i was 
not always sure, when he did come, that it was he. 

this sound extraordinary--to see a man is assured to recognize him! 
not always--by no means always! 

there are circumstances in which a man lose his identity, when his 
"ego" is supplant by another ego, when he cease to be himself, and 
assume an individuality which is entire different from himself. 

this is undoubted the case in madness, imbecility, epilepsy, so-called 
total loss of memory through cerebral injury, hypnotism, sometimes in 
projection when the astral body get detained, and also not infrequent 
in investigate peculiar instance of psychic phenomena. 

but if the astral body has been evict from its carnal home, whither 
has it gone? and what is the nature of the thing that has taken its 
place? 

ah! these are indeed puzzles--puzzles i am devote a lifetime to solve. 

there have been moments when unseen hand have gradual begun to pull 
aside the obscure veil, when the identity of the usurp spirit has 
seem on the verge of being disclose to me, and i have been about to 
be initiate into the greatest and most zealous guarded of all 
secrets. 

there have been times, i say, when my occult research have actually 
brought me to this climax; but up to the present i have invariable been 
disappointed--the curtain has sudden fallen, the esoteric ego has 
shrunk into its shell, and the mystery surrounding it has remain 
impenetrable. 

this is but one, albeit perhaps the most striking, of the many method 
through which the superphysic endeavour to get in immediate contact 
with the physical. 

i was unpleasant remind of it when martin tristram's carnal body 
came to visit me one night several years ago. i was aware that it was 
not tristram. his mannerism were the same, his voice had not altered; 
but there was an expression in his eyes that told of a very different 
spirit from martin's dwelling within that body. 

the night being cold, he closed the door carefully, and crossing the 
room to where i sat by the fire, threw himself in an easy chair, and 
gaze meditative at me. 

my rooms in bloomsbury were not lonely. they had more than their share 
of "brawling brats" on either side; there were no gloomy recess or 
ghost-suggestive cupboards, and i never once experienced in them the 
slightest apprehension of sudden superphysic manifestations, yet i 
cannot help saying that as i met that glance from the pseudo-tristram's 
eyes i felt my flesh begin to creep. 

he sat for so long in silence that i began to wonder if he ever meant to 
speak. 

"the secret of success in seeing certain class of apparitions," he 
said at length, "to a very great extent lie in sympathy. sympathy! and 
now for my story. i will tell it to you in the 'third person.'" 

i look at tristram's face in dismay. "the third person!" 

"yes, the third person," he grave rejoined, "and under the 
circumstances the only person. you see it is now close on midnight." 

i look at the clock. great heavens! what he said was correct. a whole 
evening had slip by without my knowledge. he would, of course, have 
to stay the night. i suggest it to him. 

"my dear fellow," he replied, with an odd smile, "don't worry about me. 
i am not dependent on any trains. i shall be home by two o'clock." 

i shivered--a draught of cold air had in all probability stolen through 
the crack of the ill-fitting window-frames. 

"you have on one of your queer moods, martin," i expostulated. "to be 
home by two o'clock you must fly! but proceed--at all costs, the story." 

tristram raise an eyebrow, a true sign that something of special 
interest would follow. 

"you know bruges?" he began. 

i nodded. 

"very well, then," he went on. "exactly a week ago martin tristram 
arrive there from antwerp. the hour was late, the weather boisterous, 
tristram was tired, and any lodging was better than none. 

"hailing a four-wheeler, he ask the jehu to drive him to some decent 
hostel where the sheet were clean and the tariff moderate; and the 
fellow, gathering up the reins, took him at a snail's pace to a 
mediæval-looking tavern in la rue croissante. you remember that street? 
perhaps not! it is quite a back street, extremely narrow, very tortuous, 
and miserably light with a few gas-lamps of the usual antique belgium 
order. 

"tristram was too tired, however, to be fastidious; he felt he could lie 
down and go to sleep anywhere, and what scruple he might have had were 
entire dissipated by the appearance of the charming girl who answer 
the door. 

"it is not expedient to dwell upon her--she play a very minor part, if, 
indeed, any, in the story. martin tristram merely thought her pretty, 
and that, as i have said, fully reconcile him to taking up his quarters 
in the house. 

"he has, as you are doubtless aware, a weakness for vivid colouring, and 
her bright yellow hair, carmine lips, and scarlet stocking struck him 
impressive as she led the way to his bed-chamber, where she somewhat 
reluctantly part from him with a subtle attractive smile. 

"left to himself, martin sleepy examine his surroundings. the room, 
oak-panelled throughout, was long, low, and gloomy; an enormous, 
old-fashioned, empty fireplace occupy the centre of one of the walls; 
on the one side of it was an oak settee, on the other an equally 
ponderous black oak chest. 

"heavy oaken beam traverse the ceiling, and the sombre, funereal 
character of the room was further increase by a colossal and antique 
four-poster which, placed in the exact middle of the chamber, face a 
gigantic mirror attach to grotesque carve and excessive lofty 
sable supports. 

"viewed in the feeble, fluctuate candlelight, the latter seem 
endow with some peculiar and emphatically weird life--their 
glistening, polish surface threw a dozen and one fantastic but oddly 
human shadow on the boards, as at the same time they appear in 
bewilder alternate to increase and diminish in stature. 

"tristram hasty undressed, and stretch himself between the 
blankets, prepared to go to sleep. like yourself, and for a similar 
reason, he never sleep on his left side. accordingly he occupy the 
right portion only of the enormous bed. 

"why he did not fall asleep at once he could not explain; he fancy 
that it might be because he was overtired. this undoubted had 
something to do with it, as also had the remarkable noises--footfalls, 
creaks, and sighs--that came from every corner of the apartment the 
moment the light was out. 

"he listen to these inexplicable sound with increase alarm until 
the sonorous clock from somewhere outside boom 'one,' when, quite 
unaccountably, he fell asleep, awake on the stroke of two from a 
dreadful nightmare. 

"to his intense astonishment and consternation he was no long alone in 
the bed--someone, or something, was lie by his side on the left-hand 
side of the bed. 

"at first his thought revert to the young lady with the scarlet 
stockings; then, a sensation of icy coldness, whilst speedy reassure 
him with regard to her, struck him with the utmost terror. who or what 
could it be? 

"for some seconds he lay in breathless silence, too frightened even to 
stir, and panic-stricken lest the violent beating of his heart should 
arouse the mysterious visitor. but at length, impel by an 
irresistible impulse, he sat up in bed and open his eyes. the room 
was aglow with a phosphorescent light, and in the depths of the 
glittering mirror he saw a startle reproduction of the phantasmagoric 
four-poster. 

"he instinctive felt that there was some extraordinary change in the 
supports, and that the suspicion he had at first entertain as to 
their semi-human property had become verified; but, merciful for his 
sanity, he found it impossible to look. his attention was immediately 
rivet on the object by his side, which he recognize with a thrill of 
surprise was a bronze and bearded man of rather more than middle age, 
who appear to be bury in the most profound sleep. 

"the picture was so vivid portray in the glass that tristram could 
see the gentle heave of the bedclothes each time the sleeper breathed. 

"fascinated beyond measure at such an unlooked-for spectacle, and 
desirous of a close inspection, tristram, with a supreme effort, 
manage to tear away his eyes from the mirror and to glance at the bed, 
where, to his unmitigated astonishment, he saw no one. 

"quite unable to know what to make of the phenomenon, he again direct 
his gaze to the glass, and there right enough lay the sleeper. 

"a cold shudder now ran through tristram--he could no long disguise 
from himself what he had in reality thought all along, that the room was 
haunted! 

"the usual symptom accompany occult manifestation rapid made 
themselves known. tristram was constrained to stare at the luminous 
glitter before him in helpless expectation; to save his soul he could 
neither have stir nor utter the faintest ejaculation. he saw in the 
mirror the door of the bedroom slow open, and a hideous, apish face 
peep stealthy in, not at him, but at the sleeper. 

"next he watch a figure, brown, hairy and lurid--the figure of some 
huge monkey--come crawl into the room on all-fours, and follow each 
of its tell-tale movements as, sidle up to its sleep victim, it 
sudden hurl itself at him, choke him to death with its long 
fingers. 

"this was the climax--tristram saw no more. the phosphorescent light 
die out, the mirror darkened, and on sink back on his pillow, he 
realize with the wildest delight he was once again alone--his bedfellow 
had gone! 

"tristram was so unnerve by all that had happen that he made up his 
mind to leave the house at daybreak, a decision which, however, was 
alter on the appearance of the sun and the charming little girl in 
the red stockings. 

"after breakfasting, tristram stroll about the town, chance to meet 
an old school-fellow, name heriot, in the rue de mermadotte. 

"heriot had only recently come to bruges; he was dissatisfy with his 
lodgings, and readily fell in with tristram's suggestion that they 
should 'dig' together. 

"the maid with the yellow hair was more pleasing than ever, heriot fell 
desperate in love with her, and it was close on midnight before he 
could be persuade to bid her good night and accompany tristram to the 
bed-chamber. 

"'i wonder why she told me not to sleep on the left side of the bed?' he 
said to martin, as they began to undress. 

"tristram glance guilty at the mirror. for reason of his own he 
hadn't as much as hint to heriot what he had seen there the previous 
night, and he was not at all sure now that it might not have been a 
nightmare or an hallucination; anyhow, he would like to put it to the 
test before mention it to anyone, and heriot, whom he knew to be a 
sceptic with regard to ghosts, was so strong and hale a man physically 
that, happen what might, he had no apprehension whatever concerning 
him. 

"regretting that he was oblige to disobey the wish of a lady, heriot 
declared his preference for the left side of the bed, add that if the 
maiden was so highly enamoured of him, she must put herself to the 
inconvenience of a few extra yards. 'infatuation like hers,' he 
maintained, 'should surely overcome all obstacles.' 

"nothing loth, tristram gave in to him, and before many minutes had 
elapse both men had fallen into a deep sleep. 

"on the stroke of two tristram awoke, perspire horribly. the room was 
once again aglow with a phosphorescent light, and he felt the presence 
next to him of something cold and clammy. 

"unable to look elsewhere, he was again compel to gaze in the mirror, 
where he saw, to his consternation and horror, no heriot, but in his 
place the man with the bronze face and bushy beard. 

"he had hardly recover from the shock occasion by this discovery 
when the door surreptitious opened, and the figure of the ape glide 
noiseless in. 

"again he was temporary paralysed, his limb lose all their power of 
action and his tongue cleave to the roof of his mouth. 

"the movements of the phantasm were entire repetitionary of the 
previous night. approach the bed on 'all-fours,' it leapt on its 
victim, the tragedy being accompany this time by the most realistic 
choke and gurgles, to all of which tristram was oblige to listen in 
an agony of doubt and terror. the drama ended, tristram was overcome by 
a sudden fit of drowsiness, and sink back on to his pillow, slept 
till broad daylight. 

"anxious to question heriot as to whether he, too, had been a witness of 
the ghostly transaction he touched him lightly on the shoulder. there 
was no reply. he touched him again, and still no answer. he touched him 
yet a third time, and as there was still no response, he lean over his 
shoulder and peer into his face. 

"heriot was dead!" 

* * * * * 

"'this is the fourth death in that bed within the last twelve month 
that i can swear to,' the english doctor remark to tristram, as they 
walk down the street together, 'and always from the same cause, 
failure of the heart due to a sudden shock. if you take my advice, 
you'll clear out of the place at once.' 

"tristram thought so too, but before he went he had a talk with the 
girl in the red stockings. 

"'i can't tell you all i know,' she said to him, as he kiss her; 'but 
i wouldn't sleep a night in that room for a fortune, though i believe 
it's quite safe if you keep on the right side of the bed. i wish your 
friend had done so, he was so handsome,' and tristram, not a little 
hurt, let go her hand, and made arrangement for the funeral." 

* * * * * 

"and is that all?" i asked, as tristram's material body paused. 

"it may be," was the reply, "but that is why i've come to you. don't be 
gull by tristram into any investigate in that house. enthusiasm for 
his research work make him unconscious callous, and if he once got 
you there he might, even against your better judgment, persuade you to 
sleep on the left side! good night!" 

i shook hand with him and he departed. the following evening i heard it 
all again from tristram himself--the real tristram. 

needless to say, his conclude remark differ essentially. with 
unbounded cordiality he urge me to accompany him back again to bruges, 
and i--declined! 

* * * * * 

he wrote to me afterwards to say that he had discover the history of 
the house--a man, a music-hall artist, answer to the description of 
the figure in the bed--had once live there with a perform ape, an 
orang-outang, and happening to annoy the animal one day, the latter had 
kill him. the brute was eventually shot! 

"this experience of mine," tristram added, "is of the greatest value, 
for it has thorough convinced me of one thing at least--and that--that 
ape have spirits! and if that be so, so must all other kind of 
animals. of course they must." 


_phantasms of cat and baboon_ 

a sister of a well-known author tell me there use to be a house call 
"the swallows," standing in two acre of land, close to a village near 
basingstoke. 

in 1840 a mr. bishop of tring bought the house, which had long stood 
empty, and went to live there in 1841. after being there a fortnight two 
servant gave notice to leave, state that the place was haunt by a 
large cat and a big baboon, which they constant saw steal down the 
staircase and passages. they also testify to hearing sound as of 
somebody being strangled, proceeding from an empty attic near where 
they slept, and of the scream and groan of a number of people being 
horrible torture in the cellar just underneath the dairy. on go to 
see what was the cause of the disturbances, nothing was ever visible. by 
and by other member of the household began to be harass by similar 
manifestations. the news spread through the village, and crowd of 
people came to the house with lights and sticks, to see if they could 
witness anything. 

one night, at about twelve o'clock, when several of the watch were 
station on guard in the empty courtyard, they all saw the form of a 
huge cat and a baboon rise from the closed grating of the large cellar 
under the old dairy, rush past them, and disappear in a dark angle of 
the walls. the same figures were repeatedly seen afterwards by many 
other persons. early in december, 1841, mr. bishop, hearing fearful 
screams, accompany by deep and hoarse jabberings, apparently coming 
from the top of the house, rush upstairs, whereupon all was instantly 
silent, and he could discover nothing. after that, mr. bishop set to 
work to get rid of the house, and was fortunate enough to find as a 
purchaser a retired colonel, who was soon, however, scared out of it. 
this was in 1842; it was soon after pull down. the ground was use 
for the erection of cottages; but the haunting being transfer to 
them, they were speedy vacated, and no one ever daring to inhabit 
them, they were eventually demolished, the site on which they stood 
being convert into allotments. 

there were many theory as to the history of "the swallows"; one being 
that a highwayman, known as steeplechase jock, the son of a scotland 
chieftain, had once ply his trade there and murder many people, 
whose body were supposed to be bury somewhere on or near the 
premises. he was said to have had a terrible though decidedly unorthodox 
ending--falling into a vat of boil tar, a raving madman. but what 
were the phantasm of the ape and cat? were they the earth-bound spirits 
of the highwayman and his horse, or simply the spirits of two animals? 
though either theory is possible, i am inclined to favour the former. 


_psychic bears_ 

edmund lenthal swifte, appoint in 1814 keeper of the crown jewel in 
the tower of london, refer in an article in _notes and queries_, 1860, 
to various unaccountable phenomena happening in the tower during his 
residence there. he say that one night in the jewel office, one of the 
sentry was alarm by a figure like a huge bear issue from 
underneath the jewel room door. he thrust at it with his bayonet, which, 
go right through it, stuck in the doorway, whereupon he drop in a 
fit, and was carry senseless to the guard-room. when on the morrow mr. 
swifte saw the soldier in the guard-room, his fellow-sentinel was also 
there, and the latter testify to have seen his comrade, before the 
alarm, quiet and active, and in full possession of his faculties. he was 
now, so mr. swifte added, change almost beyond recognition, and die 
the following day. 

mr. george offer, in refer to this incident, allude to queer noise 
have been heard at the time the figure appeared. presume that the 
sentinel was not the victim of an hallucination, the question arise as 
to the kind of spirit that he saw. the bear, judge by case that have 
been told me, is by no means an uncommon occult phenomenon. the 
difficulty is how to classify it, since, upon no question appertain 
to the psychic, can one dogmatize. to quote from a clever poem that 
appear in the january number of the _occult review_, to pretend one 
know anything definite about the immaterial world is all "swank". at 
the most we--parsons, priests, theosophists, christian scientists, 
psychic research professors,--at the most can only speculate. 
nothing--nothing whatsoever, beyond the bare fact that there are 
phenomena, unaccountable by physical laws, has as yet been discovered. 
all the time and energy and space that have been devoted by scientist 
to the investigate of spiritualism and to making test in automatic 
writing are, in my opinion--and, i believe, i speak for the man in the 
street--hopelessly futile. no one, who has ever really experienced 
spontaneous ghostly manifestations, could for one moment believe in the 
genuine of the phenomena produce at séances. they have never 
deceive me, and i am of the opinion spirits cannot be convoke to 
order, either through a so-called medium fall into a so-called 
trance, through table-turning, automatic writing, or anything else. if a 
spirit comes, it will come either voluntarily, or in obedience to some 
unknown power--and certainly neither to satisfy the curiosity of a crowd 
of sensation-loving men and women, nor to be analyse by some cold, 
calculating, presumptuous professor of physics whose proper sphere is 
the laboratory. 

but to proceed. the phenomenon of the big bear, provided again it was 
really objective, may have been the phantasm of some prehistoric 
creature whose bone lie inter beneath the tower; for we know the 
valley of the thames was infest with giant reptile and quadruped of 
all kind (i incline to this theory); or it may have been a 
vice-elemental, or--the phantasm of a human being who live a purely 
animal life, and whose spirit would naturally take the form most close 
resemble it. 

* * * * * 

judge by the number of experience related to me, haunting by phantom 
hare and rabbit would appear to be far from uncommon. there is this 
difference, however, between the haunting by the two species of 
animal--phantom hare usually portend death or some grave catastrophe, 
either to the witness himself, or to someone immediately associate with 
him; whereas phantom rabbit are seldom prophetic, and may generally be 
look upon merely as the earth-bound spirits of some poor rabbit that 
have met with untimely ends. 


_hauntings by a white rabbit_ 

mr. w.t. stead, in his _real ghost stories_, give an account of the 
haunting by a phantom rabbit in a house in ---- road. he do not, 
however, mention any locality. after describe several of the phenomena 
which disturbed various occupant of the place, he go on to say, in 
the language of mrs. a., who narrate the incident:-- 

"a dog which lay on the rug also heard the sounds, for he prick up his 
ear and barked. without a moment's delay she flew to the door, calling 
the dog to follow her, intend as she did so to open the hall door and 
call for assistance, but the dog, though an excellent house dog, 
crouch at her feet and whined, but would not follow her up the stairs, 
so she carry him up in her arms, and reach the door, call for 
assistance; when, however, the dining-room door were opened, the rooms 
were in perfect quiet and destitute of any sign of life." 

the behaviour of the dog here accord exactly with the behaviour of dogs 
i have had in haunt houses, and substantiate my theory that dogs are 
excellent psychic barometers. 

"after the family had been in the house a few weeks, a white rabbit made 
its appearance. this uncanny animal would sudden appear in a room in 
which member of the family were seated, and after gliding round and 
slip under chair and tables, would disappear through a brick wall 
as easily as through an open door." 

this is the invariable trick of ghosts; they seldom, however, open 
doors. mrs. a. adds:-- 

"some years have now elapse since the incident i have now related took 
place, and again, in response to orders given by the enterprising 
landlord of the property, long-closed door and window have been thrown 
open, and painter and paperhanger have brought their skill to bear 
upon gruesome rooms and halls; the house is once more inhabited, this 
time by a widow lady and some grown-up sons. these tenant come from a 
distance, and are entire stranger both to the neighbourhood and the 
former history of the house, but, to use her own words, the mistress 
'cannot understand what ail the house,' her son insist on sleep 
together in one room, and the quiet of the house is constant being 
broken by the erratic appearances of a large white rabbit, which the 
inmate are frequent engaged chasing, but are never able to find." 

mr. stead offer no explanation. i can see no other conclusion, however, 
than that this ghost was the actual phantasm of some rabbit that had 
been done to death in the house, probably by the boy whose apparition 
was among the other manifestation seen there. 


_john wesley's ghost_ 

in his article "more glimpse of the unseen" (_occult review_, october, 
1906), mr. reginald b. span writes:-- 

"during the extraordinary manifestation which occur in the house of 
john wesley at epworth, the phantom form of two animal appeared, one 
being a large white rabbit, and the other an animal like a badger, which 
use to appear in the bedroom and run about and then disappear, whilst 
the various bang and rap were at their loudest." 

this is the only case i have ever come across of the ghost of a badger. 
i think it must be unique. mr. span adds: "many strange and inexplicable 
things occur in that house which were not due to any natural cause or 
reason. i remember that loud rap use to sound round my room at 
nights, even when i had a light burning. i was often awaken by 
rap on the floor of my bedroom, which would then sound on the wall 
and furniture, and were heard by other occupy rooms some distance 
off." this, again, is most interesting, as ghost seldom visit light 
rooms. mr. span continues:-- 

"it was in the afternoon in broad daylight when my brother saw this 
mysterious animal. 

"he was in the drawing-room alone, and as he was standing at one side of 
the room look at a picture on the walls, he heard a noise behind him, 
and found, on look round, that a sofa which generally lay against 
one of the wall had been lift by some unknown power into the middle 
of the room, at the same time he saw an animal like a rabbit run from 
under the sofa across the room and disappear into the wall. he search 
everywhere for the animal, which could not have escape from the room, 
as the door and window were closed, but was unable to find any sign of 
one or any hole whereby one might have pass out." 


_the psychic faculty in hare and rabbits_ 

hare and rabbit are very susceptible to the superphysical, the 
presence of which they scent in the same manner as do horse and dogs. 

i have known them to evince the greatest symptom of terror when brought 
into a haunt house. 




chapter vi 

inhabitant of the jungle 


_elephants, lions, tigers, etc._ 

elephant undoubted possess the faculty of scent spirits in a very 
marked degree. it is most difficult to get an elephant to pass a spot 
where any phantasm is known to appear. the big beast at once come to a 
halt, trembles, trumpets, and turning round, can only be urge forward 
by the gentlest coaxing. 

jungle are full of the ghost of slain men and animals, and afford more 
variety in haunting than any other localities. the spirits of such 
cruel creature as lions, tigers, leopards, are very much earth-bound, 
and may be seen or heard night after night haunting the site of their 
former depredations. 

the following case of a tiger ghost was narrate to me years ago by a 
gentleman whom i will style mr. de silva, p.w.d. i publish his account 
in a popular weekly journal, as follows:-- 


_the white tiger_ 

"tap! tap! tap. someone was coming behind me. i halted, and in the 
brilliant moonlight saw a figure hobble along--first one thin leg, 
then the other, always with the same measured stride--accompanied with 
the same tap of the stick. i had no wish for his company, though the 
road was lonely, and i fear the presence of tigers, so i hurried on, 
and the fast i went, the near he seem to come. tap! tap! tap! the 
man was blind and a leper, and so repulsive ugly that the nigger on 
the settlement regard him with superstitious awe. i had a horror of 
tigers, but of leper even greater. and i love my wife with no ordinary 
love. so i hurried on, and he follow quick after me. 

"the night was brilliant, even more so, i thought, than was ordinary, 
and the very brilliancy made me fear, for my shadow, the shadow of the 
trees, shadow for which i had no name, flicker across the road, were 
lost to sight to return again, and the jungle was get nearer. the 
open country on either side ceased, one by one tall blade of jungle 
grass shook their head in the gentle breeze, and the silence of the 
dark beyond began to make itself felt. a night bird whizz past me, 
croak out a dismal incantation from its black throat; something at 
which i did not care to look clatter from under a stone i loosen 
with my foot, and sped into the shade, and i hasten on. 

"tap! tap! tap! fast and faster, and fast came the blind man. i 
could smell the oil on his body, hear his breathing. 

"'whoever you are, sahib, stop!' 

"there was fear in his voice as he whine out these words, a fear which 
increase my own; but i pretended not to hear, and pressed on faster. 

"the dark grew; high over my head at either side of the road wave 
the grass, rustling to and fro, and singing to sleep the insect 
nestling on its green stalk with its old-time song of the jungle. 

"the grass ahead of me slow parted; my heart beat quicker, the tap 
behind me ceased--it was only some small animal. what was it? a small 
hyaena? no. a jackal, a lame jackal, and it look at me from out of 
eyes that for some reason or other made me shiver. i did not know what 
there was about the jackal that was different from what i had seen in 
any other jackal, but there was a something. and as i look at it in 
awe, it vanished--melted into thin air. 

"the moment after a second jackal appear just where the other one had 
been standing, but there was nothing remarkable about this one, and on 
my bend down, pretend to look for a stone to throw at it, it slunk 
back silent and stealthy whence it had come, and i hurried on 
fast than ever, knowing a tiger was near at hand. 

"tap! tap! tap! i blessed the presence of the blind man. 

"'for god's sake, sahib, stop! for the love of allah, sahib, stop!' (you 
know how they talk, o'donnell.) 'the jackals, did you see them? i knew 
them by their smell, the smell of the living and of the dead. walk with 
me, sahib, for allah's sake.' 

"presently, o'donnell, i heard a heavy rustling in the grass than the 
wind makes; a rustling that kept pace with me and went along by my side, 
never halting, but fast and faster, and faster. 

"a short distance ahead of me was a patch of bright light, where the 
cross-roads met. a few yard more and the jungle grass would end. 

"i thought of this, o'donnell--the beggar might not know the road so 
well as i. he had no wife, no child; he was a leper, only a leper--and 
my teeth chattered. 

"here the colonel pause and wipe his forehead. 

"i slacken my speed, the rustling by my side slow down, and the 
tap grew faster. i was close to the whiten road. 

"'sahib, the blessing of allah be on you for stopping. sahib, let me 
walk by your side.' 

"(to the end of my days, o'donnell, i shall never forgive myself, and 
yet i want you to understand it was for my wife--and child.) i slunk 
into the shade. two steps more and the tap would pass me. the stick 
struck the ground within one inch of my foot; my heart almost cease to 
beat; i gaze in fascination at the spot in the jungle opposite. the 
heavy rustling had stopped; only the gentle sigh of the wind went on. 
the two steps were taken, the blind man pause on the cross-roads. he 
was ghastly in the moonlight. i shuddered. his eyes peer enquiring 
round on all sides; he was look for me; he had lost his way; he 
fear the tiger. 

"suddenly something huge shot like an arrow from the dark opposite 
me. i bowed my head, o'donnell, and mutter a prayer, for i thought my 
end had come. 

"a terrible scream rang out in the clear night air. i was saved. 

"'allah curse you and yours, sahib.' 

"i open my eyes; an enormous tiger was bend over the leper, 
searching for the most convenient spot in his body to afford a tight 
grip. 

"the man's sightless eyes were turn towards the moon, his teeth shone 
white and even; with the striped horror purr in his face, he thought 
of vengeance on me. 

"i dare not move. i could not pass, o'donnell. i had no gun. the big 
brute found a nice place to catch hold. it open its mouth so that i 
could see its glisten teeth. it look down at its paws, where the 
cruel claw glittered, and they seem to afford it keen 
satisfaction--it was a tigress and vain--then it lower its head, and 
the leper shrieked. i watch it pick him up as if he were one of its 
cubs; saw the blood trickle down its soft white throat into the dusty 
road, and then it trot graceful away, and was lost in the dark 
of the jungle. there was a deathlike silence after this. i wait a few 
minutes, and then i got up. 

"i had only a short distance to go, and i no long fear the presence 
of man-eaters--there was not likely to be another. hour afterwards, 
o'donnell, when i lay in my hammock as safe as a fortress, i fancy i 
heard the dead man's cry, fancy i heard his curse. no one was more 
devoted to a wife than i was to mine. ours had been purely a love match, 
and it was against my wish that she had accompany me to such an 
out-of-the-way place as seconee. i told her about my adventure, 
suppress the leper's curse; and i was glad i did so, as she was 
greatly distressed. 

"'thank goodness you escaped, charlie,' she said. 'i am so sorry for the 
poor leper. i suppose you couldn't have help him.' 

"'i might have fetch my rifle,' i replied, 'and try to rescue him, 
of course. but i fear it wouldn't have been of much avail, as he would 
have been badly maul by then.' 

"my wife sighed. 'ah, well,' she said, 'love is selfish! it make one 
forget others. still, i wouldn't have it otherwise.' 

"'i wish this railway job here was over,' i murmured, sitting with my 
elbow on my knee and look over the flat ground, sun-baked and 
barren, away towards the dark jungle and the still dark mountain 
towering above them; and as i gaze a shadow seem to blur my vision 
and a voice to whisper in my ears, 'beware of my curse.' 

"i took cushai, one of the native servants, into confidence. 

"'now, cushai,' i said, 'you know all the superstition of the 
country--the evil eye and the rest of them. tell me, what can the die 
curse of a leper do?' 

"cushai turn pale under his skin. 

"'not of nahra!' he stuttered, swinging the knife with which he had been 
cutting maize in his hand, 'not of nahra, the leper of futtebah. sahib, 
if you were cursed by him, beware. he was learned in the black arts; he 
could heal ulcer by repeating a prayer, he could bring on fever.' 

"at this, o'donnell, i turn cold. i had live long in india. i had 
seen their so-called juggling, had experienced also strange case of 
telepathy, and knew quite sufficient of their intimacy with the 
supernatural elements to be afraid. 

"'you must keep the young sahib safe,' cushai said, 'and the white lady. 
i wish it hadn't been nahra.' 

"i took his advice. my boy, eric, was more close supervise than ever, 
and as to my wife, i beg and entreat her not to move from the house 
until the tiger was dead, and i search for it everywhere. 

"the dry season passed, the wet came, and my work still kept me in 
seconee. at times there came to us rumour of the man-eater--of another 
victim--but it never visit our bungalow, where the bright rifle lean 
against the wall wait for it. 

"i certainly did meet with slight misfortunes, which the more timid 
might have put down to the working of the curse. 

"my little finger was squash in the lay down of a rail, and ere 
had several bout of sickness. 

"it was nearly a year after the leper's death that alarm rumour of a 
man-eater have been at work again were spread about us. several 
nigger were carry off or badly bitten, and the wound show 
symptom of the loathsome disease so well known and fear by us 
all--leprosy. 

"i knew from that it must be the same tiger. 

"'the tiger is near,' someone would cry out, and a stampede among the 
native workmen would ensue. 

"'why the white tiger?' i ask cushai. 

"'because, sahib,' he replied, 'the leprosy has made it so! tigers, like 
men, and all other animals, go white even to their hair. i have not told 
them the story, sahib; they only know it must have caught the leprosy. 
to them nahra is still living.' 

"then, o'donnell, when i thought of what was at stake, and of all the 
hideous possibility the presence of this brute created, i took my 
rifle and went out to search for it. in the evenings, when the dark 
cloud from the mountain descended and the wind hiss through the 
jungle grass, i plod along with no other companion than my winchester 
repeater--searching, always searching for the damn tiger. i found it, 
o'donnell, came upon it just as it was in the midst of a meal--dining 
off a native--and i shot it twice before it recover from its 
astonishment at seeing me. the second shot took effect--i can swear to 
that, for i took particular note of the red splash of blood on its 
forehead where the bullet entered, and i went right up to it to make 
sure. as god is above us, no animal was more dead. 

"'the curse won't come now, cushai,' i said, laughing. 'i've kill the 
white tiger.' 

"'killed the white tiger, sahib! allah bless you for that!' cushai 
replied. 

"'but don't laugh too soon. nahra was a clever man, wonderful clever; 
he did not speak empty words,' and as his eyes wander to the dark 
hill again i fancy a shadow dart along the sky, and the curse came 
back to my ears. 

"i was superintend the line one afternoon; the back of the nigger 
were bend double under the burden of the great iron rod when i heard 
a terrible cry. 

"'the white tiger! the white tiger!' rod fell with a crash, spade 
follow suit, a chorus of shriek fill the air, and leg scamper 
off in all directions. i was fifty yard from my rifle, and a huge 
creature was slow approach between it and me. 

"i could hardly believe my eyes--the white tiger, the tiger i knew i had 
killed! here it was! here before me! the same in every detail, and yet 
in some strange, indefinable manner not the same. on it came, a huge 
patch of luminous white, noiselessly, stealthily--the mark of the bullet 
plainly visible on its big, flat forehead. step by step it approach 
me, its paw no long with the colouring of health, but dull and worn. 
and as it came, the cold shadow of desolate seem to fall around it. 
nothing stirred; there was no noise whatever, not even the sound of its 
feet crush the loosen soil. on, on, on nearer, near and nearer.
