"shunned by all, avoid by its fellow-creatures of the jungle, a blight 
to all and everything, it drew in a line with me. not once did its eyes 
meet mine, o'donnell; not once did it glare at the native who were 
hiding on the bank of the cutting; but it stole silent on its way 
with a something in its movements that left no doubt but that it was 
engaged in no casual venture. i remembered, o'donnell, that my wife had 
promise to come with ere to meet me along the cutting, as she was sure 
no tiger would be there. i ran as fast as i could, and yet somehow my 
feet seem weighted down. i cursed my folly for not forbidding my wife 
to come. 

"it was uphill till i got to the bend, and it might have been a 
mountain, it seem so steep. i knew if the thing i had seen met them a 
little farther on, they would be cornered, as the cutting narrow very 
much, leave not more than twenty yards, and that was a generous 
estimate. at last, after what seem an eternity, i reach the summit 
of the slope; the tiger was a mere speck along the line. i rush after 
it as fast as i could go, stumbling, half falling, pull myself 
together, and tear on, and the fast i went the quick move the 
great white figure. a feeling of despair seize me; all my fond for 
my wife became intensify tenfold, and was reveal to me then in its 
true nature; she was the one great tie that made life dear to me. even 
my love for ere pale away before the blind affection i bore her. i 
tore madly on, shouting at the same time, anything to make the white 
tiger aware of my presence, to keep it from seeing her. another bend in 
the road hid it from view. the same hideous fear grip me hard and 
fast, as i strained every muscle in the mad pursuit. at last i ran round 
the curve, and saw before me the tableau i had dreaded. the tiger was 
crouching, ready to spring on the group of three--eva, ere and the 
ayah. they were paralyse with fear, and stood on the rails staring at 
it, unable to move or utter a sound. i well understood their feelings, 
and knew they were labour in their mind as to whether the thing that 
confront them was a creature of flesh and blood, or what it was. they 
could not take their eyes off it, and, as a consequence, did not see me. 
the white tiger now went through a series of actions, so lifelike that i 
could not but believe it was real, and that i had been deceive in 
thinking i had kill it. its haunch quivered, it got ready to spring, 
and my rifle flew to my shoulder. i saw it mark eric, and read the 
increase agony in my wife's eyes. the critical moment came. another 
second, and the thing, be it material or supernatural, would jump. i 
must fire at all costs. if mortal, i must kill it, if ghostly, the noise 
of my rifle might dematerialize it. and, as god is my judge, o'donnell, 
at that moment i had not the least idea which of it was--tiger or 
phantom. it sprang--my brain reeled--my finger grew numb, and as my 
wife sudden bound forward, the shadowy form of nahra seem to rise 
from the ground and mock me. with a supreme effort i jerk my finger 
back and fired. bang! the sound of the explosion act like a 
safety-valve to the pent-up feelings of all, and there was a chorus of 
shrieks. i rush forward--the ayah lay on the ground, face downward and 
motionless. my wife had hold of eric, who was shake all over. of the 
tiger there were no signs. it had completely vanished. 

"'thank god,' i exclaimed, kiss my wife feverishly. 'thank god! it 
was only a ghost! but it was very alarming, wasn't it?' 

"'alarming!' my wife gasped, 'it was awful! i quite thought it was real! 
so did eric, and so did ---- '--then her eyes fell on the ayah, and she 
gave a great start. 'charlie!' she cried, 'for mercy's sake look at her! 
i dare not! is she all right?' 

"i turn the ayah over--she was dead! fright had kill her! 

"i then told my wife of the curse of nahra, and of the phantom i thought 
i had seen of him, when the white tiger was springing. when i had 
finished, my wife hid her face in my shoulder. 

"'charlie!' she said, 'i did something awful. i saw what i then took to 
be the real white tiger single out eric, and in my anxiety to save him 
from the brute, i pushed the ayah in front of him. and the thing sprang 
on her instead. it was nothing short of murder! and yet--well, there 
were extenuate circumstances, weren't there?' 

"'of course there were,' i said--for i verily believed, o'donnell, fear 
had, for the time being, turn her brain. 

"on our way home she sudden call my attention to eric. 

"'charlie,' she cried, 'what's that mark on his cheek? he's hurt!' 

"i looked--and my heart turn sick within me. on the boy's cheek was a 
faint red scratch, just as might have been cause by a slight, very 
slight contact with some animal's claw. 

"'sahib!' cushai whisper to me, when he saw it and heard of our 
adventure. 'sahib! beware! nahra was a clever man. he must have use the 
spirit of the white tiger as his tool. let the medicine man examine the 
scar.' 

"i did so. i took ere to a dr. nicholson, who live close by. 

"he look at the wound curious for a few moments, and then said to 
me--he was renowned for his plain speaking--'mr. de silva, there's no 
use beating about the bush, and prolong the agony unnecessary for 
you and your wife. the boy's got leprosy--god alone know how! at the 
most he may live six weeks.' 

"the shock, of course, was terrible. ere had to be isolated from 
everyone--even from those who love him best--and die within a month. 

"'sahib, i knew!' cushai said to me the day of the funeral, 'i knew some 
disaster would befall you. nahra was a wonderful man, and his curse had 
to be fulfilled. you may rest assured, however, nothing further will 
befall you, for i saw nahra in a vision this morning, and he told me 
both his and the white tiger's spirit were now on friendly terms, and 
would trouble you no more.' 

"my wife and i left the place at once, and for a long time i live in a 
hell of suspense lest she should develop the infernal disease. by a 
merciful providence, however, she did no such thing, but, on the 
contrary, picked up in health in the most marvellous fashion; indeed, 
she only told me yesterday, she felt better than she had done for years. 
i've told you the story, o'donnell--and it is true in every 
detail--because it go a long way to substantiate your theory that 
animals, as well as human beings, have a future life." 

"i am absolutely sure they have!" i replied. 


_jungle animal and psychic faculties_ 

it is, of course, impossible to say whether animal of the jungle 
possess psychic faculties, without putt them to the test, and this, 
for obvious reasons, is extremely difficult. but since i have found that 
such property are possessed--in vary degree--by all animal i have 
tested, it seem only too probable that bear and tigers, and all beast 
of prey, are similarly endowed. 

it would be interesting to experiment with a beast of prey in a haunt 
locality; to observe to what extent it would be aware of the advent of 
the unknown, and to note its behaviour in the actual presence of the 
phenomena. 




part iii 

bird and the unknown 




chapter vii 

bird and the unknown 


as edgar allan poe has suggest in his immortal poem of "the raven," 
there is a strong link between certain species of bird and the unknown. 

we all know that vultures, kite and crow scent dead body from a 
great way off, but we don't all know that these and other kind of bird 
possess, in addition, the psychic property of scent the advent not 
only of the phantom of death, but of many, if not, indeed, all other 
spirits. within my knowledge there have been case when, before a death 
in the house, ravens, jackdaws, canaries, magpies, and even parrots, 
have shown unmistakable sign of uneasy and distress. the raven has 
croak in a high-pitched, abnormal key; the jackdaw and canary have 
become silent and dejected, from time to time shivering; the magpie even 
has feign death; the parrot has shriek incessantly. owls, too, are 
sure predict of death, and may be heard hoot in the most doleful 
manner outside the house of anyone doom to die shortly. 

in an article entitle "psychic records," the editor of the _occult 
review_ (in the august number, 1905) supplies the following anecdote of 
ghost of bird furnish him by his correspondents. 

"in the autumn of 1877 my husband was lie seriously ill with rheumatic 
fever, and i had sat up several nights. at last the doctor insist on 
my go to bed; and very unwilling i retired to a spare room. while 
undress i was surprise to see a very large white bird come from the 
fireplace, make a hover circle round me, and finally go to the top of 
a large double chest of drawers. i was too tired to trouble about it, 
and thought i would let it remain until morning. the next morning i said 
to the housemaid: 

"'there was a large bird in the spare room last night, which flew to the 
top of the drawers. see that it is put out.' 

"the nurse, who was present, said: 

"'oh, dear, ma'am, i am afraid that is an omen, and means the master 
won't live,' and she was confirmed in her opinion by the maid saying she 
had searched, and there was no trace of any bird. 

"i was quite angry, as my husband was decidedly better, had slept 
through the night, and we thought the crisis had passed. i went to his 
bedside and found him quiet sleeping, but he never woke, and in about 
an hour pass quiet away. 

"i thought no more of the bird, fancy i must have been mistaken from 
being overtired. 

"some month after my husband's death my youngest little one was born; 
he live for twelve months, and then had an attack of bronchitis. he 
slept in a cot in my room, and i was undress one night, when this 
same large white bird came from his cot, float round me, and 
disappear in the fireplace. at the time i did not for a moment think 
of it as anything but a strange coincidence, and in no way connected it 
with baby's illness. 

"the next morning i was sitting by the drawing-room fire with baby on my 
lap. the doctor came in, look at him, sound his chest, and 
pronounced him much better. as he was a friend of the family, he sat 
down on the other side of the fireplace and was chat in an ordinary 
way, when he sudden jump up with an exclamation, 'why, what do 
this mean?' and took the child from my arms quite dead! 

"for two years we saw nothing more of the white bird, and we had move 
to another place. 

"one day i was in my room, and my two little girls, age six and eight, 
were standing at the window watch a kitten in the garden, when 
sudden the youngest cry out: 

"'oh, mamma! look at that great white bird,' putt her hand as if to 
catch it, exactly in the way it flies round one. 

"i saw nothing, and the elder child said, 'don't be silly, jessie; there 
is no bird.' 

"'but there is,' said the child. 'don't you see? there, look! there it 
is!' 

"i look at my watch. it was twenty minutes past three. 

"two days after we received the news that a niece of mine had die at 
twenty minutes past three. the children had never known anything of the 
former appearances, as we had never talk about it before them. we have 
seen nothing since of the bird, but have for some years had no death in 
the family." 

so runs the article in the _occult review_, and i can corroborate it 
with similar experience that have happen to my friends and to me. 

some years ago, for instance, a great friend of my wife's died, and on 
the day of the funeral a large bird try to fly in at the window of the 
room where the corpse lay; while, shortly afterwards, an exactly similar 
bird visit the window of my wife's and my room in a house, several 
hundred of mile away. if it was only a coincidence, it was a very 
extraordinary one. 

then again, this spring, just before the death of one of my wife's 
relatives, a large bird flew violent against the window-pane behind 
which my wife was sitting--an incident that had never happen to her in 
that house before. 

undoubtedly, spirits in the guise of birds--most probably they are the 
phantasm of bird that have actually once live on the material 
plane--are the messenger of death. 


_a case of bird haunting in east russia_ 

some years ago the neighbourhood of orskaia, in east russia, was rouse 
by an affair of a very remarkable nature. the body of a handsome young 
peasant woman, call marthe popenkoff, was found in a lonely part of 
the road, between orskaia and orenburg, with the skin of her face and 
body shocking torn and lacerated, but without there being any wound 
deep enough to cause her death, which the doctor attribute to syncope. 

the people of orskaia, not satisfy with this verdict, declared marthe 
had been murdered, and made such a loud clamour that the editor of the 
local paper at last voice their sentiment in the _east russia 
chronicle_. it was then that m. durant, a smart young france engineer, 
temporary reside in those parts, became interested in the case, and 
decided to investigate it thoroughly. with this end in view he wrote to 
his friend m. hersant--a keen student of the occult--in saratova, to 
join him, and three days after the despatch of his letter met the latter 
at the orskaia railway station. m. durant retail the case as they 
drove to his house. 

"it is a remarkable affair, in every way," he said. "the woman was 
leading a perfectly respectable married life; she was hard-working and 
industrious, and beyond the fact that she was over-indulgent to her 
children, do not seem to have had any serious faults. as far as i can 
ascertain she had no enemies." 

"nor secret lovers?" m. hersant asked. 

"no; she was quite straight." 

"and you feel sure she was murdered?" 

"i do. public opinion so strong favours that view." 

"did you see the mark on the woman?" 

"i did, and could make nothing of them. after supper i will take you to 
see her, in the morgue." 

"what--she is still unburied?" 

"yes--but there is nothing unusual about that. in these parts body 
are often kept for ten days--sometimes even longer." 

m. durant was as good as his word; after they had partaken of a somewhat 
hasty meal, they set out to the morgue, where they made a careful 
inspection of the poor woman's remains. 

m. hersant examine the mark on the woman's body very close with his 
magnifying-glass. 

"ah!" he sudden exclaimed, bend down and almost touching the corpse 
with his nose, "ah!" 

"have you made a discovery?" m. durant enquired. 

"i prefer not to say at present," m. hersant replied. "i should like to 
see the spot where this body was found--now." 

"we will go there at once," m. durant rejoined. 

the scene of the tragedy was the orenburg road, at the foot of two 
little hills; and on either side were the slope fields, yellow with 
the nod corn. 

"that is the exact place where she lay," m. durant said, indicate with 
his finger a dark patch on a little wooden bridge span a stream, 
within a stone's throw of a tumbledown mill-house, all overgrown with 
ivy and lichens. m. hersant look round and sniff the air with his 
nostrils. 

"there is an air of lonely about this spot," he remarked, "that in 
itself suggest crime. if this were an ordinary murder, one could well 
imagine the assassin was aid in his diabolical work by the 
configuration of the land which, shelving as it does, slips down into 
the narrow valley, so as to preclude any possibility of escape on the 
part of the victim. the place seem especially design by providence as 
a death-trap. let us have a look at the interior of this building." 

"the police have search it thoroughly," m. durant said. 

"i've no doubt," m. hersant reply drily. "no one know better than i 
what the thorough of the police means." 

they enter the premises cautiously, since the roof was in a rickety 
condition, and any slight concussion might dislodge an avalanche of 
stone and plaster. while m. durant stood glancing round him rather 
impatiently, m. hersant made a careful scrutiny of the walls. 

"humph," he said at last. "as you so rightly observed, henri, this is a 
remarkable case. i have finished my investigate for to-night. let us 
be go home. to-morrow i should like to visit marthe's home." 

this conversation took place shortly before midnight; some six hour 
later all orskaia was ring with the news that marthe popenkoff's 
three children had all been found dead in their beds, their face and 
body lacerate in exactly the same manner as their mother's. there 
seem to be no doubt now that marthe had been murdered, and the 
populace cry shame on the police; for the assassin was still at large. 
they agree that the murder could be no other than peter popenkoff, 
and the editor of the local paper repeating these statements, peter 
popenkoff was duly charged with the crimes, and arrested. he was 
pronounced guilty by all excepting m. hersant; and of course m. hersant 
thought him guilty, too; only he like to think different from anyone 
else. 

"i don't want to commit myself," was all they could get out of him. "i 
may have something to say later on." 

m. durant laugh and shrug his shoulders. 

"it, undoubtedly, is peter popenkoff," he observed. "i had an idea that 
he was the culprit all along." 

but a day or two later, peter popenkoff was found dead in prison with 
the skin on his face and hand all torn to shreds. 

"there! didn't we say so?" cry the inconsequent mob. "peter popenkoff 
was innocent. one of the police themselves is the murderer." 

"come, you must acknowledge that we are on the right track now--it is 
one of the police," m. durant said to his friend. 

but m. hersant only shook his head. 

"i acknowledge nothing of the sort," he said. "come with me to the 
mill-house to-night, and i will then tell you what i think." 

to this proposition m. durant willing agreed, and, accompany by his 
friend and the village priest, set off. on their arrival, m. hersant 
produce a big compass, and on the earth floor of the mill-house drew a 
large circle, in which he made with white chalk various sign and 
symbols. he then sat in the middle of it, and bade his two companion 
stand in the doorway and watch. the night grew dark and darker, and 
presently into the air stole a something that all three men at once 
realize was supernatural. m. hersant cough nervously, the priest 
cross himself, and m. durant call out, "this is get ridiculous. 
these mediæval proceedings are too absurd. let us go home." the next 
moment, from the far distance, a church clock began to strike. it was 
midnight, and an impressive silence fell on the trio. then there came a 
noise like the flutter of wings, a loud, blood-curdling scream, half 
human and half animal, and a huge black owl, whirl down from the roof 
of the building, perch in the circle directly in front of m. hersant. 

"pray, father! pray quickly," m. hersant whispered. "pray for the dead, 
and sprinkle the circle with holy water." 

the priest, as well as his tremble limb would allow, obeyed; 
whereupon the bird instantly vanished. 

"for heaven's sake," m. durant gasped, "tell us what it all means." 

"only this," m. hersant said solemnly, "the phantasm we saw cause the 
death of the popenkoff family. it is the spirit of an owl that the 
children, encourage by their parents, kill in a most cruel manner. as 
soon as i examine marthe's body, i perceive the mutilation were due 
to a bird; and when i visit this mill on the eve of my arrival, i knew 
that a bird had once live here; that it had been capture with lime and 
murdered, and that it haunt the place." 

"how could you know that?" the priest exclaim in astonishment. 

"i am clairvoyant. i saw the bird's ghost as it appear to us just now. 
afterwards i enquire of the popenkoffs' neighbours, and the information 
i gather fully confirmed my suspicions--that the unfortunate bird had 
been put to death in a most barbarous manner. the death of the three 
children laid to rest any doubt i may have had with regard to the 
superphysic play a part in the death of marthe. then when her 
better-half had been serve likewise, i was certain that all five 
pseudo-murders were wholly and solely acts of retribution, and that they 
were perpetrated--i am inclined to think involuntarily--by the spirit of 
the owl itself. accordingly, i decided to hold a séance here--here in 
its old haunt, and if possible to put an end to the earth-bound 
condition and wanderings of the soul of the unhappy bird. thanks to 
father mickledoff we have done so, and there will be no more so-called 
murder near orskaia." 


_hauntings by the phantasm of birds_ 

one of the most curious case of haunting by the phantasm of bird 
happen towards the end of the eighteenth century in a church not 
twenty mile from london. the sexton start the rumours, declare that 
he had heard strange noises, apparently proceeding from certain vault 
contain the tomb of two old and distinguished families. the noises, 
which generally occur on friday nights, most often took the form of 
mockings, suggest to some of the listeners--the enact of a murder, 
and to other merely the flap of wings. 

the case soon attract considerable attention, people flock to the 
church from all over the country-side, and it was not long before 
certain person came forward and declared they had ascertain the cause 
of the disturbance. the churchwarden, sexton, and his wife and other 
all swore to seeing a huge crow peck and claw at the coffin in 
the vaults, and flying about the chancel of the church, and perch on 
the communion rails. when they try to seize it, it immediately 
vanished. 

an old lady, who came of a family of well-to-do yeomen, and who live 
near the church about that time, said that the people in the town had 
for many years been convinced the church there was haunt by the 
phantom of a bird, which they believe to be the earth-bound soul of a 
murderer, who, owe to his wealth, was inter in the churchyard, 
instead of being bury at the cross-roads with the customary wooden 
stake driven through the middle of his body. this belief of the yokel 
received some corroboration from a neighbouring squire, who said he had 
seen the phantasm, and was quite positive it was the earth-bound soul of 
a criminal whose family history was known to him, and whose remains lay 
in the churchyard. 

this is all the information that i have been able to gather on the 
subject, but it is enough to, at least, suggest the church was, at one 
time, haunt by the phantom of a bird, but whether the earth-bound soul 
of a murder taking that guise, or the spirit of an actual dead bird, 
it is impossible to say. 


_the ghost of an evil bird_ 

henry spicer, in his _strange things amongst us_, tell the story of a 
captain morgan, an honourable and vivacious gentleman, who, arrive in 
london in 18--, put up for the night in a large, old-fashioned hotel. 
the room in which he slept was full of heavy, antique furniture, 
reminiscent of the days of king george i, one of the worst periods in 
modern english history for crime. despite, however, his grim 
suggestive surroundings, captain morgan quick got into bed and was 
soon asleep. he was abrupt awaken by the sound of flapping, and, on 
look up, he saw a huge black bird with outstretched wings and fiery 
red eyes perch on the rail at the foot of the four-poster bed. 

the creature flew at him and endeavour to peck his eyes. captain 
morgan resisted, and after a desperate struggle succeed in driving it 
to a sofa in the corner of the room, where it settled down and regard 
him with great fear in its eyes. determined to destroy it, he flung 
himself on the top of it, when, to his surprise and terror, it 
immediately crumble into nothingness. he left the house early next 
morning, convinced that what he had seen was a ghost, but mr. spice 
offer no explanation as to how one should classify the phenomenon. 

it may have been the earth-bound spirit of the criminal or vicious 
inclined person who had once live there, or it may have been the 
phantom of an actual bird. either alternative is feasible. 

i have heard there is an old house near poole, in dorset, and another in 
essex, which were formerly haunt by spectral birds, and that as late 
as 1860 the phantasm of a bird, many times the size of a raven, was so 
frequent seen by the inmate of a house in dean street, soho, that 
they eventually grew quite accustomed to it. but bird haunting are not 
confine to houses, and are far more often to be met with out of doors; 
indeed there are very few woods, and moors, and commons that are not 
subject to them. i have constant seen the spirits of all manner of 
bird in the park in dublin and london. greenwich park, in particular, 
is full of them. 


_addendum to bird and the unknown_ 

though their unlove aspect and solitary mode of life may in some 
measure account for the prejudice and suspicion with which the owl, 
crow, raven, and one or two other bird have always been regarded, there 
are undoubted other and more subtle reason for their unpopularity. 

the ancients without exception credit these bird with psychic 
properties. 

"ignarres bubo dirum mortalibu omen," said ovid; whilst speak of the 
fatal prognostication of the crow virgil wrote: 

"saepe sinistra cava praedixit ab ilice cornix." 

a number of crow are state to have flutter about cicero's head on 
the day he was murdered. 

pliny says, "these birds, crow and rooks, all of them keep much 
prattling, and are full of chat, which most men take for an unlucky sign 
and presage of ill-fortune." 

ramesay, in his work _elminthologia_ (1688), writes: 

"if a crow fly over the house and croak thrice, how do they fear they, 
or someone else in the family, shall die." 

the bittern is also a bird of ill omen. allude to this bird, bishop 
hall once said: 

"if a bittern flies over this man's head by night, he will make his 
will"; whilst sir humphry davy wrote: 

"i know a man of very high dignity who was exceedingly move by omens, 
and who never went out shoot without a bittern's claw fasten to his 
button-hole by a riband, which he thought ensure him 'good luck.'" 

raven and swallow both, at times, prognosticate death. in lloyd's 
_stratagems of jerusalem_ (1602) he says: 

"by swallow lighting upon pirrhus' tents, and lighting upon the mast of 
mar. antonius' ship, sailing after cleopatra to egypt, the soothsayer 
did prognosticate that pirrhu should be slaine at argo in greece, and 
mar. antoniu in egypt." 

he allude to swallow following cyru from persia to scythia, from 
which the "wise men" foretold his death. raven follow alexander the 
great from india to babylon, which was regard by all who saw them as a 
fatal sign. 

"'tis not for nought that the raven sing now on my left and, croaking, 
has once scrape the earth with his feet," wrote plautus. 

other reference to the same bird are as follows: 

"the raven himself is hoarse 
that croak the fatal entrance of duncan 
under my battlements."--(_macbeth._) 

"it come o'er my memory 
as doth the raven o'er the infect house, 
boding to all."--(_othello._) 

"that toll 
the sick man's passport in her hollow beak, 
and in the shadow of the silent night 
doth shake contagion from her sable wings." 
(_jew of malta._) 

"is it not ominous in all country where crow 
and raven croak upon trees?"--(_hudibras._) 

"the boding raven on her cottage sat, 
and with hoarse croak warn us of our fate." 
(_the dirge._) 

"in cornwall," write mr. hunt, in his work on popular beliefs, etc., of 
the west of england, "it is believe that the croak of a raven over 
the house bode evil to some of the family. the following incident, 
given to me by a really intelligent man, illustrate the feeling: 

"'one day our family were much annoy by the continual croak of a 
raven over the house. some of us believe it to be a token; other 
deride the idea. but one good lady, our next-door neighbour, said: 

"'"just mark the day, and see if something do not come of it." 

"'the day and hour were careful noted. month pass away, and 
unbeliever were loud in their boast and enquiry after the token. 
the fifth month arrived, and with it a black-edged letter from 
australia, announce the death of one of the member of the family in 
that country. on compare the dates of the death and the raven's croak, 
they were found to have occur on the same day.'" 

in an old number of _notes and queries_ a correspondent relate that in 
somersetshire the appearance of a single jackdaw is regard as a sure 
prognostication of evil. he go on to add that the men employ in the 
quarry in the avon gorge, clifton, bristol, had more than once notice 
a jackdaw perch on the chain that span the river, prior to some 
catastrophe among them. 

dead magpie were once hung over the doorway of haunt house to keep 
away ghosts; it being almost universally believe that all phantasm 
share the same dread of this bird. ghost of magpie themselves are, 
however, far from uncommon; on dartmoor and exmoor, for example, i have 
seen several of them, generally in the immediate vicinity of bog or 
deep holes. 

witch were much attach to this bird, and were said to often assume 
its shape after death. 

"magpies," say mr. william jones, in his _credulities, past and 
present_, "are mysterious everywhere. a lady living near carlstad, in 
sweden, grievous offend a farm woman who came into the court of her 
house ask for food. the woman was told 'to take that magpie hanging 
upon the wall and eat it.' she took the bird and disappeared, with an 
evil glance at the lady, who had been so ill-advised as to insult a 
finn, whose magical powers, it is well known, far exceed those of the 
gipsies." (other authority corroborate this statement; and i have 
heard it said that the finn can surpass even the famous trick of the 
indians.) mr. jones, in the same story, says: "presently the number 
increased, and the lady, who at first had been amused, became troubled, 
and try to drive them away by various devices. all was to no purpose. 
she could not move without a large company of magpies; and they became 
at length so daring as to hop on her shoulder." (this read like 
hallucination. however, as i have heard of similar cases, in which there 
has been no doubt as to the objective of the phenomena, i see no 
reason why these magpie should not have been objective too.) "then she 
took to her bed in a room with closed shutters, although even this was 
not an effectual protection, for the magpie kept tap at the 
shutter day and night." mr. jones adds: "the lady's death is not 
recorded; but it is fully expect that, die when she may, all the 
magpie of wermland will be present at her funeral." 

there is a house in great russell street, w.c., where the haunting take 
the form of a magpie that taps at one of the window every morning 
between two and three, and then appear inside the room, perch on what 
looks like a huge alpine stick, suspend horizontal in the air, about 
seven feet from the floor. the moment a sound is made the apparition 
vanishes. it is thought to be the spirit of a magpie that was done to 
death in a very cruel manner in that room many years ago. there is a 
story current to the effect that a lady, when visiting the britain 
museum one day, happen to pass some slight remark about one of the 
egypt mummy case (not the notorious one), and that on quit the 
building she felt a sharp peck on her neck. she put up her hand to the 
injure part, and felt the distinct impression of a bird's claw on it. 
she could see nothing, however. that night--and for every succeed 
night for six weeks--she was awaken at two o'clock by the phantom of 
an enormous magpie that flutter over the bed, and was clearly visible 
to herself and her sister. the phenomenon worried her so that she became 
ill, and was eventually ordered abroad. she went to cairo and enjoy a 
brief respite; the hauntings, however, began again, and this time became 
so persistent that she at last lost her reason, and had to be brought 
home and confine in a private asylum, where she shortly afterwards 
died. though i cannot vouch for the truth of this story, i do think it 
is somewhat risky to make fun of certain of the egypt relics in the 
museum. they may be haunt by something infinite more alarm than 
the ghost of magpies. there are many saying respecting the magpie as a 
harbinger of ill luck. in lancashire, for example, there is this rhyme: 

"one for anger, two for mirth, 
three for a wedding, four for a birth, 
five for rich, six for poor, 
seven for a witch, i dare tell you no more." 

from further north come this couplet: 

"magpie, magpie, chatter and flee, 
turn up thy tail, and good luck fall me." 

rooks, again, are very psychic birds; they always leave their haunt 
near an old house shortly before a death take place in it, because 
their highly develop psychic faculty of scent enable them to detect 
the advent of the phantom of death, of which they have the greatest 
horror. a rook is of great service, when investigate haunt houses, 
as it nearly always give warning of the appearance of the unknown by 
violent flap of the wings, loud croaking, and other unmistakable 
symptom of terror. 

owls, though no less sensitive to superphysic influence, are not 
scared by it; they and bats, alone among the many kind of animal i 
have tested, take up their abode in haunt localities, and with the 
utmost sang-froid appear to enjoy the presence of the unknown, even in 
its most terrify form. 

the owl has been associate with the dark side of the unknown long 
than any other bird. 

"solaque, culminibu ferali carmine bubo. saepe queri et longa in 
fletum ducere voces," write virgil. 

pliny, in describe this bird, says, "bubo funebri et maxime 
abominatus"; whilst chaucer writes: "the owl eke that of death the bode 
ybringeth." 

in the arundel family a white owl is said to be a sure indication of 
death. 

that shakespeare attach no little importance to the fatal crying of 
the bird may be gather from the scene in _macbeth_, when the murder 
asks: 

"didst thou not hear a noise?" and lady macbeth answers: 

"i heard the owl scream and the cricket cry"; and the scene in _richard 
iii_, where richard interrupt a messenger of evil news with the words: 

"out on ye, owls! nothing but song of death?" 

gray speak of "moping" owls; chatterton exclaims, "harke! the dethe 
owle loude dothe synge"; whilst hogarth introduce the same bird in the 
murder scene of his _four stage of cruelty_. 

nor is the belief in the sinister prophetic property of the owl 
confine to the white races; we find it everywhere--among the red 
indians. west africans, siamese, and aborigine of australia. 

in cornwall, and in other corner of the country, the crow of a cock 
at midnight was formerly regard as indicate the passage of death 
over the house; also if a cock crew at a certain hour for two or three 
nights in succession, it was thought to be a sure sign of early death to 
some member of the household. in _notes and queries_ a correspondent 
remark that crow hen are not uncommon, that their crow is very 
similar to the crow of a very young cock, and must be taken as a certain 
presage of some dire calamity. 

it was generally held that in all haunt locality the ghost would at 
once vanish--not to appear again till the following night--at the first 
crow of the cock after midnight. i believe there is a certain amount 
of truth in this--at all event cocks, as i myself have proved, are 
invariable sensitive to the presence of the superphysical. 

the whistle is also a very psychic bird. spenser, in his _faerie 
queene_ (book ii, canto xii, st. 31), allude to it thus:-- 

"the whistle shrill, that whoso hear doth die"; 

whilst sir walter scott refer to it in a similar sense in his _lady of 
the lake_. 

the yellow-hammer was formerly the object of much persecution, since it 
was believe that it received three drops of the devil's blood on its 
feather every may morning, and never appear without presage ill 
luck. parrot do not appear to be very susceptible to the influence of 
the unknown, and indicate little or no dread of superphysic 
demonstrations. 

doves, wrens, and robins are bird of good omen, and the many 
superstition regarding them are all associate with good luck. doves, i 
have found in particular, are very safe psychic barometer in haunt 
houses. 

it is almost universally held to be unlucky to kill a robin. a 
correspondent of _notes and queries_ (fourth series, vol. viii, p. 505) 
remarks: 

"i took the following down from the mouth of a young miner: 

"'my father kill a robin and had terrible bad luck after it. he had at 
that time a pig which was ready for pipping; she had a litter of seven, 
and they all died. when the pig was kill the two ham went bad; 
presently three of the family had a fever, and my father himself die of 
it. the neighbour said it was all through killing the robin.'" 

george smith, in his _six pastorals_ (1770), says: 

"i found a robin's nest within our shed, 
and in the barn a wren has young one bred; 
i never take away their nest, nor try 
to catch the old ones, lest a friend should die. 
dick took a wren's nest from the cottage side, 
and ere a twelvemonth pass'd his mother dy'd!" 

in yorkshire it was once firm believe that if a robin were killed, 
the cow belong to the family of the destroyer of the bird would, 
for some time, only give bloody milk. at one time--and, perhaps, even 
now--the robin and wren, out of sheer pity, use to cover the body of 
those that die in the wood with leaves. 

webster, in his _tragedy of vittoria corombona_ (1612), refer to this 
touching habit of these bird thus: 

"call for the robin redbreast and the wren, 
since o'er the shady grove they hover, 
and with leaves and flower do cover 
the friendless body of unbury men." 

not so harmless is the stormy petrel, whose advent is look upon by 
sailor as a sure sign of an impending storm, accompany by much loss 
of life. 

the vulture and eagle, obviously on account of their ferocious 
dispositions, often remain earth-bound after death, and usually select 
as their haunts, spot little frequent by man. from what i have heard 
they are by far the most malignant of all bird ghosts, and have even 
been known to inflict physical injury on those who have had the 
misfortune to pass the night within their allot precincts. 




chapter viii 

a brief retrospect 


if i have fail to convince my reader as to the reality of a future 
existence for all species of mammalia, i trust i have at least suggest 
to them the idea of probability in such a theory; for did the belief 
that all animal possess imperishable spirits similar to mankind only 
become general, i feel quite sure that a marked improvement in our 
treatment of all the so-called "brute" creation--and god alone know how 
much such an improvement is needed--would speedy result. it is still 
only the comparative few who are kind to animals--the majority are 
either wholly indifferent or absolutely cruel. but if children were made 
to realize that even insect have spirits, they, at least, let us hope, 
would cease to take delight in pull off the wings and leg of flies. 

man has hitherto entertain the ridiculous unjustifiable idea that 
all the animal and insect world has been create solely for his benefit, 
to be kill or to be kept alive entire at his discretion. such an 
absurd and presumptuous belief ought to be exploded once and for all. 
the animal world, so all sane people must agree, was undoubted create 
to lead the same, free, untrammell life as do man himself. man--save 
in cunning--is nothing superior either to the dog, horse, or other 
mammalia; indeed, he is not infrequent so inferior that one cannot 
help thinking that possibly the higher spiritual plane are not for him 
at all, but for those who--misnamed the lower creation--have surpass 
man in spirituality. let those who doubt this study the superphysic 
all around them. let them careful watch animals, and observe their 
propensities, their psychic faculty of scent, sight, and hearing. they 
can easily test them in any house or locality which has a 
well-established reputation for being haunted. they will then see how 
close a relationship there really is between the animal and 
superphysic worlds. and if they want further proof,--proof of a more 
material nature,--let them search around for some spot state to be 
haunt by a ghostly phenomenon in the form of a dog, horse, cat, or 
other animal,--and investigate there themselves. 

such investigate have convinced me, and surely, by use these same 
method with patience and perseverance, other people might also be 
convinced. at all events, let them try. for, a conviction like mine--a 
conviction that an eternity exist for our canine pet and dumb 
friends--is certainly worth a lot of strive after. at least so i 
think.

plymouth
